<pb n="[5]"
<div id="gfprice001" type="chapter">
<head rend="center">HER PRICELESS LOVE.</head>
<milestone unit="section" rend="line"/>
<body>
<head rend="center">CHAPTER 1.</head>
<byline>ALONE IN THE STREETS.</byline>
<p>The weary salesgirls were hurrying out of the rapidly darkening store of Hardman & Son.</p>
<p>It was after ten o'clock at night, and all the other stores on Sixth Avenue had already closed.</p>
<p>Hardman & Son were always last to release their tired workers, as if determined to get just a little more than they paid for in labor.</p>
<p>And, as usual, they had been the first to begin the late hours of the Christmas season, so that this night the avenue was almost deserted, except for the throngs that poured from the great store.</p>
<p>This way and that the girls hurried, some going east.<sic/> some west, some north, and others south.</p>
<p>As much as possible, they went in pairs; for when they left the well-lighted avenue to enter the gloomier side streets, at that hour of the night, a girl naturally felt timid when alone.</p>
<p>You could see those who were not fortunate enough to have companions wrap their outer garments more closely about them, and pull thick veils over their faces, while they half ran through the streets.</p>
<pb n="6 Alone in the Streets."/>
<p>Many of the girls were too young and pretty to have been out alone at that hour of the night; but what help was there for it? Fathers, mothers, and brothers were all hard at work, too, and the girls must take care of themselves.</p>
<p>Among the last to leave the store was one who did well, indeed, in closely veiling her face; for it was one to tempt an anchorite to a second glance, so witching was it in its seductive beauty.</p>
<p>But for all the roguish glances of the dancing brown eyes, and the merry play of dimples about the rosebud lips, there was nothing coquettish in beautiful Viola Redmond.</p>
<p>She knew as well as anybody the dangers of being in the streets of the great metropolis at night; but there was no sign of apprehension in the radiant face as she drew the thick veil over it. There was no tremor in the softly undulating bosom, as she buttoned her coat closely about her perfect form.</p>
<p>She looked up and down the avenue as she stepped over the threshold to the pavement. There was no one to keep her company, so quickly had the tired girls hurried homeward.</p>
<p>But Viola only laughed softly, with a certain subdued happiness, and started uptown.</p>
<p>"Wait a moment, little Viola; I am going your way," said a man's voice in a low, wheedling tone.</p>
<pb n="7 Alone in the Streets."/>
<p>Viola recognized the voice, and shuddered at its sound.</p>
<p>Her immediate impulse was to accelerate her pace into a run. Anything to get away from Peter Harriem, the confidential clerk of Hardman & Son.</p>
<p>But it was in the man's power to have her discharged from her position. She restrained her impulse, and turned quickly to say in a low tone:</p>
<p>"You are very kind, but&#x2014;I do not go the same way as you, sir. Thank you, just the same."</p>
<p>He stepped to her side, and kept pace with her, emitting a low, chuckling laugh as he did so, and saying in a peculiarly oily tone:</p>
<p>"Well, it won't be any hardship to go out of my way a little to walk with pretty Viola Redmond."</p>
<p>He touched her plump arm as he spoke, as if he intended to walk with his hand on her elbow.</p>
<p>She shuddered, and drew away.</p>
<p>She could see the long, bony hand, which always made her think that it was about to get a stealthy clutch on something. She could not bear to have him touch her.</p>
<p>"Such a little beauty as you should not be alone in the wicked streets so late," he said, sidling up to her again, and once more taking her elbow in his hand.</p>
<p>There was something so sleek in his manner, that
<pb n="8 Alone in the Streets."/>
Viola had the feeling she would have had if a snake had begun to coil about her.</p>
<p>"I can walk alone, please," she said, quickening her pace.</p>
<p>"What a coy little thing it is!" he murmured.</p>
<p>It seemed to her that there was a threatening note in his voice.</p>
<p>She thought of his power, and of the sick mother at home.</p>
<p>"I&#x2014;I only meant that&#x2014;that I am used to walking alone, and that&#x2014;that I can walk faster if&#x2014;if no one assists me," she stammered.</p>
<p>"Yes," said her companion, in a musing tone, and as if continuing a thought that had been in his mind, "it is not nice for such beauties to be out at night. But, after all, Viola, it is a great deal to have work to do these hard times, isn't it?"</p>
<p>"Ye-es, sir," she stammered, in a low tone. ,"l am sure I am grateful. I&#x2014;I do not mind being out. It is not very far for me, and&#x2014;and I can hurry."</p>
<p>He was close to her again, and although he did not again attempt to touch the rounded elbow, he touched her shoulder with his. She bore the contact shudderingly.</p>
<p>"Mr. Hardman," he went on cunningly, "was asking me to-day if there
was no way in which he could get rid of some of the girls. He seemed to think we could do with less help."</p>
<p>"Everybody seems to work hard," she ventured to say.</p>
<p>"Yes; I know it," he said, in a sly, stealthy way, that made her flesh creep. "But I shall have to send a few girls away just to please him. But I will look out for you, little Viola. You shan't be one of the unlucky ones."</p>
<p>She felt his hand crawl, so to speak, along her arm and rest again on her elbow. She knew that he was making that liberty the price of her being kept in the store.</p>
<p>Her heart throbbed violently, and the blood leaped to her face. Oh, how indignant she was! Fiery words were on her tongue, and she felt an angry desire to fling his hand from her.</p>
<p>Then there rose before her eyes the picture of the pain-furrowed face of her sick mother, and she smothered her anger, and let him walk thus by her side.</p>
<p>But she felt as the man does who wakes in the woods after a sweet, refreshing sleep, to find a deadly snake passing over his body. To protest is to court instant death.</p>
<p>She permitted Peter Herriem to walk by her side, though her whole
nature was convulsed with fear and loathing.</p>
<pb n="10 Alone in the Streets."/>
<p>"I have sometimes thought I would have your wages raised," he murmured. She felt as if the snake were hissing in her ear. "Would you
like that?"</p>
<p>"If&#x2014;if I could earn it," she answered faintly.</p>
<p>He chuckled.</p>
<p>"Don't you think you could earn it now?" he asked, bending over her
arm a little.</p>
<p>She could not tell why he frightened her so, but she could not answer, so great was the effor to keep from throwing his hand off, and
running away with all her speed.</p>
<p>"But it doesn't matter whether you earn it or not," he went on, his
voice growing lower and more oily with each word, while his hot breath
was perceptible on her cheek, even through her thick veil. "If I wish to raise your wages, I can do so. Shall I do it, my pretty one?"</p>
<p>"Her breath came swiftly as she listened, and the increasing pressure on her plump arm drove her into a frantic fear.</p>
<p>She could not answer. She did not know what she was doing.</p>
<p>She was aware only of the feeling that somehow she must get him away from her.</p>
<p>She stammered an incoherent something about speaking to her mother about it, and broke away, running rapidly down a side street.</p>
<p>She ran swiftly and with a palpitating fear of some-
<pb n="11 Alone in the Streets."/>
thing horrid and dreadful which she could not understand.</p>
<p>She felt as if she had been contaminated by the touch as of some slimy thing.</p>
<p>She ran until she feared to attract observation, then stopped with beating heart and listened; she could hear no pursuing footsteps.</p>
<p>She looked back, and could see no one answering to the tall, stooping figure and shuffling walk of the confidential man of Harman &
Son.</p>
<p>That fact made her breathe more freely, but the fear of what she had experienced, and of what might be the result on the morrow, made
her go swiftly but trembling on.</p>
<p>But presently, she paused and looked about with a start. She examined the houses in the block as if looking for some landmark.</p>
<p>"I was so frightened I almost forgot," she murmured, and if her face could have been seen it would have revealed a blush of sweet consciousness.</p>
<p>She walked more leisurely now, and as she neared the corner of Seventh Avenue she looked eagerly yet furtively around.</p>
<p>The under cover of the darkness her cheeks took on a rosier hue still, a little panting murmer of joy came from between her parted kips, and her brown eyes were cast demurely down.</p>
<pb n="12 Alone in the Streets."/>
<p>One would have thought she could not see anything that took
place. But it would have been a mistake, for she did see a man's figure start from the shadow of a house and approach her with quick, eager strides.</p>
<p>"Is it you, Viola?" fell on her ear the next moment, and the rich, baritone voice vibrated in her heart till it was tremulous with a sweet delight.</p>
<p>"Is it you, Mr. Walter?" she answered timidly.</p>
<p>"Yes, I have been waiting for you. I cannot bear to have you walk home alone so late. I may go with you, mayn't I?"</p>
<p>"I am afraid it will be a trouble to you," she murmured faintly.</p>
<p>"A trouble!" he repeated in a low, reproachful tone. "Oh, you know better than that. I waited for you because there is nothing else I care so much for as a walk with you."</p>

<pb n="[13]"/>
<div id="gfprice002" type="chapter">
<head rend = "center">CHAPTER II.</head>
<byline>LAW FOR TWO HEARTS.</byline>
<p>They walked along silently for a while, perhaps because they were too happy to talk, perhaps because what they might have to say could be said more impressively in some more secluded street than Seventh Avenue.</p>
<p>People turned and looked at them as they passed. Nor was it strange that they should, for the two made a pretty picture.</p>
<p>He had the lithe, sinewy frame and the broad shoulders of an athlete, and the face of an Apollo. Besides, he was dressed in the height of the fashion, and was plainly a swell.</p>
<p>Her veil covered her face so that none could see its exquisite beauty, but shabby as her gown and coat were, they were trim and jaunty, and revealed a form so rounded and graceful that it was easy to suspect a beautiful face behind the veil.</p>
<p>They walked together as if they had forgotten all the rest of the world. His hand, unrebuked, held hers in a firm, warm grasp, and they walked in step, her plump shoulder snuggled unconsciously against his stalwart arm.</p>
<p>At the first corner he led her into the quieter side
<pb n="14 Law for Two Hearts."/>
street, and she did not resist, though for some reason her breath came a little quicker.</p>
<p>"I hate that noisy avenue," he said, in a low tone. "It is better
than the quieter streets when one is alone," she answered, in a tone as low as his own. "You ought not be alone in the streets at night,"
he said earnestly.</p>
<p>"It is only during the Christmas shopping season that I am out so late," she replied. "Besides, I keep on the lighted avenue, and no one is likely to trouble me."</p>
<p>"But it is better for you to have some one with you," he said. "Are you glad to have me with you?"</p>
<p>There was silence for a moment, then she answered in a voice that was a little unsteady:</p>
<p>"I&#x2014;I am always pleased to see you, Mr. Walter. You have been so kind to me ever since you saved me from those drunken men. But&#x2014;but&#x2014;"</p>
<p>"Well?" he asked softly, as she hesitated.</p>
<p>"I&#x2014;I am afraid it is not right to meet you in this way. You&#x2014;you know that your father would be angry if he knew."</p>
<p>He made a gesture of impatience.</p>
<p>"Father might at first," he said. "But he is always willing to let me have my own way."</p>
<p>Viola sighed, then turned with a gesture of decision, and said quickly, as if she feared to give herself time for hesitation:</p>
<pb n="15 Law for Two Hearts."/>
<p>"I have enjoyed knowing you so much, Mr. Walter, and I will confess that I have looked forward to meeting you. But I know it cannot be best, for I have not dared to tell mamma about it. I know she would say that I must not do it."</p>
<p>"You mean," he exclaimed reproachfully, "that I must not wait for you to take you home these nights that you are out so late?"</p>
<p>I&#x2014;I think it is the only way," she faltered. "Besides&#x2014;</p>
<p>"Besides what?" he demanded, a little bitterly.</p>
<p>"Besides, I cannot&#x2014;dare not&#x2014;do anything that will be likely to cost me my position in the store. If your father should have the faintest idea of our meeting he would discharge me. And if I were to lose my place now, what would become of my mother? I must think of her, you know."</p>
<p>"If I were only in the store it would not be so," he said moodily. "I have asked him to let me be a member of the firm in reality as well as in name. But he won't. It is Hardman & Son, but he won't even let me learn the business. If he should die, no one would know anything about it but that sneak, Peter Harriem."</p>
<p>Viola shuddered involuntarily at the name, and Walter noticed it at once, and cried out with keen intuition:</p>
<pb n="16 Law for Two Hearts."/>
<p>"Has that snake been saying anything to you, Viola?"</p>
<p>Viola feared to tell the young man what had occurred.</p>
<p>"He&#x2014;he wished to&#x2014;to walk home with me to-night," she answered, in a low tone, "but I told him I would rather go alone."</p>
<p>"Confound his impudence!" said Walter Hardman angrily. "How dare he?"</p>
<p>Viola did not speak, nor did Walter for some time, but walked by her side in moody silence. Suddenly he broke out:</p>
<p>"Viola, I shan't give up taking you home these nights. You ought not ask it."</p>
<p>She trembled at his vehemence, but did not yield. "I must ask it," she said.</p>
<p>"Only because you are afraid of losing your place?" he asked.</p>
<p>"Not only that," she answered; and then went on reproachfully: "Oh, Mr. Walter! it is not like you to be selfish. Can't you see that it may put me in a false position?"</p>
<p>"No, I can't. How?" he retorted.</p>
<p>"You are rich, and I am poor. You are a gentleman with certain
prospects in life, and I am only a salesgirl in your father's store. If we were seen together there would be talk. You ought not
make me tell you this."</p>
<pb n="17 Law for Two Hearts."/>
<p>"Somebody has told you that I am engaged to Eunice Carroll?" he cried, in sharp, angry tones.</p>
<p>"No," she panted, involuntarily drawing away from him. "I did not know that. Aristocratic Eunice Carroll! I never heard that."</p>
<p>"But it is not true," he said eagerly, taking his place by her side again. "My father wishes me to marry her because she is mad on the subject of aristocratic birth and position. Self-made men are always that way."</p>
<p>"It is another reason why we should not meet again," Viola said, in a low tone.</p>
<p>She tried to make herself believe that she had expected something of the sort from the first; but there was a misery in her heart she could not quell.</p>
<p>But Walter would not accept her conclusion. He bent lower, and said softly in her ear:</p>:
<p>"Viola, it is not a reason for not meeting again. I care nothing for her, and I will not marry her to please my father. I shall marry where my heart is or not at all."</p>
<p>"We must not meet again," she said, in a voice she vainly tried to keep firm. "I&#x2014;I will say good-night here. There is my house. Good-night!"</p>
<p>"No," he said, holding her gently. "I cannot let you go now. We have said too much or too little; too little, I think. Viola, I did not mean to speak to you to-night!
I&#x2014;I was afraid you were not ready&#x2014;"</p>
<pb n="18 Law for Two Hearts."/>
<p>"Mr. Walter," panted Viola, in a sort of exquisite terror, which she could not have defined, "I&#x2014;I&#x2014;it is late. Let me go!"</p>
<p>"Not until I have told you that I love you, Viola. I must say it, dear. I&#x2014;I know that you are not ready to hear me say it. But I cannot let you go after what has been said without telling you that I love you.</p>
<p>"I do not expect you to love me, dear. I know it is sudden; I know I am unworthy of you. But you are sending me away from you, and I cannot go without a word of hope. Viola, darling, won't you try to love me? You don't speak. Is there some other? Oh, Viola!"</p>
<p>They were in a deserted part of the street, with no one likely to see or overhear them.</p>
<p>His arm had crept about her waist, but at the miserable thought that she might love another, he drew it away and caught both her hands in his.</p>
<p>She held her head averted, and did not answer. If he had not loved her so ardently he might have known by her silence and by her quick breathing that her answer would not be unfavorable.</p>
<p>"Viola," he cried passionately, "why do you not answer me? It is true we have known each other but three months. But I loved you from the first, and have loved you better and better all the time.</p>
<pb n="19 Law for Two Hearts."/>
<p>"All my thoughts have been of you. I have dreamed of you at night, and have lived through the days on the hope of meeting you at night when you went home from the store.</p>
<p>"My heart has bled for you as you toiled so hard, and I have prayed Heaven that you would love me so that I could rescue you from the want and care that oppress you by making you my wife.</p>
<p>"Viola, is there another whom you love better? Is there some one you have given your heart to? Is there? Won't you answer me that, dear?"</p>
<p>She shook her head. Her heart was too full for speech, and he could not see the happiness in her face.</p>
<p>"There is no one?" he cried. "Then may I hope that by and by you will learn to love me? Think, dear, of the happiness of a little home of your own, where you can give your mother all the care and attention she needs. Won't you give me one word of hope, Viola, dear?"</p>
<p>His arm was around her waist again, and his face was close to hers.</p>
<p>He was pleading with all the fervor of his soul, hardly daring to hope, and yet each moment filled with an increasing thrill of joy.</p>
<p>She did not draw away from him, she did not give a sign of repugnance.</p>
<pb n="20 Law for Two Hearts."/>
<p>And now he could feel the trembling of the hand he held.</p>
<p>He uttered a passionate cry of hope and happiness as it was borne in upon his heart that she was silent from acquiescence and shyness.</p>
<p>"Viola, my love!" he cried, pressing her to his heart. "Do I read your silence aright? May I hope?"</p>
<p>Her head fell on his breast, and a low sob broke from her lips.</p>
<p>"Oh, I love you," he heard her say; "but how can it ever be? I love you, but we must part."</p>
<p>It seemed almost as if he would crush her in the 'ecstasy of his joy.</p>
<p>He snatched the envious veil away from her beautiful face and rained fervent kisses on her lips.</p>
"Part!" he cried. Do you think I will ever let you go? Never! If you love me, nothing can keep us apart. I will never give you up. Promise me, Viola, that you will never be persuaded to give me up. What higher law than love is there for two hearts? Promise me, Viola!"</div>
<p>"Your father?" she murmured, finding it easy to yield to his loving vehemence.</p>
<p>"My father will make a fuss at first," he replied confidently. "But he only lives to make me happy. I am his infatuation. He hoards his money for me to spend. He will do as I wish, never fear."</p>
<pb n="21 Law for Two Hearts."/>
<p>"But he is proud and anxious for a lofty marriage for you. He will be disappointed," said Viola.</p>
<p>Walter laughed as men laugh whose happiness has intoxicated them so that all obstacles seem shadowy.</p>
<p>"I will answer for my father. Let us go see your mother."</p>


<pb n="[22]"/>
<div id="gfprice003" type="chapter">
<head rend = "center">CHAPTER III.</head>
<byline>"COME TO MY ARMS."</byline>
<p>Peter Harriem, meanwhile, had looked after the fleeing form of the beautiful salesgirl, a scowl of chagrin on his face.</p>
<p>It was humiliating to be left in that way, just as he seemed on the point of conquering.</p>
<p>He looked for a moment with his head thrust forward very much in the manner of a snake that has struck at some creature which has escaped just in time.</p>
<p>Then his shoulders drooped, and he slunk across the street to the opposite side, and followed Viola as she sped down the pavement.</p>
<p>He seemed fitted by nature for what is known in detective parlance as a "shadow," for he seemed to know how to melt his attenuated form into the very railings in front of the houses.</p>
<p>He could not have told why he followed her. Probably he was merely doing what was most natural to him.</p>
<p>Craft, and guile, and subtlety were his processes always.</p>
<p>When he saw Viola joined by Walter he started, and uttered an exclamation of surprise, showing that he had not really expected to discover anything.</p>
<pb n="23 Come to My Arms."/>
<p>In fact, he was not yet near enough to be able to distinguish who it was that had been waiting for the pretty girl he had marked for his own prey.</p>
<p>All that was clear to him was that the young man was well dressed, and bore himself like a gentleman.</p>
<p>"So that's it, eh?" he muttered to himself.</p>
<p>"She has a swell for a lover, already. That is why she spurns my advances. I thought it was odd. And yet she looks so innocent! You never can tell with girls. They can give us men points in deception."</p>
<p>It never occurred to his base mind to doubt that Viola had fallen a victim to the greater attractions of some young rou&#x00E9;.</p>
<p>"Well," he muttered, as he sneaked after the lovers, "she is too pretty to give up right away. My turn may come yet. I will see his face, and that may help me to an idea. I am not handsome; but I have more wit and cunning than better-looking men."</p>
<p>He chuckled as he spoke; for his own words recalled to him the numerous times that he had set out to accomplish something, and had succeeded.</p>
<p>While the lovers stood in the shadow and opened their hearts to each other, he stood on the opposite side of the street, under a stoop, watching them with all his eyes.</p>
<p>He saw all that happened, but heard nothing, though
<pb n="24 Come to My Arms."/>
he racked his brains to think of some way of catching only a few words.</p>
<p>There was no doubt in his mind, however, that Viola loved the young aristocrat, and he was surprised to discover how angry the thought made him.</p>
<p>"Curse the girl!" he muttered. "Why did she want to fall in love with him? It would have been better for her to look on me with favor. I can do more for her, and would not tire of her as soon. But I will find some way of getting between them. Some brainless dude, no doubt."</p>
<p>He cared more for Viola than he had supposed. He had watched her covertly for weeks, and had gloated over her with the thought that when the time was ripe, he would get her into his power.</p>
<p>And now that he had decided to take the first step toward winning her, this stranger had stepped in and taken her from him.</p>
<p>He ground his teeth as he watched Walter pressing her rosebud lips. He had looked at those perfect lips many a time with silent gloating, dreaming of the time when they would be free to him.</p>
<p>He had gazed at her rounded form with delight. And he hated the man whose arm was about her waist now.</p>
<p>"I would have married her," he hissed to himself. "That young aristocrat will ruin her!"</p>
<pb n="25 Come to My Arms."/>
<p>In truth his motives from the first had been of the very basest. It was only now that he realized that her wonderful beauty had captured what took the place of a heart in his despicable nature.</p>
<p>While the two were occupied with their happiness, and were oblivious of all that passed about them, he stole from his hiding place and sneaked to the corner, where he knew they must pass.</p>
<p>He drew his hat low, turned his coat collar up, and crouched against the wall like a homeless tramp, waiting for them to come by.</p>
<p>And at last they did come by, the veil still off the sweet face, now irradiated with joy and fixed with passionate gaze to the face of Walter Hardman.</p>
<p>They passed, and Peter Harriem gazed after them with baleful eyes fixed on the young man.</p>
<p>"Walter Hardman!" he gasped. "That whelp! It is something else I owe him for. He scorns me, and looks down upon me, the white-handed aristocrat! Now he has stolen her from me. Oh, it will be a very sweet revenge! How I hate him!<sic/></p>
<p>"He has always had all that has been denied to me; he has had ease and luxury from his babyhood; he has been petted and spoiled; he is handsome and talented.</p>
<p>"I was born virtually in the gutter. I have had nothing but hard work and cold looks. I have been kicked
<pb n="26 Come to My Arms."/>
and cuffed from place to place. I am ugly and commonplace.</p>
<p>"But the money he was born to will some day be mine. And money is power. I shall make men bow to me because I hold the wealth to make them. And he will have to work with his white hands, or starve. Curse him! I hope he will starve.</p>
<p>"And now he comes to take my beautiful Viola. But I will tear them apart, and bring her to my feet. I will punish them both, and yet possess her. She shall be mine.</p>
<p>"I will work and scheme and plot until he is ruined, and she is
forced to come to my arms. Then let her shudder because I touch her dainty arm! t<sic corr="T"/>hen let her start from me!"</p>
<p>He half crept, half slouched away, his shambling form seeming to move sideways as he progressed.</p>
<p>He had a room in a great, lonely building in an out-of-the-way quarter of the city, because it was cheaper to rent a small room and buy only what he wanted to eat.</p>
<p>He would not let any rapacious landlady get rich at his expense.</p>
<p>So he lived in the cheerless little room, cooked his own breakfast and supper, and bought only his dinner in a cheap restaurant, where he could tell just which were the cheapest dishes to buy.</p>
<pb n="27 Come to My Arms."/>
<p>He dressed well because Silas Hardman demanded that of everybody connected with Hardman & Son.</p>
<p>Now that he was in his cold room, he threw off his good clothes, donned a shabby suit, far too short in the legs and arms for him, and then lighted the fire in the little cookstove.</p>
<p>His supper was a simple affair, and did not take long to cook&#x2014;a tiny piece of chuck steak and two slices of stale bread.</p>
<p>He had discovered that stale bread could be bought for two cents less a loaf than fresh bread.</p>
<p>After his supper he paced the room so that he might think of what he would better do in relation to Viola and Walter. Besides, if he walked he would not need much fire in the stove.</p>
</div>

<pb n="[28]"/>
<div id="gfprice004" type="chapter">
<head rend = "center">CHAPTER IV.</head>
<byline>"I LOVE THAT SALESGIRL."</byline>
<p>Silas Hardman, grim and keen-eyed, sat in his private office on the second floor of the great building in which the enormous business of Hardman & Son was carried on.</p>
<p>Excepting for Walter, he cared for no human being. The business of his life was to amass more and more wealth, until at last it should be said of him that no one was richer.</p>
<p>Walter was to be a gentleman, and to have all that he never had had, or even cared for. Walter should take his place in the world of fashion, and be talked of in the papers.</p>
<p>Walter might have his yacht and his horses. He must belong to the fashionable clubs, and be a first-nighter at the theaters. There <hi rend="italic">was</hi> a box at the opera and a country seat up the Hudson.</p>
<p>And Walter was to marry into the most exclusive family in New York. He was to ally himself to beauty, wealth, and blood. Eunice Carroll seemed to have fulfilled Walter's fate by loving him.</p>
<p>Silas Hardman had discovered it by accident, and had hailed it as a dispensation of Providence. Walter
<pb n="29 I Love That Salesgirl."/>
was to marry Eunice. And it never had occurred to his father that he would object.</p>
<p>There was something inflexible in the love of the elder for his son. It almost seemed as if he was trying to live Walter's life for him.</p>
<p>Walter wondered sometimes if his father was not vicariously enjoying the youth he had lost.</p>
<p>Peter Harriem, with his sphinxlike face and reptile manner, stood in front of Mr. Hardman, reading, in a low, yet distinctly audible tone, from some papers relating to the affairs of the firm.</p>
<p>Mr. Hardman listened intently, and made curt comments, which the other noted down with a subtle reverence of manner which pleased the employer. Any other sort of flattery he would have resented, but he liked that. It seemed so unintentional.</p>
<p>While they were still thus occupied, there came a soft tap at the door. Peter looked with humble inquiry at Mr. Hardman.</p>
<p>"Come in!" said Mr. Hardman curtly, and with a note of impatience in his tone.</p>
<p>The door opened, and with flushed face and hesitating step, Viola entered, carrying a package, which she handed silently to Peter, who stood quite still, so that she was obliged to cross the room to him.</p>
<p>Mr. Hardman looked at her with cold, inquiring
<pb n="30 I Love That Salesgirl."/>
eyes, seeing her exquisite beauty without being affected by it.</p>
<p>"Lace samples from Merivale, Parker & Co.," said Peter.</p>
<p>"Go!" said Mr. Hardman curtly.</p>
<p>Viola fled with trepidation, wondering in her quaking heart how that man could be the father of her Walter.</p>
<p>"Why was she sent?" demanded Mr. Hardman, in his cold way. "Are there no messengers?"</p>
<p>"If you please, Mr. Hardman," answered Peter, in his most cringing tone, "I left word to have her sent up."</p>
<p>Mr. Hardman was never angry in the usual sense. It was seldom necessary. He glanced with surprised coldness at his confidential man, and said:</p>
<p>"Why?"</p>
<p>"Will you pardon me, Mr. Hardman," said Peter humbly, "if I explain in my own way? I assure you it is a matter of importance."</p>
<p>"Go on; you know by this time that I do not wish to waste time. What is it you wish to tell me?"</p>
<p>He supposed there had been a theft, in which the girl was mixed up.</p>
<p>"Did you notice how unusually beautiful the girl was, sir?" Peter asked, his manner apologizing for him for referring to such a matter in business hours.</p>
<pb n="31 I Love That Salesgirl."/>
<p>"I suppose she was beautiful. Yes. Well?" was the curt and somewhat surprised rejoinder.</p>
<p>"I hope," Peter went on, effacing himself more and more, "that I do not need to tell you that I am devoted to your interests?"</p>
<p>"I pay you to be," was the curt, cynical response.</p>
<p>"Yes, sir, you pay me too liberally, and that's why I try to do my duty. It is a new and painful duty now, sir."</p>
<p>"Then waste no time in doing it, Harriem,'' said the other, as impatiently as he ever permitted himself to speak.</p>
<p>"It was quite by accident, sir, but as I went home last night, I saw this girl being embraced and kissed on one of the side streets by a young man."</p>
<p>"Discharge her! Why should I be bothered with the shameless story?"</p>
<p>"The young man, sir," said Peter, dropping his voice to a sibilant whisper, "was Walter."</p>
<p>A dead silence followed. Silas Hardman grew white, and the hand that held the ebony penholder trembled a little. Then the lines around his mouth harlened<sic/>, and he spoke.</p>
<p>"You did well to tell me. You have the honor of my name at heart. You shall not regret it. We expect these things of young men about town. I only wish it had been with some one not in our employ. We
<pb n="32 I Love That Salesgirl."/>
will have to provide for the girl in some way. Walter must be more careful. He will be here this morning."</p>
<p>Peter knew it would be unwise to press the subject, and only said:</p>
<p>"I hope, sir, you will not let me incur Mr. Walter's ill will by revealing my part in the matter."</p>
<p>"Certainly not. Go!"</p>
<p>And that was how the subject of the working girl's probable ruin was dismissed from the mind of Silas Hardman. It would be demoralizing to discipline to have Walter ruining the salesgirls of Hardman & Son.</p>
<p>Walter came in just as Peter was leaving the office. The latter bowed with a cringing humility that disgusted Walter, and made him return it with a curt nod.</p>
<p>"Good morning, father!" he said cheerily, as the door closed on the shambling head clerk.</p>
<p>The change in the expression of Silas Hardman was something marvelous. It could be seen that the love and affection which the ordinary man bestows on many was in him all concentrated on Walter.</p>
<p>"My boy," he murmured, "what is it this morning? More money for your extravagance, eh, you young dog?"</p>
<p>"No, father," replied Walter; "you never let me have a chance to run short of money. I came to see you about a more important matter than that."</p>
<p>"More important than money!" cried his father, ris-
<pb n="33 I Love That Salesgirl."/>
ing and slapping him genially on the shoulder. "Oh, oh, what is that, I would like to know?"</p>
<p>He laughed as he spoke. No one but Walter ever heard him laugh.</p>
<p>"Love and marriage are more important than money, father, I think."</p>
<p>"Oh-o!" said his father; "it has come to this, has it? Well, my boy, I am rejoiced to hear it. But, Walter, this is a very busy day with me. I shall be home at four o'clock this afternoon. Let us leave the matter till then. Will that suit? I shall be rejoiced to talk with you about it then."</p>
<p>"All right, father, I would not have come here, only I was afraid I would have no other chance to talk with you. You are so busy. I wish you would let me help you.</p>
<p>"Tut, tut! your business is to enjoy yourself, Walter. Oh, by the way!"</p>
<p>He lowered his voice and drew Walter toward the window that looked out on Sixth Avenue. Walter stared wonderingly at him.</p>
<p>"What is it, father?" he asked.</p>
<p>"Walter," said his father, "I know well enough that young men will be young men. I don't want you to be a saint. I know, too, what a temptation a pretty girl is wherever you find her. I never had time for
<pb n="34 I Love That Salesgirl."/>
that sort of thing, myself, but, of course, it is different with you."</p>
<p>Walter flushed and looked uncomfortable, but did not quite comprehend.</p>
<p>"Well, father?" he said, looking into the serious face of the other.</p>
<p>"Well," Silas Hardman said, with an expression that told how sorry he was to find any fault with his son, "I would not inquire into your little escapades, and I don't mean to say there is any harm in them, only I wish you would not select any of the girls of the establishment. See?"</p>
<p>The flush of embarrassment died out of Walter's checks, and gave place to a dull white.</p>
<p>"Just what do you mean, father?" he asked, in a low tone.</p>
<p>"There, now! don't be hurt, Walter," the other said soothingly. "I happened to hear how you had been kissing her on the street last night. But don't look so worried over it. I don't think anything of it, my boy. I know what young men of fashion are. Now, good-by and this afternoon we will talk over this matter of your marriage. By the way, be careful not to let Eunice get wind of the pretty little salesgirl. Good-by</p>
<p>"Father!" Walter's face was white, and there was a note of vehement indignation in his voice.</p>
<pb n="35 I Love That Salesgirl."/>
<p>"Well, Walter? I am afraid you are angry with me. I didn't mean to make you angry. I thought I was doing the right thing, my boy."</p>
<p>"You don't understand," Walter said, in a low tone. "I did not come here to speak of marriage with Eunice Carroll. I&#x2014;"</p>
<p>"Well, never mind! I had set my heart on Eunice, but if there is some one else just as good, I shall make no objection. If that is all, why&#x2014;"</p>	
<p>"But it is not all, father. I came to speak of one a thousand times more good and beautiful than Eunice Carroll."</p>
<p>"What a persistent boy!" said his father, in a sort of good-natured impatience. "Why won't you wait until this afternoon?"</p>
<p>"Because there is not one moment to lose before I set you right. I could not go away and leave the one I love under an imputation so false and outrageous, so insulting and base. I must tell you now, father, that the woman I love and will make my wife is that salesgirl of whom you have spoken such terrible words."</p>
<p>Silas Hardman turned ghastly white and looked at Walter with incredulous, yet horrified eyes.</p>
<p>"Walter!" he gasped.</p>
<p>"Yes, father, it is true," answered Walter, throwing his head back with a gesture of proud nobility; "I love Viola Redmond, and intend to make her my wife!"</p>
</div>

<pb n="[36]"/>
<div id="gfprice005" type="chapter">
<head rend = "center">CHAPTER V.</head>
<byline>THE GREATEST DEVOTION.</byline>
<p>All his life long Walter had been indulged by his father in every whim; it seemed to him that his father could refuse him nothing.</p>
<p>Perhaps the same thought flashed through the mind of Silas Hardman as he turned his white, set face toward his son.</p>
<p>Never before had there been even a difference between them; for Walter had never asked for anything that his father had not been more than willing to grant him.</p>
<p>The first impulse of the elder man was to break out in furious denial of Walter's right to choose for himself in this matter.</p>
<p>But, as he gazed into the defiant face of his son, it came to him that it might be easier to make a breach than to heal it.</p>
<p>With any other person he would have been hard and unyielding. It seemed impossible to be so with Walter; and yet he did not dream of letting his son have his own way.</p>
<p>The hard, stern light faded out of his eyes as he looked at the handsome face of the boy he worshiped,
<pb n="37 The Greatest Devotion."/>
and it came to him that there was more than one way of having his will.</p>
<p>"Is your heart so set on this girl, Walter?" he said, in a low tone.</p>
<p>"I can never love any but her, father," answered Walter, with a secret exultation at seeing how mild his father was.</p>
<p>It seemed to him that this was turning out exactly as he had expected. His father could not cross him.</p>
<p>"Are you sure of it?" the other asked. "How long have you known her?"</p>
<p>"I have known her for about three months," Walter answered eagerly. "I loved her from the moment I saw her, and I have grown to love her better every day.</p>
<p>Silas Hardman bit his lip with anger. It was not, as he had hoped, merely the infatuation of a young man for a pretty face.</p>
<p>"Are you engaged?" his father asked.</p>
<p>"Yes, I told her of my love last night, and went home with her to ask her mother's consent."</p>
<p>"And the mother," sneered Silas Hardman, "was only too glad to say yes."</p>
<p>Walter smiled triumphantly.</p>
<p>"You, are entirely mistaken, father," he said quickly. "She said she could not consent to the engagement unless you consented first. She would not have it
<pb n="38 The Greatest Devotion."/>
look as if she and her daughter had entrapped the son of a rich man."</p>
<p>"And what did you say to that, my boy?" the older man inquired.</p>
<p>"I said you had never denied me anything yet, and I did not believe you would begin when my whole happiness was dependent on your consent."</p>
<p>"Do you mean, then, Walter," said the older man, "that if I say no, it will be final with you?"</p>
<p>Walter threw his head back with his proud gesture.</p>
<p>"No, sir; I would not like to run counter to your wishes, but this is a matter in which I admit of no interference."</p>
<p>"And you would marry. her though I refused my consent?" his father asked, eying Walter keenly. "What then becomes of the unwillingness of the mother? Is the daughter ready to defy her parent, too?"</p>
<p>"I have not asked her, nor considered it," Walter replied. "I was sure you would consent if you knew that my happiness depended on it. And if you will but see her, father, and speak with her, I am sure you will consent."</p>
<p>"I have seen her. What is her name?"</p>
<p>"Viola Redmond."</p>
<p>"Ah, yes! And you think she will marry you whether I consent or not?"</p>
<pb n="39 The Greatest Devotion."/>
<p>I think she so loves me that she will wait until I have earned the right to go to her and say that I can take care of her by my own efforts," Walter answered.</p>
<p>"Then, if I refuse, you will cut loose from the father who has given his whole life to you?" queried Silas Hardman, his voice breaking with an emotion that was strange in his breast.</p>
<p>Walter went over to his father and placed his hand on his shoulder.</p>
<p>"Why do you put it that way, father?" he said. "I would do anything else but give her up. I cannot do that. If I told you I would, I could not keep my promise, for I love her with a passion that would control me in spite of all. Why not, consent? She is all that even you could ask. She is poor, it is true, but what of that? We are rich enough. And she has such beauty, purity, and goodness as you could find in no other woman. Eunice Carroll is not to be thought of in comparison with Viola."</p>
<p>The old man walked up and down the room, his brain seething with fierce, furious thoughts toward the girl who tried to rob him of his son.</p>
<p>But he betrayed nothing in his hard, impassive face. He had schooled his countenance, long ago, never to betray what was in his heart.</p>
<p>"And if I consent," he said slowly, stopping and
<pb n="40 The Greatest Devotion."/>
facing Walter, "would you be willing to wait the time set by me before your marriage?"</p>
<p>"There is little I would not do to earn your consent, father!" cried Walter, with eager joy.</p>
<p>A gleam of triumph lighted up the cold eyes of Silas Hardman.</p>
<p>"I distrust a love that is so sudden," he said slowly.</p>
<p>Walter smiled confidently.</p>
<p>"If that is all," he said, "you need have no fear. I love her truly, and can never love any other."</p>
<p>"But how can you be sure that she loves you equally well?" his father asked.</p>
<p>"If you knew her you would not ask that!" Walter responded, with a lover's confidence. "She is truth itself."</p>
<p>"How can you be sure," his father persisted, "that you will not change? You have seen so little of the world. If you had done as I wished you to, if you had visited all the gay capitals of Europe and met the beauties there&#x2014;if you had gone the rounds of the fashionable summer resorts as I have begged you to do, you might have seen some one who would be more to you than this pretty salesgirl."</p>
<p>"That is impossible, father," answered Walter earnestly. "I love Viola, and it is not possible to love more than one in all the world as I love her. I might have liked some other well enough to think I could
<pb n="41 The Greatest Devotion."/>
wed her. But if in the workings of fate I had afterward seen Viola, I should have been obliged to love her even as I do now."</p>
<p>In his heart Silas Hardman cursed the girl; but on his thin lips there was an incredulous smile, as if he knew his son was only talking the nonsense of youth.</p>
<p>"If I thought that," he said, "I would not hesitate a moment; but I cannot believe it, knowing the world as I do."</p>
<p>"If you could put me to the test in any way," Walter said eagerly, "you would find that what I say is true."</p>
<p>Again the cold eyes gleamed with furtive triumph. It was the point to which, with his icy cunning, he had been leading his son.</p>
<p>"A test," he said, as if he had not before thought of such a thing. "How can there be a test? If I could think of any I would gladly accept your suggestion."</p>
<p>He thus threw the burden of the idea on Walter, and the latter, in his open honesty, never dreamed that all his father had said before was but to lead him up to this thought.</p>
<p>"We can find a test," Walter said quickly, pleased with the
thought. "Try us anyhow you please."</p>
<p>His father put out his hand with a smile.</p>
<p>"Walter," he said, "I was vexed when you told me the truth, but it was the best thing to do. I do seek
<pb n="42 The Greatest Devotion."/>
your happiness above everything else. I had other plans for you, but if you can be happy only in this way, why, we must be reconciled to it."</p>
<p>Walter shook his hand with fervent joy.</p>
<p>"You are always good to me," he said. "You do consent, then?"</p>
<p>"Consent! Well, I suppose it amounts to that. You spoke of a test. I do not wish you to take a step you will regret. I must think of some test, if I can. If I cannot, then I must submit and take your word for it."</p>
<p>"Thank you, sir! oh, thank you! I have not told you, because it was impossible, how much I love Viola. And you will love her, too."</p>
<p>Silas Hardman smiled grimly.</p>
<p>"You talk as if you had passed successfully through the test you suggested. Why, I cannot even be sure that you will be willing to apply the test when I have thought of it."</p>
<p>"I will, father," cried Walter eagerly. "Do not be afraid of that. I shall be glad to prove to you the depth and strength of my passion for her."</p>
<p>"You promise, then, that you will not rebel and say the test is too severe?"</p>
<p>"You will not make it an unreasonable one?" queried Walter.</p>
<p>His father laughed in his grim way, but Walter saw by his face that he was pleased.</p>
<pb n="43 The Greatest Devotion."/>
<p>"You may, and no doubt will, think it unreasonable, but I shall endeavor to think of something which shall be fair to you and to her."</p>
<p>"Then you need not doubt that I will accept your judgment, father. When will you let me know what it is?"</p>
<p>"I shall be home at four this afternoon. By that time I shall have thought of something. Come there."</p>
<p>Walter smiled joyously, and caught up his hat.</p>
<p>"I shall be there, father," he said. "Sorry to have interrupted your morning. Good-by!"</p>
<p>"You will say nothing to the girl until we have talked the matter over and reached a final decision?" his father said.</p>
<p>"I shall not be able to see her until to-night," Walter answered. "I am to meet her and take her home to-night when she leaves the store. I would not attract attention to her by talking with her in the store."</p>
<p>"That is right," his father said. "Good-by, then, and don't bother a busy man any more, you idle dog!"</p>
<p>"I would not be an idle dog if you would only let me work," Walter replied, with a joyous laugh.</p>
<p>Silas Hardman stood at his desk in silence for a moment after the door closed; then, with a sudden hardening of the lines about the straight mouth, he pushed the electric bell which he used to call Peter Harriem.</p>
</div>

<pb n="[44]"/>
<div id="gfprice006" type="chapter">
<head rend = "center">CHAPTER VI.</head>
<byline>"NEVER WED SUCH A GIRL."</byline>
<p>Peter was sitting at his desk, his long, lean legs wound about each other, his yellow teeth gnawing viciously at his scanty mustache, when the bell summoned him to his employer.</p>
<p>He would have given all the world for the chance to hear all that passed between the father and son, but circumstances were not favorable for listening at the door.</p>
<p>He had rendered it so purposely when he did not dream that anybody but himself would hold confidential conversations with Silas Hardman.</p>
<p>Walter had passed him without noticing him, and in the furtive glance Peter stole at his handsome face he saw that it was irradiated with happiness.</p>
<p>Then, while the bell was still ringing, he rose and gazed after the young man instead of hastening with celerity to his employer.</p>
<p>He waited until he saw Walter pass by the counter where Viola was stationed&#x2014;where he had put her, in fact, so that he might surreptitiously feast his eyes on her beauty.</p>
<p>He noticed that Walter did not stop to say any-
<pb n="45 Never Wed Such a Girl."/>
thing, but that he gave a quick, almost imperceptible nod, which she seemed to comprehend, for in an instant her fair face was suffused with a flush of happiness.</p>
<p>"Has the old fool yielded in this as in everything else?" he hissed to himself. "Will he consent to let that whelp ruin his salesgirl? Curse him if he has! I will fight him, too. No one shall take her from me."</p>
<p>He turned and crept in his stealthy way to the office upstairs, where Silas Hardman was impatiently awaiting him.</p>
<p>He glided into the room, casting one furtive glance at the hard face of the old man, and then taking his place in front of the desk with humble attention.</p>
<p>"Finish the papers!" the old man said curtly.</p>
<p>The papers were finished, and Peter made his usual report.</p>
<p>In fact all the business of the morning was concluded, and Peter, with fierce chagrin, believed he was to be dismissed without hearing a word that would enlighten him.</p>
<p>He turned toward the door with reluctant step. The old man let him reach the door and place his hand on the knob ere he recalled him.</p>
<p>"Stop! there is something else," he said. "You did well to inform me of what you saw."</p>
<pb n="46 Never Wed Such a Girl."/>
<p>"I am glad if I have pleased you," Peter whined, his shifting gaze telling him that there was more to come.</p>
<p>He did not, however, take his hand from the knob, but stood there as if he had no thought of remaining longer than to hear a brief word or two.</p>
<p>"Come back here," said Silas Hardman. "You thought Walter intended the ruin of this girl&#x2014;Viola Something."</p>
<p>"Viola Redmond; yes, sir," assented Peter, his snaky eyes trying to read the old man's thoughts.</p>
<p>"Well, you were wrong. He wanted to marry her. To marry her! Curse the girl!" said the old man, with a quiet vehemence and passion more terrible than a violent outburst.</p>
<p>"Im-possible!" ejaculated Peter slowly.</p>
<p>"Yes, you may well say impossible, but it is so. My son marry such as she! I do not know by what low cunning she entrapped my boy, but it is certain that he is infatuated with her."</p>
<p>He thumped the desk in anger. Peter stared at him with growing passion.</p>
<p>Why had Walter gone out in triumphant happiness? Had his father consented to the marriage in spite of his dislike to it?</p>
<p>Well, even that should never be. Did they think Peter Harriem could be thrust aside so easily? Let them see!</p>
<pb n="47 Never Wed Such a Girl."/>
<p>"And you consented to the marriage?" insinuated the cringing wretch.</p>
<p>"Consented!" repeated Silas Hardman, with angry vehemence. "Why do you think that?"</p>
<p>"I saw Mr. Walter's face when he passed me. He looked as happy as one does who has gained his will."</p>
<p>"Ah!" said the old man briefly. "He thinks I have consented. But we shall see. Do you wish to help me in this affair? It may be worth your while, even though it is not strictly business."</p>
<p>"Anything is strictly business that concerns you, sir," answered Peter, in his wheedling tone.</p>
<p>"Yes, I know you are faithful, or I would not have thought of you. I did not dare refuse Walter for, fear he would do something rash. He is innocent and off his guard; cunning will accomplish the same end better. He must not marry that girl."</p>
<p>Peter softly and stealthily rubbed his lean hands to gether. The game was to be in his hands after all.</p>
<p>"It would be a disgrace," he murmured. "I will do anything I can. Have you any idea of a way, sir?"</p>
<p>"Yes; and I need you to help me carry it out."</p>
<p>"You know you can depend on me, sir."</p>
<p>"I shall make it worth your while," the old man said coldly. He intended to pay well for the service. "Walter has consented to submit to a test of his love. I thought to send him away for a year. I could send
<pb n="48 Never Wed Such a Girl."/>
him to London and Paris to transact some trifling business for the house."</p>
<p>"And in the meantime," murmured Peter, his snaky eyes gleaming, "the girl could be weaned from him. Is that the plan, sir?"</p>
<p>The cold eyes of his employer rested on his sallow face.</p>
<p>"She weaned from him! That is a foolish thought. Do you think she
will ever give him up while there is a ghost of a chance? No. She must
be placed in a position which will render marriage with Walter impossible. Do you comprehend?"</p>
<p>"Not&#x2014;not quite," answered Peter, in a low, husky voice.</p>
<p>"I thought you were keener than that," sneered Silas. "Well, you know that my son would never wed a girl whose name has been dragged in the mire of infamy."</p>
<p>"I think I understand now," said Peter slowly. "Would&#x2014;would you expect me to compass that result?"</p>
<p>Silas Hardman glanced contemptuously over the gaunt, shambling form of the confidential clerk, and gave vent to a short, scornful laugh.</p>
<p>"You don't look much like a lady killer, Harriem; but I will leave the details to you. Will you undertake it?"</p>
<p>A flush passed over the ugly face of the younger
<pb n="49 Never Wed Such a Girl."/>
man. He did not relish being reminded of his lack of attractions.</p>
<p>"Let me see if I fully understand my task," he said, as if he had no feeling whatever. "I am to so situate the girl that she cannot marry your son. If I can succeed in wedding her to some one&#x2014;that will do."</p>
<p>"You have stated it exactly," said Mr. Hardman icily, and with as little compunction as if they had been discussing the life of a chicken.</p>
<p>Peter stood quite still, except for a gentle rubbing of his hands together.</p>
<p>Mr. Hardman looked at him for a moment, and then smiled.</p>
<p>"I see. You are wondering what your reward for all this is to be."</p>
<p>"It would seem more businesslike," replied Peter humbly, "if all the details were to be settled now."</p>
<p>"You do not care to trust to my generosity?" queried the other, looking keenly at Peter.</p>
<p>"Generosity," said Peter, with the utmost humility, "is a word that
means so many different things."</p>
<p>"For example?" queried the other.</p>
<p>"At this moment," answered Peter, with a furtive glance upward, "when you have need of me, it might mean a slight share in the business of Hardman & Son. After the affair was accomplished, it might mean no more than a raise of salary."</p>
<pb n="50 Never Wed Such a Girl."/>
<p>The face of Silas Hardman flushed with anger.</p>
<p>"Do you mean," he said, in a low, incisive tone, "that you have the audacity to dream of being a member of the firm?"</p>
<p>Peter had never shrunk together more humbly than at that moment. Yet his response was perfectly clear, though spoken in the most whining tones.</p>
<p>"All of us have our mad ambitions," he answered. "And I am sure nothing could be more honorable than the desire to be a member of such a firm as that of Hardman & Son."</p>
<p>"A mad ambition, truly," said Silas harshly. "Have you really dreamed of such a thing? And do you make it the price of doing this for me?"</p>
<p>"I know how presumptuous it must seem," whined Peter; "but it is an honorable ambition."</p>
<p>"Is it your price?" demanded Silas Hardman, his brows contracted.</p>
<p>"I would not put it quite that way," responded Peter, cringing and squirming as if with a sense of his own unworthiness.</p>
<p>"But is it your price?" persisted the old man.</p>
<p>"I don't see how I could undertake the risk except for some adequate recompense," said Peter.</p>
<p>"Very well," said Silas, in a decisive tone. "I will give you a tenth interest in the firm if you succeed in separating those two."</p>
<pb n="51 Never Wed Such a Girl."/>
<p>"Oh, thank you, sir! How can I believe my ears? A member of the great firm of Hardman & Son! Oh, I can hardly believe it!"</p>
<p>Silas Hardman eyed him curiously.</p>
<p>"You have evidently formed a plan which you believe will be successful," he said to him. "Is it so?"</p>
<p>"I think I can succeed," answered Peter, in the purring tone of a great lean cat.</p>
<p>"Under the circumstances I ought not to be troubled with any knowledge of the details," said the old man. "All you have to do is to earn your reward. All I have to do is to give it."</p>
<p>"That is all," answered Peter, twining his long, bony fingers in and out. "Of course, you will give me a little memorandum, saying that I am to be made a partner. It will be such a spur to my activity to have it where I can look at it now and then."</p>
<p>Silas bit his lip. Peter was one who thought of everything. The old man had made up his mind, however, and took up a sheet of paper and began to write. When he had finished, he handed it to Peter, who read:</p>
<p>"I, Silas Hardman, do hereby solemnly agree with Peter Harriem that on the day he brings me proof that he has rendered it impossible for my son to marry Viola Redmond, I will give him a one-tenth interest in the business of Hardman & Son.</p>
"SILAS HARDMAN."
<pb n="52 Never Wed Such a Girl."/>
<p>"That is all that is necessary," said Peter, "excepting a witness and an acknowledgment before a notary public."</p>
<p>"You shall have that, too," said Silas, with an icy smile. "Neither witness nor notary need know the contents of the paper. Anything else?"</p>
<p>"It will cost a great deal of money, probably," said Peter, his little eyes glittering greedily.</p>
<p>"I will put five thousand dollars to your credit in any bank you designate," said the old man. "Is that all?"</p>
<p>"That is all for myself. But I would have you stipulate with Mr. Walter that there is to be nothing binding between him and the girl while he is away."</p>
<p>"Wise precaution! I hope you will succeed."</p>
<p>"No man ever had more to work for," answered Peter, unable to subdue his exultation.</p>
</div>

<pb n="[53]"/>
<div id="gfprice007" type="chapter">
<head rend = "center">CHAPTER VII.</head>
<byline>"SHE IS A WORKING GIRL."</byline>
<p>Walter could not have told how he passed the part of the day that intervened between leaving his father at the store and seeing him again in the afternoon.</p>
<p>He had won his consent when he had not dared to hope for it, but had braced himself for a violent scene and a determined denial. And after that he had had a glimpse of the adorable face of his darling, and had exchanged with her an ecstatic look of hope and happiness.</p>
<p>He went about as if walking on air, devouring slow-moving time in all sorts of ways, and ending by going home and waiting there until his father came.</p>
<p>"You have determined on a test, father?" he cried, the moment he entered the library.</p>
<p>"Yes. I wonder if you will fight against it?"</p>
<p>"Tell it to me&#x2014;let me know what it is. It must needs be a very unreasonable one if I do not hail it with joy," answered Walter.</p>
<p>"Well, I have combined business with it," his father replied. "It occurred to me that in the time to come, when you had tasted every pleasure, you might like to know something of the business."</p>
<pb n="54 She is a Working Girl."/>
<p>"It is my dearest wish, father."</p>
<p>"Well, we need a man to represent us in London and Paris," said the old man.</p>
<p>Walter's face fell. He suspected in a moment what the test was to be.</p>
<p>"You are going to send me away?" he cried. "For a year."</p>
<p>"Oh, father!"</p>
<p>"Does it seem long, Walter? Yes, of course it does; but a year is only a year, and it is soon gone by. Go there and remain the year, and if, when you return, you find that you still love this girl, I will place no obstacle in your way. You shall do as you will."</p>
<p>"It is a severe test, father," Walter protested. "If it were only that we could meet occasionally. It is hard to be separated from the one we love."</p>
<p>"A year is soon gone, Walter. You said you would agree to the test."</p>
<p>"Let me consult with Viola," said Walter.</p>
<p>"Consult with her? And what if she says she cannot have it? Will you go back in your promise to me to accept a reasonable test?"</p>
<p>"She will not ask me to do that," Walter answered, realizing at once that an appeal to her would make no difference.</p>
<p>"Then why appeal to her?" his father said. "Why
<pb n="55 She is a Working Girl."/>
not settle this matter between ourselves? Will you go, Walter? It is not much to ask of you."</p>
<p>"Yes, I will go. She will understand. I will go, father; I can trust her, and she can trust me. I know that she would be the first to insist if I were to ask her. But what of her while I am gone?"</p>
<p>The wily old man hesitated, then spoke:</p>
<p>"That is all that troubles me. If she is what you say, she will be too high-minded to accept pecuniary help from you or me."</p>
<p>"That is true," Walter said; "and yet, to know that she is working so hard all that year. Don't you think, father, that your girls work altogether too hard?"</p>
<p>The old man bit his thin lip to keep back the sneer that rose to it.</p>
<p>"You shall regulate that when you are managing the business," said he. "In the meantime, as to Viola. The only way I can think of is to give her a better position and more pay. I can do that without letting her know that I am watching over her."</p>
<p>"How good and generous you always are when I am concerned!" Walter cried.</p>
<p>"On whom else should I lavish my affection, my boy? Then that is settled. By the way, it might be better not to let her have any idea that I am doing anything for her."</p>
<p>"I will not speak of it to her!" Walter said eagerly.</p>
<pb n="56 She is a Working Girl."/>
<p>"And another thing," said his father: "I think there should be no positive engagement. Just tell her that I am willing that you should marry if you continue to love when you return. There must be no engagement."</p>
<p>Walter smiled joyously.</p>
<p>"There is no need of an engagement, father. She and I know what our hearts mean. Oh, I am impatient to see her and tell her. I wish you would see her and talk to her. You would love her. I know you would."</p>
<p>"I saw her twice to-day," his father answered. "She is very beautiful. I hope she will be a good subject for promotion, for I shall not be comfortable until I have put it in her power to earn more money."</p>
<p>"You are so good," said Walter; and so the arrangement was made.</p>
<p>That night Walter was waiting near the corner of Sixth Avenue for her, and had her little hand clasped in his in an instant.</p>
<p>Neither saw the prowling, shambling shadow that glided after them on the other side of the way.</p>
<p>Peter Harriem had no reason for following and watching them now, but he could not help it. It seemed as if he followed them in order that he might suffer the pain and misery of witnessing their loving talk.</p>
<p>Mrs. Redmond was sitting propped up in a chair
<pb n="57 She is a Working Girl."/>
when the lovers entered the shabby but exceedingly neat little room where the invalid passed her days.</p>
<p>"Oh, mamma!" Viola murmured, "it is all right."</p>
<p>The invalid looked almost incredulously at the handsome face of Walter, as if she would detect there something that would give the lie to the happy words of her child.</p>
<p>"Your father has consented?" she asked wonderingly.</p>
<p>"He has consented in a way," answered Walter. "I am to go away for a year to attend to the business of the firm. There is to be no set engagement, but if we still love on my return, we are to be married."</p>
<p>"Yes, that is wise," said the widow slowly.</p>
<p>"No set engagement!" laughed Viola. "As if we needed any promise to hold us faithful to each other. But," she added, with a sigh, "a year is such a long time."</p>
<p>Peter Harriem, meanwhile, had turned away from a contemplation of the humble tenement into which Walter and Viola had entered.</p>
<p>He sped along the streets as if with a defined purpose in his mind, and more than one person stopped to look back at him, so weird and uncanny an object did he seem.</p>
<p>He stopped in front of a mansion on Madison Avenue.</p>
<pb n="58 She is a Working Girl."/>
<p>He looked up and hesitated for a moment, rubbing his hands, and seeming to shrink down between his high shoulders in self-abasement.</p>
<p>Then with a sidelong movement he ran up the steps of the stoop and rang the bell.</p>
<p>"Is Miss Carroll at home?" he asked of the servant who opened the door and stared at him in a most supercilious fashion.</p>
<p>"Who is it shall I say?" the man demanded insolently.</p>
<p>Peter looked up from under his eyebrows, and answered slowly:</p>
<p>"The confidential clerk of Hardman & Son."</p>
<p>The man, like all servants, knew enough of the private affairs of the household to flush with mortification, and to hasten to invite the unprepossessing clerk into the house, saying:</p>
<p>"Oh, oh! I beg pardon! Walk right in! I will tell Miss Carroll's maid. In there, please."</p>
<p>Peter crept into the reception room, with a sneer on his thin lips, muttering as he rubbed his hands over and over again:</p>
<p>"How they all cringe before wealth and power! Some day I shall be Hardman & Son, and then how I shall enjoy my power! Then I can make Viola as great a lady as this proud girl whose rustling silks I hear now."</p>
<pb n="59 She is a Working Girl."/>
<p>He cringed and bowed as she entered the room with a wondering look on her dark, haughty face.</p>
<p>"Did you come with any message?" she asked, when, after a stare of proud inquiry at him, she saw that he was not disposed to speak.</p>
<p>"No, I brought no message, if you please, Miss Carroll. I came on my own account to ask you to do me a service."</p>
<p>"I do not understand," she said coldly. "My maid told me that you were the head clerk, or something of the sort, of Hardman & Son. Is it not so?"</p>
<p>"Yes, Miss Carroll, it is so, and I have taken this very great liberty because&#x2014; I wonder if you will forgive me if I speak freely?"</p>
<p>"Speak freely," she said, inspired in spite of herself with uneasiness.</p>
<p>"You will not betray me to Mr. Walter, I hope," he said, glancing up furtively to see how she took the utterance of that name.</p>
<p>A quick, eager flush passed over her face, and then was hidden under a mask of indifference.</p>
<p>"Certainly not, if it is anything that does not concern him," she answered.</p>
<p>"But it does concern him," whined Peter, writhing humbly. "It concerns him and me and the girl I love&#x2014;such a beautiful girl, miss!"</p>
<p>The black eyes flamed and then looked cold.</p>
<pb n="60 She is a Working Girl."/>
<p>"I do not comprehend you," she said. "Go on!"</p>
<p>"I have your kind permission to go on?" he queried.</p>
<p>"You heard me," she responded.</p>
<p>"He says he is in love with her and wishes to marry her," Peter said abruptly, his snaky eyes never leaving the darkly beautiful face, and reading every tremor in it.</p>
<p>She started, and flames darted from her eyes. Her cheek paled.</p>
<p>Then her lips parted as she said coldly:</p>
<p>"Who is the girl?"</p>
<p>"She works in the store&#x2014;a salesgirl. Oh, so beautiful! Mr. Hardman has commissioned me&#x2014; May I proceed?"</p>
<p>She stamped her foot angrily.</p>
<p>"I tell you to go on. Why do you keep asking permission. You know you may continue with your story."</p>
<p>"I was not sure I would not offend," Peter said, in a cringing tone. "Thank you. Mr. Hardman wishes you to be his son's wife. Don't be offended with me! And he has commissioned me to break off the other&#x2014;the entanglement."</p>
<p>Eunice Carroll's face was crimson, but her self-control was wonderful, and she only said calmly:</p>
<p>Why did you come here?"</p>
<pb n="61 She is a Working Girl."/>
<p>"To see if you would help me," he answered, his beady eyes flashing.</p>
<p>"I! How?"</p>
<p>"Walter is going to Europe to remain a year. Suppose the girl should become infamous while he was away? Suppose she should marry another? If you would help me, I would answer for the rest."</p>
<p>"How could I help you?" she asked.</p>
<p>"She could be sent to you to show you some laces. If there were jewels on your table at the time, and if one should be missed, and afterward found in her pocket. Ah! An arrest, imprisonment, thrown out of work, unable to find it anywhere. A sick mother, too, starving, dying, maybe. Ah!"</p>
<p>He had crept up to her as he spoke until he was close by her side and was pouring his awful picture into her ear.</p>
<p>She shut her teeth hard together, and whispered huskily:</p>
<p>"Send her with laces!"</p>
</div>

<pb n="[62]"/>
<div id="gfprice008" type="chapter">
<head rend = "center">CHAPTER VIII.</head>
<byline>THE BEGINNING OF INFAMY.</byline>
<p>Walter had taken his departure, and Viola had been promoted to the lace department.</p>
<p>The parting had been a grief to her, and she had clung to Walter, sobbing and murmuring words of love and devotion.</p>
<p>"Be true to me and our love!" Walter had said.</p>
<p>"I will be true through everything," she had responded.</p>
<p>Viola had studied hard to learn her new duties, happy in the increase of pay that had come to her. It seemed to her that the future was very bright.</p>
<p>"Miss Redmond," said the head of the department to her the day before Christmas, "here is a package of laces to be taken at once to this address. The young lady is a good customer, and I wish her to be pleased."</p>
<p>Viola took the package, knowing that sometimes such things were done for their best customers.</p>
<p>She started and flushed as she looked at the address. She had heard of Eunice Carroll from Walter, and had read of her in the fashion notes in the daily papers.</p>
<p>Perhaps she was not sorry to see the woman from
<pb n="63 The Beginning of Infamy."/>
whom she had taken Walter. She was only human, and she wished to see with her own eyes if Eunice was as beautiful as the papers all said.</p>
<p>She took rather more time than usual in the cloak-room before she started. She was naturally not unwilling to appear at her best before Eunice Carroll.</p>
<p>It was impossible for her not to feel a little awe when she entered the magnificent mansion on Madison Avenue, and she was ushered into the boudoir of the fashionable beauty.</p>
<p>There was a bright glitter in the sloe-black eyes of Eunice Carroll when the salesgirl was brought to her. She stared at her with a cold, hard light growing in the black orbs.</p>
<p>Viola cast her eyes down in her embarrassment, and then stated her errand, beginning to untie the package as she spoke.</p>
<p>"You may go, Stella," Eunice said to her maid. "Never mind the jewels! I have no doubt the girl is honest."</p>
<p>Viola turned crimson. What an insolent way to speak of her!</p>
<p>No wonder Walter had not liked the proud creature, in spite of her imperious beauty.</p>
<p>In her humility, Viola did not hesitate to decide that Eunice was more beautiful than she; though more
<pb n="64 The Beginning of Infamy."/>
than once she had heard herself admiringly called the Belle of the Dry Goods District.</p>
<p>She could not help flashing an indignant glance at the jewels that were spread out on the dressing table. The maid noticed the glance as she went from the room.</p>
<p>Viola went on untying the package and presently had spread the laces out so that Eunice might see them.</p>
<p>Eunice, meanwhile, was staring at the girl, whose beauty had won Walter Hardman.</p>
<p>And as she looked, she was forced to admit that such brown eyes and dimpled cheeks must be very winsome to a man.</p>
<p>She studied vengefully the low, white brow, and the cherry, pouting lips; the curling chestnut hair, and the snowy throat.</p>
<p>Then she saw that the hands were white, dimpled, with tapering fingers, and that the form that was displayed by the snugly fitting coat was perfect in its proportions.</p>
<p>She wished to hear the voice that would issue from between the dewy lips and framed a question.</p>
<p>"Have you brought samples of all your best laces?"</p>
<p>"No, miss; I have only a selected few. The manager thought you would not like to see too many."</p>
<p>The voice was sweet and flutelike in its clear, musical
<pb n="65 The Beginning of Infamy."/>
tones; and Eunice Carroll hated the innocent girl with a deadly, unreasoning hatred.</p>
<p>"Place the box on the dressing table," she said, "and let me have the laces in my lap."</p>
<p>Viola, remembering what Eunice had said of the jewels, placed the box on the table without approaching nearer than she could help, and then stepped far away from it.</p>
<p>"Tell me the prices," said Eunice.</p>
<p>Viola stepped to her side and looked at the marks on the laces, reading them off as she did so.</p>
<p>Her hands were engaged, her eyes busy, she unconscious.</p>
<p>The hand of Eunice Carroll flashed in the light, and a triumphant glitter filled her black eyes.</p>
<p>"I will take these two," she said sharply, and thrust the others into Viola's hands.</p>
<p>Viola gathered up the laces, folded them neatly, and was taking the box from the table when Stella, the maid, entered, having been summoned by the ringing of the bell.</p>
<p>"Help her!" Eunice said sharply.</p>
<p>The maid assisted Viola as much as she could, and showed her out of the room.</p>
<p>"Put the jewels away, now," said Eunice. "I hope they are all right."</p>
<p>There was a prolonged silence. Stella was carefully
<pb n="66 The Beginning of Infamy."/>
putting the jewels away. Suddenly the silence was broken by a sharp cry.</p>
<p>"Oh, Miss Eunice, the diamond solitaire! I cannot find it! Oh, let me call the girl back."</p>
<p>"Search first. Be sure! I do not suspect you. We must not be foolish about it. Are you sure it was there? Is it not in its place?"</p>
<p>"It was here, Miss Eunice. Let me call her back. It is not in its place. I will call her back, Miss Eunice."</p>
<p>"It is too late for that now. I heard the door close. Send James for a detective. He will know what to do. Ah! that miserable thief!"</p>
<p>Stella flew from the room on her errand. Eunice Carroll fell back in her chair with white face and flaming eyes.</p>
<p>"It is the beginning of her infamy," she murmured.</p>
</div>

<pb n="[67]"/>
<div id="gfprice009" type="chapter">
<head rend = "center">CHAPTER IX.</head>
<byline>"WHO SAID MARRIAGE?"</byline>
<p>Viola's nature was sweet and gentle, but it was not weak. She would endure and suffer much from those she loved, but she was too high-spirited not to feel an insult keenly.</p>
<p>The manner of Eunice Carroll, more than her words, had stung Viola, and she left the house of the haughty beauty with her heart throbbing with indignation.</p>
<p>She had expected to find her cold and haughty, but had not dreamed that she would insult one whose only crime could be her poverty.</p>
<p>At least it never occurred to Viola that Eunice could have any reason for hating her and wishing her ill.</p>
<p>It was no longer difficult for her to comprehend why Walter had not loved the one chosen for him by his father.</p>
<p>"She is very beautiful," she murmured, "and if looks were all, Walter might easily care more for her than for me, but I hope I shall never have her proud, cold spirit."</p>
<p>It would have surprised Viola had she known that almost any man would have declared her more beautiful than Eunice.</p>
<pb n="68 Who Said Marriage?"/>
<p>She was modest, and her modesty did not in the least detract from her exquisite beauty in the eyes of the men who looked back at her that day as she walked briskly along the crowded streets.</p>
<p>Fortunately, she was not one to brood over a wrong, and it was not long before she had forgotten the purse-proud, insolent manner of the wealthy beauty, and was enjoying the crowds of Christmas buyers which crowded the streets.</p>
<p>Partly because the air was keen and sharp, and partly because she did not wish to waste the time of her employers, she stepped briskly along, threading her way with rosy cheeks and dancing eyes through the throngs.</p>
<p>She did not like it when rude men stared at her, but once she laughed outright when she heard a lady say to a friend:</p>
<p>"How strange it is that these shopgirls should be, so pretty!"</p>
<p>Viola laughed heartily, and wondered why it should be strange that a working girl should be pretty.</p>
<p>She was still laughing softly when she entered the store of Hardman & Son, and made her way through the crowds that filled it.</p>
<p>"Why are you laughing?" one of the girls asked her, as she took her place behind the lace counter after taking off her things in the cloakroom.</p>
<pb n="69 Who Said Marriage?"/>
<p>There was hardly time to tell why she was laughing, so she merely answered gayly:</p>
<p>"Oh, because I feel happy."</p>
<p>"I wonder at that," the other said fretfully; "I should think it would be enough to dampen any one's spirit to have a sick mother and such small pay to support her on."</p>
<p>The forewoman, who had listened, shrugged her shoulders and said in a rather spiteful way:</p>
<p>"You mustn't forget that Viola is a pet here. Hasn't she just been promoted when some of the others have been sent away?"</p>
<p>Viola was busy showing a customer some laces, but she heard the remark, and turned to say good-naturedly:</p>
<p>"I admit that I ought to be happy on that account. But that is not the only reason for my happiness."</p>
<p>"I guess Viola's got a lover," laughed one of the other girls softly.</p>
<p>Viola blushed and began to tell the customer the merits of the lace she was looking at.</p>
<p>The other girls noticed her confusion and laughed as they showed goods to customers.</p>
<p>The customers could not hear what they said to each other; but all the time they were selling they kept up a fire of mischievous talk.</p>
<p>"As sure as you live, Rose," said one of the girls
<pb n="70 Who Said Marriage?"/>
who had not yet spoken, "Viola has a lover. I knew she couldn't be long without one. My! look how red she is!"</p>
<p>The girls laughed again.</p>
<p>"It is to be worn at the neck," said Viola to her customer, trying to pretend that she had not heard, though her cheeks were the color of a peony.</p>
<p>"How well it suits her!" murmured Rose. "She ought to let him see her with it on. He would give it to her for a wedding present."</p>
<p>Viola had put the lace to her white throat to show the effect.</p>
<p>"He may be too poor to buy it," said the other teasingly.</p>
<p>"I know better than that," said Rose, as she measured off three yards of lace trimming. "Such a beauty as Viola would never marry a poor man."</p>
<p>"Who said anything about marrying," said the other.</p>
<p>"Why, Molly!" said Rose; "would you marry for money?"</p>
<p>"If I could," answered Molly, with a laugh; "wouldn't you, Viola?"</p>
<p>Viola had gained command of herself by this time and was able to turn around and answer laughingly:</p>
<p>"What should I know about it? I suppose I shall marry the man I love if he has the good sense to ask me."</p>
<pb n="71 Who Said Marriage?"/>
<p>"I've noticed," laughed Rose, "that Viola always goes home alone. I wonder why that is?"</p>
<p>"You mean," said Molly, "that she leaves the store alone. How do you know she goes home alone? I know she doesn't. Look at her face now!"</p>
<p>Viola had not suspected that anybody had ever seen her with Walter, and the thought of it brought the rich blood to her round cheeks.</p>
