<p>She could hear the girls giggling, and it seemed to her that there was a faint smile on the face of her customer.</p>
<p>"Is it anybody we know?" demanded Rose eagerly. "I should say so. Just see how she blushes!" replied Molly, bent on teasing Viola.</p>
<p>"Do tell us who it is," said Rose.</p>
<p>No one to have seen the girls would have supposed they had anything else in their heads than the selling of laces they were showing the ladies on the other side of the counter.</p>
<p>"What would you say&#x2014;" began Molly, when Viola turned quickly and said pleadingly:</p>
<p>"Please don't, Molly."</p>
<p>Molly started in surprise.</p>
<p>"You don't really mean anything," answered Viola in distress.</p>
<p>She could not bear to have it noised abroad in the store that Walter was her lover.</p>
<pb n="72 Who Said Marriage?"/>
<p>"Tell me who it is, Molly," said Rose, passing behind her to reach down a box of laces. "If you don't tell me I'll pinch you."</p>
<p>"Please, Molly," pleaded Viola, her cheeks burning.</p>
<p>"It is too good to keep," answered Molly, stifling a laugh. "Rose, it's&#x2014;</p>
<p>"I think it awfully mean of you, Molly" pleaded Viola.</p>
<p>"Quick, Molly!" said Rose, holding the box of laces in her hand.</p>
<p>"Mr. Harriem&#x2014;sneaking Peter," whispered Molly.</p>
<p>Viola gave vent to a sigh of relief, then laughed as she turned and caught the amazed look on Rose's face.</p>
<p>"Really?" demanded Rose, pausing to talk in violation of the rules of the store.</p>
<p>"No!" said Viola emphatically. "How ridiculous!"</p>
<p>"Young ladies!" said the forewoman angrily, as she came swiftly toward them, "do you wish me to report you? A nice way to deserve promotion!" she added, with a scornful glance at Viola.</p>
<p>She was, nearing thirty-five, and disliked the girls in proportion to their youth and beauty.</p>
<p>But Viola was too much relieved to discover that Molly had not guessed right, to care much what Miss Flynn said.</p>
<pb n="73 Who Said Marriage?"/>
<p>She turned again to the counter and began to wait on the crowds there.</p>
<p>A man with a keen, black eye and a drooping black mustache passed and stared furtively at the girls.</p>
<p>At Viola, when his eyes fell on her, he stared rather longer than at the others, murmuring as he moved on:</p>
<p>"That must be the one, though the mistress and the maid disagreed as to her good looks. A mighty pretty girl! I wonder why one pretty woman hates to acknowledge the beauty of another?"</p>
<p>He was moving on as he soliloquized, and did not stop until he reached the desk occupied by Peter Harriem.</p>
<p>He looked at Peter with the searching look that seemed habitual to him.</p>
<p>Peter returned his glance with a curiosity that seemed to be quickly satisfied, for he asked coldly:</p>
<p>"Did you wish to see me?"</p>
<p>"I wish to see Mr. Hardman, or whoever is the managing head of the firm."</p>
<p>"I am the confidential man," Peter replied. "What is your business?"</p>
<p>"Can I speak to you in private?"</p>
<p>"No one will overhear you here," Peter said.</p>
<p>The man glanced about him with a swift look that seemed to take in everything in sight, and said, in a low tone:</p>
<pb n="74 Who Said Marriage?"/>
<p>"I am a detective. There is a suspicion of theft against one of your employees, and I am here to investigate it. Will you help me?"</p>
<p>"I think you are mistaken," Peter said slowly. "If there had been any such charge made, I should have known it. We do not suspect anybody in the establishment."</p>
<p>"The charge is made by some one outside of the store&#x2014;a Miss Carroll, in fact."</p>
<p>"Not Miss Eunice Carroll?" cried Peter, with an assumption of deep surprise.</p>
<p>"Miss Eunice Carroll."</p>
<p>"Tell me the circumst said with an imperturbable calmness:</p>
<p>"I did not say she was guilty. She is suspected, and,
<pb n="75 Who Said Marriage?"/>
if you will assist me, I will endeavor to unravel the facts."</p>
<p>Peter flashed a swift, furtive glance at the detective before he answered:</p>
<p>"I will do anything I can. Hardman & Son would not for the world have any customer subjected to the least annoyance. Shall I send for Miss Redmond?"</p>
<p>"This will not be a good place to. question her, particularly if she turns out innocent," the detective said, his keen eyes fixed on Peter with a puzzled expression, which passed away as if by magic the instant Peter raised his eyes.</p>
<p>"Let us go to the cloakroom, then," Peter said; "no one will interrupt us there."</p>
<p>"In the cloakroom, then," the detective said.</p>
<p>"That is the room, there," Peter said. "If you will go to it I will bring Miss Redmond to you, though I am satisfied that the charge will be found unwarranted by the facts."</p>
<p>"Precious sly fox," murmured the detective, as he went toward the cloakroom. "If he had been accused I would have arrested him on his looks. Meanwhile, I'm sorry for the pretty girl."</p>
</div>

<pb n="[76]"/>
<div id="gfprice010" type="chapter">
<head rend = "center">CHAPTER X.</head>
<byline>THE BURNING EYES.</byline>
<p>"Miss Redmond, I would like to see you alone for a few minutes."</p>
<p>The oily voice of Peter falling on her ear was the first intimation to Viola that he was near her.</p>
<p>She started, flashed a swift look of roguish chagrin at her two companions who had been teasing her, and stepped from behind the counter after him.</p>
<p>She had no idea that there was anything more in his invitation than a desire on his part to make a plea of business the excuse for talking with her.</p>
<p>Her promotion had acted as a sort of assurance to her that she was under the protection of Mr. Hardman; and she was not afraid of Peter.</p>
<p>She followed him in silence to the cloakroom, rather wondering why he led her thither, and staring in unfeigned surprise when she saw the man who was waiting there.</p>
<p>"This is Miss Redmond," Peter said to the detective. "Miss Redmond, this gentleman wishes to ask you a few questions. I hope you will answer them with perfect frankness."</p>
<p>Viola stared in blank amazement as she noted the solemn, almost pitying face of the detective.</p>
<pb n="77 The Burning Eyes."/>
<p>"Are you the person who took some laces from this store to the house of Miss Eunice Carroll?" the detective asked.</p>
<p>"Yes, sir," Viola answered wonderingly.</p>
<p>The eyes of the detective were fixed on her as if they would search out the truth in the very depths of her soul.</p>
<p>He found it extremely difficult to believe in advance of complete evidence that she was guilty of theft. And yet in his experience there had been cases too strange to enable him to say from her appearance that she was guiltless.</p>
<p>"Do you remember seeing some jewels lying on her dressing table?" he asked.</p>
<p>A startled look leaped into her brown eyes. She remembered the words Eunice had uttered to her, and the thrill of impending danger shook her.</p>
<p>"I&#x2014;I do," she answered, her little hands coming together, and clinging.</p>
<p>"There was a diamond ring lost from the table, and its loss was discovered just after your departure," he said, with slow emphasis, as if he would have every word sink into her brain.</p>
<p>At these words, which were so little short of accusation, the high spirit which was dormant in her soul was aroused; the queenly little head was thrown back; the brown eyes flamed with indignation.</p>
<pb n="78 The Burning Eyes."/>
<p>"And does she dare to say that I took her ring?" she cried. "As if I would touch it! How dare she say such a thing?"</p>
<p>"That is just what I said, Viola," Peter said, with an air of anxious solicitude. "Of course such a charge must be proven."</p>
<p>The detective turned from the flushed face of Viola to look at Peter.</p>
<p>"There has been no charge," he said dryly. "The jewels were said to be there, and one of them is said to have been lost. This young lady is not accused. But, like the maid, who was also in the room, she is properly under suspicion, and must remain so until she has permitted such steps to be taken as will prove her innocence. Even if I feel obliged to arrest her, it will prove nothing against her. Innocent persons have been arrested."</p>
<p>"Arrest me!" gasped Viola, quivering with terror at the mere word.</p>
<p>"You shall not be arrested if I can save you, Viola," murmured the oily voice of Peter.</p>
<p>"I only spoke of that as an extreme measure," the detective said. "I suppose you will make no objections to a search."</p>
<p>Viola flushed and drew herself up.</p>
<p>"Is that necessary?" she asked. "I assure you that
<pb n="79 The Burning Eyes."/>
I did not go near enough to the table to touch a thing on it."</p>
<p>"I cannot insist upon a search," he said; "but I have a warrant for your arrest, and when you are in custody you will be forced to submit. If you are innocent it will be better to acquiesce now."</p>
<p>Viola tottered.</p>
<p>A warrant for her arrest! It seemed to her as if the disgrace was hers already.</p>
<p>"I will call Miss Flynn," Peter said, and was about to go to the door when the detective stopped him by a quiet yet firm touch on the arm.</p>
<p>"One moment; I will tell you when I need your assistance. Miss Redmond, will you point out to me the garment among these which is yours?"</p>
<p>Viola went with uneven steps to where her sack hung on its hook, and took it down.</p>
<p>She handed. it to the detective, who passed it at once to Peter, saying as he did so:</p>
<p>"Please examine the pockets."</p>
<p>"May I?" Peter said, looking at Viola as if to have her comprehend that he was her firm friend.</p>
<p>She could only nod her head, afraid to open her lips lest they should betray her and let out the sob of fear which she was trying to suppress.</p>
<p>Peter thrust his bony fingers into the pockets, feeling a thrill of delight at the thought that the little
<pb n="80 The Burning Eyes."/>
hands he would have given so much to hold in his were in the habit of snuggling in these same pockets.</p>
<p>His face wore an expression of sorrow and distress, as if the task were not at all to his liking, but suddenly changed.</p>
<p>He stared from her to the detective.</p>
<p>"Well?" demanded the latter sharply, as with a stride he had the coat in his grasp. "What have you found? Let me see it."</p>
<p>Reluctantly, and as if he was in the greatest pain in doing so, Peter drew from the pocket a ring set with one large brilliant.</p>
<p>"Oh, Viola!" he cried.</p>
<p>"Ah!" said the detective, snatching the ring from the bony fingers of the other and turning to Viola, "how came this in your pocket? Is it yours?"</p>
<p>Viola stared with wide-open, incredulous eyes; her face became ghastly pale, and a terrible fear smote her heart.</p>
<p>"Had some one played a trick on her?" was the question that formed in her brain.</p>
<p>"It&#x2014;it is not mine," she stammered.</p>
<p>"How came it in your pocket?" demanded the detective.</p>
<p>"I&#x2014;I do not know. Some one must have placed it there," she said. "I know nothing about it. It is some conspiracy against me."</p>
<pb n="81 The Burning Eyes."/>
<p>The detective shrugged his shoulders. He had heard the same plea so many times.</p>
<p>"It may be some accident," Peter suggested, rubbing his hands together in a way to make the detective look at him in disgust.</p>
<p>"It may be," the detective responded, and cast a look on Viola that made her cry out:</p>
<p>"Oh, sir, do not take me to prison! I am innocent; indeed I am innocent. I knew nothing of the presence of the ring there. I would not have touched it for all the world!"</p>
<p>"Must you take her to prison, sir?" Peter asked.</p>
<p>"I must. It is hard to believe her guilty; but I have no choice. Come, Miss Redmond, you will be wise to put on your coat and come with me, without disturbance. It will be better for you in any case."</p>
<p>Viola became so ghastly pale that both the men sprang toward her, but she waved them aside and sank into a chair, murmuring:</p>
<p>"I shall be better in a moment. I am innocent, but I will go with you. Oh, if Walter were only here!"</p>
<p>Peter bit his lip and turned yellow with anger.</p>
<p>"Will you let me have a few words alone with her?" he asked of the detective. "I have good reason for asking. She cannot escape. I will not keep you long."</p>
<p>The detective hesitated, and then seemed to make up his mind quickly.</p>
<pb n="82 The Burning Eyes."/>
<p>"Yes; but do not keep me long."</p>
<p>He retired from the room, and Peter glided to the side of the overwhelmed and prostrated girl.</p>
<p>"Viola," he murmured in her ear, "I can save you yet, if you will but listen to me."</p>
<p>She looked up into his face with burning eyes, seeming to search his soul through the mask that hid it. Then she shuddered and turned away with a pitiful moan of anguish.</p>
<p>"I know you are innocent," he said, "but no one else will believe it. The evidence is such that all will think you guilty, and your whole life will be ruined. You will be taken to prison, and after the trial you will be sent to the penitentiary."</p>
<p>"Why do you torture me?" she wailed, turning from him, and making as if to rise and flee to the door.</p>
<p>"I do not say it to torture you," he answered eagerly, in his wheedling voice, "but in order that you may understand. There is one way of escape if you will grasp it."</p>
<p>"Escape!" she said.</p>
<p>"I do not mean to run away," he answered. "You cannot escape that way. The police would soon hunt you down. I mean that you can keep out of prison, and escape all the infamy of this charge."</p>
<p>She looked at him with eyes that should have melted his heart.</p>
<pb n="83 The Burning Eyes."/>
<p>"Point out the way," she said miserably.</p>
<p>"I love you madly, Viola. Be my wife and I will save you. Give me your promise and you shall never go to prison.</p>
<p>"I cannot be your wife," she wailed. "I love another. Ah, Heaven, why is he not here to protect me?"</p>
<p>"You love Walter Hardman," Peter said huskily. "I know that, and I
know the conditions of his going away. But do you think his father
would let you marry Walter with such a charge as this against you? Do
you think that even Walter would wed you when you have been sentenced
to prison?"</p>
<p>"Oh, do not torture me. Let me go to prison and die, for I know I shall die of shame and agony when I am in that awful place."</p>
<p>"And your mother," he went on, "do you think she will find anybody to care for her while you are in prison&#x2014;do you think any one will wish to help the mother of one who is a convicted thief?"</p>
<p>"Why do you tempt me?" she cried fiercely. "I do not love you and never can. You know it. Let me go to prison."</p>
<p>"Think of your future!" he said angrily. "Do you know what it will be? Do you know the fate of the outcast? Do you know that you will never again be
<pb n="84 The Burning Eyes."/>
able to secure work in this great city? You will have to starve. Will you not accept my offer and by my wife?"</p>
<p>"I cannot," she answered. "Oh, sir, why can you not be generous? Why, if you can save me, do you let me go to prison? Why do you exact a price so terrible? Save me and I will give you my gratitude."</p>
<p>"If you will be my wife I will save you, not else. It will cost me a great deal of money."</p>
<p>"Walter will repay you every penny and more, Viola cried, grasping him by the arm in her eager entreaty.</p>
<p>"I know nothing of Walter Hardman," he said, with an angry hiss. "Be my wife and I will save you. Refuse, and I will call in the detective."</p>
<p>"I must refuse," she wailed. "What can I do but refuse?"</p>
<p>He glided to the door and placed his hand on the knob, a malignant expression on his hideous face.</p>
<p>"Once more I give you the chance," he said. "Will you save yourself by becoming my wife?"</p>
<p>"I cannot. Oh, mother, what will become of you?"</p>
<p>He opened the door and beckoned the man in.</p>
<p>"Take her with you," he said.</p>
<p>The detective fixed his eyes on Peter Harriem for but a second, then turned to Viola and said kindly:</p>
<p>"Come, my girl."</p>
</div>

<pb n="[85]"/>
<div id="gfprice011" type="chapter">
<head rend = "center">CHAPTER XI.</head>
<byline>A DETECTIVE BY HER SIDE.</byline>
<p>Like one bereft of all power of will, Viola donned her coat and hat, and prepared to follow the detective.</p>
<p>"Pull your veil down over your face," he said curtly, but not unkindly. "And you may go out ahead of me. Do not try to escape. I will join you on the sidewalk."</p>
<p>She did exactly as he bade her, and was moving away, when once more she heard the voice of Peter whisper in her ear:</p>
<p>"There is yet a chance. Say that you will be my wife."</p>
<p>"I cannot," she wailed.</p>
<p>"Please leave her alone," the detective said peremptorily.</p>
<p>Peter scowled and let Viola pass him.</p>
<p>She went into the crowded store with her head down and her feet dragging, feeling as if every one there knew that she was going to prison on the charge of stealing.</p>
<p>Ah! it is not guilt alone that brings horror. The mere touch of the finger of the law seems to cover a sensitive soul with shame.</p>
<pb n="86 A Detective by Her Side."/>
<p>She stepped into the street like one in a dream. The detective took his place by her side and said, in a low tone:</p>
<p>"Walk ahead and turn the first corner. No one need know that you are under arrest. No one knows me. I will join you when there is no one to see."</p>
<p>She did as he bade her. It seemed to her that if he had told her to walk to her destruction she would have done it in the same mechanical way.</p>
<p>When he joined her he was in the middle of the block, going toward Seventh Avenue. He looked pityingly down at her. She did not speak. The horror of her situation robbed her of speech.</p>
<p>"Will you tell me all about this matter?" he said to her.</p>
<p>"There is nothing to tell you," she said, in a low tone of utter misery.</p>
<p>"I am sure there must be," he answered. "If you are guilty, there is that to tell. If you are innocent, there must be something to tell. You spoke of a conspiracy. Who should conspire against you?"</p>
<p>She looked up at the immobile, sphinxlike face and shook her head.</p>
<p>"I suppose I was mad," she said. "I do not know who should conspire against me."</p>
<p>The very hopelessness betrayed in her sweet voice impressed and touched him.</p>
<pb n="87 A Detective by Her Side."/>
<p>"Will you tell me what that fellow in the store said to you when you were alone?" he asked.</p>
<p>"He said he would save me if I would marry him."</p>
<p>"And you refused?"</p>
<p>"Yes."</p>
<p>"Why?"</p>
<p>"Because I love another."</p>
<p>The detective gnawed his mustache, and looked down at the bowed head.</p>
<p>"Did that man back there say how he would save you?" he asked suddenly.</p>
<p>"No, sir."</p>
<p>"Who is the man you love? Has this man anything to do with your love story in any way?"</p>
<p>"No," answered poor Viola, too miserable to give much heed to her answers.</p>
<p>"Tell me the name of the man you love. You know I can find it out by asking that man back there."</p>
<p>That was true enough. There was no reason for a concealment. But, oh, would it get into the papers? How Mr. Hardman would hate her then! And what would Walter do? Maybe he would not hear of it.</p>
<p>"His name must not get into the papers," she pleaded.</p>
<p>"His name shall not get in through me," he said, in a tone that made her have faith in him. "What is his name?"</p>
<pb n="88 A Detective by Her Side."/>
<p>"Walter Hardman," she answered.</p>
<p>He looked at her curiously.</p>
<p>"The son of Silas Hardman?"</p>
<p>"Yes, sir."</p>
<p>"And does the old man know?" he asked quickly.</p>
<p>"Yes, sir. But Walter has gone away to remain for a year. We are not to be married unless we love at the end of a year."</p>
<p>The detective pursed up his mouth, as if about to whistle.</p>
<p>"Mr. Walter is away, eh?"</p>
<p>"Yes, sir; in Europe. Oh, he must not know of this."</p>
<p>"And you say his father approved of your intended marriage?" he queried, without paying any attention to her last remark.</p>
<p>"He didn't approve, perhaps, but he was willing, if we continued to love. I know he would have preferred Walter to marry Miss Carroll!"</p>
<p>"Miss Carroll!" exclaimed the detective, with a startled look down at the bowed head.</p>
<p>"Yes; she who accuses me of stealing her ring."</p>
<p>"Did she know you were to marry Walter Hardman?" he asked.</p>
<p>"No, I don't suppose she did," Viola replied.</p>
<p>"Have you ever been sent out with laces before?" he asked.</p>
<pb n="89 A Detective by Her Side."/>
<p>"No, sir. Other girls have been. I only went to the lace department a few days ago. It was a promotion for me."</p>
<p>"Promoted, eh?" he said, in a musing way, and then went on in silence.</p>
<p>"Viola did not note his silence, for she had only answered mechanically, and because she had felt that she might as well, since concealment was impossible.</p>
<p>The man walked by her side, telling her when to turn, and guarding her against being run over when they crossed the streets.</p>
<p>She did not see where he was leading her, and she did not care. Her thoughts wandered from Walter to her mother, and back again. They were her whole world.</p>
<p>Suddenly the detective recalled her to herself by saying sharply:</p>
<p>"Have you any money in your pocket?"</p>
<p>"A little. Three dollars."</p>
<p>"Here are twenty. I cannot afford to give you any more. I want you to go out of the city. Go into the country and hide. I think you are innocent, and there is no need for you to have your name in every paper in the city. You must escape from me. Do you understand?"</p>
<p>Viola stared at him.</p>
<p>"My mother!" she stammered.</p>
<pb n="90 A Detective by Her Side."/>
<p>"I will do what I can for her. But you go. Take the cars at the Grand Central, and go somewhere in the country. Or to some other city. Then write to your mother. I think you are innocent, and I intend to clear you. Go quickly now."</p>
<p>She seemed dazed for a moment before she could grasp the idea that he really was giving her her freedom.</p>
<p>Then she seized his hand and kissed it.</p>
<p>"I am innocent," she sobbed, "and Heaven will bless you for your goodness. Oh, thank you! thank you!"</p>
</div>

<pb n="[91]"/>
<div id="gfprice012" type="chapter">
<head rend = "center">CHAPTER XII.</head>
<byline>SNATCHING A CHILD.</byline>
<p>Peter Harriem looked after the detective as he followed Viola out of the store, and then glided swiftly to where his hat and coat hung, and hurriedly threw them on, muttering:</p>
<p>"I don't like the way that fellow looked at me and at her. I half believe he has fallen in love with her. That wondrous beauty of hers is enough to turn any fellow's head."</p>
<p>He came face to face with Mr. Hardman as he was putting his coat on.</p>
<p>"I was just looking for you," the head of the firm said. "I would like***double dash***</p>	
<p>"Do not stop me now," whispered Peter eagerly. "I have just set in motion the little plot of ours. I must go at once. I do not know when I shall return. Everything is so that it will go on without me here."</p>
<p>"Go, then. I will see that everything runs as it should."</p>
<p>Peter glided from the store, and the old man, with a cynical curl of his thin lips, murmured:</p>
<p>"A useful fellow when well controlled."</p>
<p>Peter, meanwhile, was making his way swiftly through the crowded store, and down the street.</p>
<pb n="92 Snatching a Child"/>
<p>The detective and Viola were not in sight, but Peter was not long in making up his mind what to do.</p>
<p>He ran to the nearest corner and looked up and down the street.</p>
<p>They were not there. The next thing was to run back to the other corner, and this he did.</p>
<p>He saw the pair walking down the block, and crossed over to the other side in order that he might the better follow them.</p>
<p>His experience in following Viola on other occasions was of use to him now, having given. him a good idea of the manner in which it should be done to be successful.</p>
<p>Moreover, nothing could be more in keeping with his stealthy nature than this sly spying on another.</p>
<p>A trained detective "shadow" could not have done better than he did.</p>
<p>He followed them as he went, and noted how they talked together, forming his own conclusions of what their conversation must be about.</p>
<p>Then he saw the detective give her something, though he could not tell what it was until he saw her turn away, and leave the man who should have taken her to prison.</p>
<p>"He gave her money, so that she could get out of the city," said Peter, in a tone of conviction. "It was as I
<pb n="93 Snatching a Child"/>
thought, TIe has fallen in love with her pretty face, and is assisting her to escape."</p>
<p>A fiendish smile crossed his lean face, as he saw the two separate.</p>
<p>"He little knows," he muttered, "that he is helping me more than he is her. Now she is a fugitive from justice, and she will fall into my power so much the more easily."</p>
<p>The detective followed Viola to the corner and saw her speeding uptown. He shook his head pityingly, murmuring:</p>
<p>"Poor girl! She is the victim of a conspiracy which I will ferret out. It seems quite clear, but it will not be easy to prove my belief."</p>
<p>He turned down the avenue, while Viola was hastening up it. Peter, with stealthy movement, yet making himself as inconspicuous as possible, was gliding after Viola.</p>
<p>Poor Viola! her heart was throbbing with the fear of one who has felt the clutch of the law on hRr arm, and who does not know when again it will be there.</p>
<p>Her one instinct was to get out of the city as quickly as possible; she dared not even go to her mother to explain to her what had happened, and what she intended to do.</p>
<p>So she sped up the avenue until she came to Forty-
<pb n="94 Snatching a Child"/>
second Street, where she turned toward the East Side, and kept on until she reached the depot.</p>
<p>There was the customary turmoil of moving crowds of passengers, some going away, and others returning to the city. The street was alive with electric cars and automobiles, and trucks, and wagons.</p>
<p>Viola saw nothing of it all. She was bent on getting into a train which would carry her away from the awful peril of prison.</p>
<p>She turned toward the depot of the Hudson River division, because it seemed the nearest, and was about to enter the first doorway, when she heard a scream of terror.</p>
<p>The cry came from the lips of a girl dressed as a nurse, and as Viola followed the direction in which she was looking, she saw at once the cause of the scream.</p>
<p>A pretty little boy, with golden curls hanging down his back, and showing by his costly dress that he was the child of wealthy parents, stood in the middle of the street, apparently paralyzed with terror.</p>
<p>A carriage drawn by two frightened horses was dashing down the street from the direction of Forty-third Street.</p>
<p>The child would be run over and killed if he were not snatched away from where he stood, and everybody knew it, but somehow each was watching for the other to do it.</p>
<pb n="95 Snatching a Child"/>
<p>"My boy! oh, my boy!" fell on the ears of Viola.</p>
<p>She turned, and in the flash of an eye saw a noble-looking man of middle age prostrate on the top step of the low stoop leading to the waiting room. A crutch by his side told her the story of what had happened.</p>
<p>He had forgotten a wounded foot, and had leaped to the rescue of his threatened child, and had fallen.</p>
<p>All this occupied but a second of time. It seemed as if everything here described had happened at the same moment.</p>
<p>The excitement which filled the atmosphere about her aroused Viola from the stupor .of misery into which she had fallen, and while her mind was mechanically taking in all that her eyes had seen, she had prepared herself to act.</p>
<p>The infuriated horses were almost upon the little boy, whose blue eyes were fixed on them with an awful horror in them, when Viola leaped from where she stood, and snatched the child in her arms from under the very hoofs of the plunging animals.</p>
<p>Then something struck her, and she was whirled around and tossed toward the sidewalk, her senses reeling.</p>
<p>She was only conscious of clinging to the child and of giving him up to a white-aired old lady, who snatched him to her bosom with a wild cry of joy.</p>
<p>Then she swooned, and knew no more until she
<pb n="96 Snatching a Child"/>
opened her eyes and became conscious of several things all at once.</p>
<p>She was on a railroad train, which was moving, and the face of an old lady, which seemed dimly familiar, was looking down on her.</p>
<p>She gazed into the face for a moment with a blank expression in her brown eyes, then came a sudden realization of what had happened to her.</p>
<p>The saving of the child was nothing, but her own misery was paramount.</p>
<p>He was safe, while she was a prisoner fleeing from the grasp of the law.</p>
<p>Where am I?" she cried, trying to rise.</p>
<p>"You are in good hands, my dear," was the soothing answer, as the old lady pressed her gently back. "You were injured a little on the arm, and the only thing to do seemed to bring you right into the car. We could not leave you to be cared for by strangers when you had saved our darling from a terrible death."</p>
<p>"We are leaving the city," murmured Viola, comprehending that that was exactly what she most wished.</p>
<p>"Yes, we had a stateroom in the car, so, you see, you are quite private. And we found a surgeon who at-tended to you at once, and said the injury was so trifling that you would not notice it in a few days."</p>
<p>Viola turned and looked inquiringly at her shoulder,
<pb n="97 Snatching a Child"/>
and saw that her sleeve had been cut away to permit an examination.</p>
<p>"I was with you all the time, my dear," said the lady. "And we are alone, now. My son and little Rupert are outside in the car."</p>
<p>"I am sure I can get up now," Viola said, in a low tone.</p>
<p>She felt that she must get by herself in order that she might decide what to do.</p>
<p>"You must not. Why should you? Are you worried about what your friends at home will think of your absence?"</p>
<p>Viola's eyes answered yes.</p>
<p>"I had thought of that," the old lady said, with a pleasant smile. "You may write a telegram here, and it can be sent from the first station. That will reassure your friends, and you may then go home with us and remain there until you are quite well. Won't that do?"</p>
<p>She was going away from the city, where her danger lay, and there was no reason why she should not accept the hospitality of the people for whom she had endangered her life.</p>
<p>"Thank you," she answered, "that will do. I do not like to be a trouble to you, but I suppose I cannot help it now."</p>
<p>The old lady smiled sweetly.</p>
<pb n="98 Snatching a Child"/>
<p>"That is the way to look at it. And if you only know how grateful we are to you, you would not have any feeling of discomfort in accepting every attention we can offer. Perhaps I had better write the telegram, at your dictation. I will get a blank."</p>
<p>She left the room to get the telegraph blank, and Viola had a change to consider her situation.</p>
<p>"I must not betray who I am," she said. "It is certain that my name will get into the papers, in spite of what that good detective can do. If I have enemies, they will do all they can to ruin me. I must telegraph to mamma, but I must not put my name to the message. I will sign it Viola. That will tell her everything."</p>
<p>"Now," said the old lady, returning, "what shall I say?"</p>
<p>Viola studied out the words for a moment, and the telegram was written at her dictation.</p>
<p></p>
<p>"Do not worry about me. I am quite safe, and will write to-night. Viola."</p>
<p></p>
<p>"Viola! What a pretty name," the old lady said, soothing back the brown curls that clustered around the low, white brow. "But do you not wish to say where you are going?"</p>
<p>"And now to whom is this to be sent?" the lady asked.</p>
<pb n="99 Snatching a Child"/>
<p>Viola started in dismay. She must tell her mother's name, after all, or the message would not reach her.</p>
<p>It seemed to her that it was useless to hope to escape. Somehow or other it would be sure to leak out that she was accused of theft, and there would be no asylum for her anywhere.</p>
<p>The kind old lady sat there waiting patiently, yet wonderingly. She could not comprehend the manifest agitation of the beautiful girl.</p>
<p>Viola's thoughs sped quickly. Something must be done. Her mother must know, or she would become ill worrying. Why not take the chances?</p>
<p>"Mrs. Kate Redmond," she said, in a low tone, and added the address.</p>
</div>

<pb n="[100]"/>
<div id="gfprice013" type="chapter">
<head rend = "center">CHAPTER XIII.</head>
<byline>IN FRONT OF A MIRROR.</byline>
<p>The old lady went out to give the telegram to the conductor, so that he might send it from the first station.</p>
<p>"She knew by the address that we live in a tenement house," Viola murmured to herself. "I could see that by the look of her face. I wonder if she will be just as kind to me now? Well, it does not matter. When I get to the end of the journey, I shall be able to go on if she does not seem to want me."</p>
<p>But the expression on the kind face was the same when the old lady returned, and Viola knew that she had been unjust.</p>
<p>"Then your name is Viola Redmond?" the lady said. "I suppose now you would like to know what my name is?"</p>
<p>"Yes, please," answered Viola.</p>
<p>"My name is Beekman; my son is Howard Beekman, and the little boy whose life you saved is my grandson, Rupert Beekman. My son is temporarily lame from an accident, and we are going up to the old family place to spend Christmas, and be quiet. You will like Clover Hill."</p>
<pb n="101 In Front of a Mirror."/>
<p>"I shall not trouble you for long," Viola said.</p>
<p>"You shall not go until your shoulder is quite well," Mrs. Beekman said. "But we are not going to think of that. I am not going to let you go until I must. Why, I shall be lonely up there unless I have some one with me. I was going to send down to the city for one of my young friends to come up to me. I will promise you plenty of sleighing and skating, it you care for such things."</p>
<p>Viola thought the lady must still be under a misapprehension concerning her. She said in a quiet, sad tone:</p>
<p>"I am only a working girl, Mrs. Beekman. I thought you knew that from the address I gave you."</p>
<p>Mrs. Beekman smiled sweetly, and stroked the round cheek.</p>
<p>"Of course I guessed it, my dear. Did it make any difference to you that Rupert was rich when you saved him?"</p>
<p>"No; I only saw that he was in danger," Viola answered.</p>
<p>"And do you think it makes any difference to us that you are poor? The only difference it makes is that it gives me a chance I would not have had if you had been rich."</p>
<p>Viola looked at her, but did not say anything.</p>
<pb n="102 In Front of a Mirror."/>
<p>"If you have to work for a living," said the old lady, "does it make any difference to you what you do?"</p>
<p>"Not much," Viola answered, her brown eyes fixed on the face she was learning to like.</p>
<p>"Then you must remain with me as my companion. I have been looking for some one to take the place for a long time, and I think you have been sent to me. Will you stay with me?"</p>
<p>"Oh, I shall be so glad!" Viola cried eagerly. "I will stay as long as you will keep me, and I will try so hard to please you. Only perhaps I shall not know what to do."</p>
<p>Mrs. Beekman laughed and patted her cheek.</p>
<p>"I am not afraid of that," she said. "The duties are not difficult to master. You are sure your mother will not mind?"</p>
<p>Viola thought of the sweet-faced invalid all alone in the tenement house, and the tears started to her eyes. What a cruel fate it was that divided them!</p>
<p>"She will do the best she can," she answered, and then burst out with a choking sob: "Oh, what shall I do?"</p>
<p>"Something is troubling you," said Mrs. Beekman kindly. "Is it anything I can help you about?"</p>
<p>Viola shook her head. She did not dare tell even so kind a person as this her true story.</p>
<p>She knew that the evidence was all against her. and<sic corr="And"/>
<pb n="103 In Front of a Mirror."/>
she felt that no one who knew what had happened would trust her.</p>
<p>"Well, never mind," said Mrs. Beekman kindly; "you have placed us under a great obligation, and you may be sure we shall always be glad to help you as best we can."</p>
<p>Nothing more was said on the subject, and after a little while Viola was permitted to sit up. And after her coat had been put on, Mr. Beekman and little Rupert were asked to come into the stateroom.</p>
<p>The nurse was not there, and Viola learned that she had been sent home, since it was entirely owing to her carelessness that the accident had happened.</p>
<p>Mr. Beekman was visibly interested in the beautiful girl who had saved his child, and did all he could to aid his mother in putting her at her ease.</p>
<p>But, probably, nothing he or his mother could have done would have put her as much at her ease as little Rupert's winsome prattling did.</p>
<p>He seemed to take to Viola at once, and snuggled up to her in the way so sweet in children, and presently she found herself talking to him and telling him stories, as if the older people had not been there.</p>
<p>Mrs. Beekman smiled at her son, and exchanged meaning glances with him.</p>
<p>It was plain that neither of them was sorry that Viola was going home with them.</p>
<pb n="104 In Front of a Mirror."/>
<p>And when at last the station where they were to get off was reached, it seemed to Viola almost as if she were going to a place that might be a shelter and a home during the terrible period of suspense before the kind detective could clear her name from the stain upon it.</p>
<p>A motor car was waiting at the station, and it was not long before they were being whirled up the gentle slope that led from the station to the hills beyond.</p>
<p>The sky was overcast with dull, leaden clouds, and there was that peculiar feeling in the air that betokened a coming snowstorm.</p>
<p>And indeed they had not yet reached the mansion amid the trees when the great flakes began to come floating slowly down.</p>
<p>Rupert cried out with glee at the prospect of sleighing, and began to chatter to Viola about the fun they would have together.</p>
<p>And when the house was reached and the hall door thrown open to admit them, they were greeted by the sight of a great fire of blazing logs in the capacious chimney of the wide hall.</p>
<p>It was like a new world to Viola, and she felt that if she could only rid herself of the awful fear of discovery she could be very happy there.</p>
<p>Instinctively she began to make herself useful by removing Rupert's outer garments, but was stopped by
<pb n="105 In Front of a Mirror."/>
Mrs. Beekman, who told her that Rupert would be taken care of by his old friend, Mrs. Daly, the housekeeper.</p>
<p>So Viola surrendered the child to the comfortable-looking woman, and followed Mrs. Beekman up to her room.</p>
<p>"Do you feel well enough to go down to dinner with us?" the old lady inquired, when they stood alone in the cheery apartment.</p>
<p>"I feel quite well, excepting for a sore feeling in the shoulder," Viola replied. "Bait I don't see how I can go down in the dress I have on."</p>
<p>She blushed as she spoke, thinking of the cut sleeve, which exposed her snowy shoulder. Mrs. Beekman smiled pleasantly.</p>
<p>"That can be fixed easily enough," she said. "In fact, I shall be very much surprised if Mrs. Daly has not already provided for that. The truth is, that when I telegraphed for you, I also telegraphed up here, telling the housekeeper to lay out some clothes which belonged to a dear granddaughter of mine, now dead. Come with me."</p>
<p>She led Viola to an adjoining room, which had been prepared for the coming of a guest, and there Viola. saw by the garments thrown on the bed that Mrs. Daly must have received the telegram.</p>
<pb n="106 In Front of a Mirror."/>
<p>"I think they will fit you, dear," said Mrs. Beekman. "Anyhow, I will leave you while you try them on."</p>
<p>"Won't you let me help you first?" Viola asked anxiously, eager to begin her duties.</p>
<p>"There will be time enough for anything of that sort later on, my dear. But, of course, you understand I want ou ofor a companion, and not for a maid. You may help me just as a young person would an older one, but not in any other way."</p>
<p>Perhaps there was a kindly design on the part of the old lady in leaving Viola alone for the first few minutes of her arrival in the house.</p>
<p>Certainly the young girl was glad of the opportunity to sink into a
chair, and have her thoughts to herself. It was almost a necessity that she should be able to look about her and adjust her thoughs to her new conditions.</p>
<p>What strange happenings there had been in her life! It was as if some unseen hand had been guiding her steps.</p>
<p>The more she thought over the finding of the ring in her pocket, the more certain it was in her mind that she was in fact the victim of a dreadful conspiracy, though she was not shrewd enough in guessing to arrive at the truth as the detective had done.</p>
<p>How terrible the future had looked at the moment
<pb n="107 In Front of a Mirror."/>
when she left the store of Hardman & Son, accused of theft, and on her
way to a felon's cell.</p>
<p>But how providentially the rest had happened! The detective had been won to believe her innocent, and had told her to go free.</p>
<p>But even then she must have had a terrible time but for the accident, which in the strangest way had ended in her being where she was.</p>
<p>It calmed her mind to be able to go over the events of the day, and
it seemed to her now that she could bear her troubles with equanimity</p>
<p>She would send what money she had to her mother, and all that she should earn in the future should go to her, too.</p>
<p>In the meanwhile, Walter should be written to, and he would surely advise her what to do.</p>
<p>In her heart she had no doubt that he would return to her at all hazards, and she knew that she had not the strength to bid him remain where he was.</p>
<p>She settled everything before she began to think of dressing, and the improvement in her spirits consequent on doing so was plainly apparent.</p>
<p>The world looked brighter, the future more hopeful, and when she
set about removing her own clothes to don those which had been laid
out for her.<sic corr=","/> she found herself humming a tune.</p>
<pb n="108 In Front of a Mirror."/>
<p>The room she was in was far daintier than any she ever had occupied.</p>
<p>It was furnished not only with luxury, but with such exquisite taste that it seemed like a fairy bower.</p>
<p>The great mullioned window looked down upon a lawn fast whitening under the fall of snow, and in the distance stretched the gray woods, with the lordly Hudson shimmering in the dull afternoon like a great, smooth lake.</p>
<p>The clothes fitted her as if they had been made for no one else, but when they were on, and she saw how rich and elegant they were, she was dismayed.</p>
<p>She had never worn such garments in her life before, though she had seen such in the store, and could make a reasonable guess at their cost.</p>
<p>There was a cheval glass in the room, and she stood in front of it so that she could see herself.</p>
<p>"I should like to wear such clothes," she said to herself. "But as long as they are not mine, I would rather have my shabby ones on."</p>
<p>"But they are yours, my dear," said the soft voice of Mrs. Beekman, who had suddenly entered the room. "They fit you better than they would any one else, and yours they are to be. There are ever so many more, so you see you have a complete outfit. Now, don't attempt to tell me you don't wish them, for my mind is made up, and I am mistress here."</p>
<pb n="109 In Front of a Mirror."/>
<p>"But I do not deserve so much kindness," said Viola.</p>
<p>"Nonsense!" said Mrs. Beekman. "Here is a little pin to fasten at your throat. There! now you look good enough for anything."</p>
<p>"Was the young lady Mr. Beekman's daughter?" asked Viola, shy of appearing before him if the sight of the clothes was likely to bring up unhappy recollections.</p>
<p>"Oh, dear, no! Howard is only thirty-five. Rupert is his only child. The boy's mother died when he was born, and I have always had the care of him. Come. Dear me! how prettily your hair is done up!"</p>
<p>She did not say that she had never seen such a charming little beauty in all her life before, but it was what she thought, and it was what Howard Beekman thought, too, when Viola glided into the parlor, and blushed as he greeted her.</p>

<pb n="[110]"/>
<div id="gfprice014" type="chapter">
<head rend = "center">CHAPTER XIV.</head>
<byline>JUST AS AN OLD MAN.</byline>
<p>Two weeks passed away in the delightful home of the Beekmans, and Viola lacked but one thing of perfect happiness</p>
<p>She had written to her mother, explaining everything, and inclosing
the money she had recieved from the detective, and, in return, she had received a letter telling her to be of good cheer, and remain where she was.</p>
<p>"I shall get along nicely," the letter had said. "I am sure Walter will be home the moment he hears from you. Until then remain where you are."</p>
<p>Now, if she could but get a word from Walter, she would be completely happy. But it was not yet time to receive an answer to the one she had written him about the occurrence, so there was nothing to do but have patience.</p>
<p>Her new friends were as good to her as if she had been of their own
blood, and she had not been long in discovering that the old lady's
offer to make her her companion was nothing more than a device to keep
her at the mansion.</p>
<p>The snow had come, and the lake near the house had
<pb n="111 Just as an Old Man."/>
frozen over; so she went out sleighing with Mr. Beekman and Rupert, and one day was presented with a beautiful new pair of skates, so that
she and Rupert's father might go skating together.</p>
<p>It never once occurred to her that Mr. Beekman was more than courteous to her; that his attentions were as fervent and marked as those of the one who sues for favor.</p>
<p>She often caught his eyes fixed on her face, but when she turned blushingly away, it was with no sort of consciousness that he was telling himself that she was the most beautiful creature he had ever looked upon.</p>
<p>To her, he was simply an old man. Old to her, anyhow, and no more likely to think of loving her, than she of loving him.</p>
<p>But if she did not see it, his mother did; and one day she called him to her as she sat in her favorite seat in the bay window, looking out on the lawn where Viola, with sparkling eyes and rosy cheeks, was playing in the snow with Rupert.</p>
<p>"Howard," said his mother, "she is a very pretty girl, is she not?"</p>
<p>"The most beautiful I ever saw," he answered.</p>
<p>"I have noticed that you look at her a great deal," she said. "Does
it mean anything more than admiration, Howard?"</p>
<pb n="112 Just as an Old Man."/>
<p>He looked over her head, and at the pretty sight out on the lawn before he made any response. Then he said slowly:</p>
<p>"Yes, it means more than admiration. I know that she looks upon me as an old man; but I love her, mother, with the fervor of a young heart, and if she will have me, I will make her my wife."</p>
<p>"You know nothing of her past," she said.</p>
<p>"It is enough to know the present of such a nature as hers. It is as transparent and pure as Heaven-made crystals."</p>
<p>"She may love another," she said again.</p>
<p>"I know it, but I hope not. She is very young to have thought much of such a thing as lovers, and I hope she is heart free. Do you object, mother?"</p>
<p>"You know I do not. She is a lovely girl, as good as she is beautiful, and words could go no further. Win her if you can; but prepare yourself for a disappointment, my boy."</p>
<p>"I will, mother. But do you think I would be unwise to speak to her now of my love? Might it not be well for me if I could bring her to think of me as a lover, and not only as a man who is older than she, and out of her life?"</p>
<p>"Perhaps; but an abrupt confession such as that might startle her. I do not know how to advise you."</p>
<p>"I will let opportunity decide," he said. "How beau-
<pb n="113 Just as an Old Man."/>>
tiful she is! That man will be fortunate who wins her for his wife."</p>
<p>The object of his admiration, meanwhile, was thinking of nothing so little as winning the love of any man.</p>
<p>If love was constantly in her thoughts, it was only because all her soul was filled with longing to see again the dear face of Walter.</p>
<p>She thought of him constantly, but she did not let her longing make her unhappy.</p>
<p>Unconscious now of being seen by the two who watched her from the curtained window, she gave herself up to the task of pleasing little Rupert, until he declared he was too cold to play out of doors any longer.</p>
<p>Then she caught up her skates, which she had thrown on a bench while she frolicked with him, and started down toward the lake.</p>
<p>It was a picturesque spot, and Viola went there to skate dreamily about, while her heart was filled with sweet thoughts of the absent lover.</p>
<p>She had almost forgotten her troubles in the happy belief that no one would give much thought to her now that she had disappeared from the city.</p>
<p>They saw her take up her skates and disappear in the woods by the path that led to the lake.</p>
<p>"I will go skate with her," Howard Beekman said. He hastened away
to get his skates, and his mother,
<pb n="114 Just as an Old Man."/>
looking out of the window, saw him dissappear by the same path which Viola had taken.</p>
<p>"May he win her!" she murmured.</p>
<p>The same hope was in his heart as he walked slowly along the path. He would not go too quickly, lest she might take alarm at being followed so closely.</p>
<p>So he sauntered through the wood, wondering as he drew nearer to the lake that he did not see her form flitting about over the ice.</p>
<p>Still he was not sure that he could see her so far away, though it seemed to him that he had been able to do so on previous occasions.</p>
<p>But as he drew nearer to the lake he was sure that she could not be
there, and he fancied she must have wandered on to a rock from which there was a magnificent vew of the valley of the Hudson.</p>
<p>He hesitated for a while, trying to make up his mind whether to follow her or not, but presently put his hesitation aside and went on to the lake, and looked for her footprints.</p>
<p>They were easily found and unmistakable, because they were so small.  He looked at them fondly for a moment, and then followed where
they led him.</p>
<p>It was not toward the rock, but toward the road they went, and with
a start of dismay Howard Beekman realized that they were side by side
with the footprints of a man.</p>
<pb n="115 Just as an Old Man."/>
<p>Should he follow or not? Who was the man? Was he a stranger? Might it be one of the servants, who, perhaps, had something he wished to show her?</p>
<p>He could not reason out his proper course, but followed because he could not rest without knowing.</p>
<p>Side by side the footprints went until the road was reached: there they stopped. Beads of perspiration stood out on the forehead of Howard Beekman.</p>
<p>"There was a sleigh waiting there," he muttered hoarsely, "and she must have gone in the sleigh! What does it mean?"</p>

<pb n="[116]"/>
<div id="gfprice015" type="chapter">
<head rend = "center">CHAPTER XV.</head>
<byline>"SHE WILL DO MY WORK."</byline>
<p>There was no compunction or remorse in the heart of Eunice Carroll after she had entered on the cruel plot to destroy the reputation of her hated rival.</p>
<p>She was hard by nature, and it was little to her to sweep a poor girl like Viola from her path.</p>
<p>So she waited with a fierce impatience to hear some word as to the result of the plot. It did not come until the evening.</p>
<p>Then Stella, her maid, came to her to say that the detective she had sent for earlier in the day had come, and was waiting in the reception room.</p>
<p>"Did he say anything?" she demanded eagerly.</p>
<p>"Nothing, miss, except to ask for you. I hope he has found the ring."</p>
<p>"And I hope he has the thief in prison!" was the hissing answer.</p>
<p>Stella shrugged her shoulders as she watched her mistress sweep out
of the room.</p>
<p>"How Miss Eunice hates the little shop girl," she murmured. "I wonder why?"</p>
<p>Eunice Carroll burst into the reception room, where the keen-eyed detective awaited her coming.</p>
<pb n="117 She Will Do My Work."/>
<p>"Well," she said eagerly, making no effort to conceal her venom, "have you arrested the girl?"</p>
<p>"Is this your ring?" he asked coldly.</p>
<p>She snatched the ring from him and looked at it carefully.</p>
<p>"Yes, it is mine. Did you find it on her? Did you arrest her?"</p>
<p>"I arrested her," he answered.</p>
<p>"That is good. You shall be rewarded. Those impudent creatures! they pretend to be honest with the most brazen impudence, but they will steal anything."</p>
<p>"I do not understand," he said, a look of dislike passing over his
face like a shadow.</p>
<p>"Those salesgirls," she said, stamping her little foot impatiently. "I mean them. That girl was so indignant because I told her not to go too near the dressing table, and then she stole the ring, after all. So she is in prison, and the newspapers to-morrow will print the story of her crime."</p>
<p>"No, she is not in prison," he said calmly. "She escaped."</p>
<p>"Escaped!" cried Eunice angrily. "And not caught again?"</p>
<p>"Not caught yet," he replied.</p>
<p>"Escaped from you?" she demanded, her black eyes flaming suddenly on his face.</p>
<p>"Escaped from me," he answered impassively.</p>
<pb n="118 She Will Do My Work."/>
<p>"A nice detective, you!" she sneered. "But it is not possible. It is a trick of some sort! The girl could not escape from you. She bought her freedom with her beauty. What is it in her face that makes men care for her? I will inform against you at the office. You let her go!"</p>
<p>"Is this your ring?" he asked, in his cold, unmoved way.</p>
<p>She glared at him with the furious passion of a wild animal.</p>
<p>"It is mine," she replied. "Has anything been done to recapture the girl?"</p>
<p>"The usual steps have been taken," he replied. "Good evening."</p>
<p>He bowed and left the room. She watched him, and listened until she heard the outer door slam. Then she ground her small white teeth together, and stamped upon the floor with her little foot.</p>
<p>"It is a trick, a trick," she muttered angrily. "But what does it matter? The girl is already smirched with the crime. If she has run away it is an admission of guilt. It was her beauty, I suppose. How could she run away from him? I will go see that sneaking wretch who proposed the plot."</p>
<p>Half an hour later her carriage stood in front of the store of Hardman & Son, and she was inside asking for Peter Harriem.</p>
<pb n="118 She Will Do My Work."/>
<p>She was informed that he had gone out earlier in the day, and had not yet returned. Nor did any one know when he would return.</p>
<p>"Perhaps Mr. Hardman will know," she said, in her imperious way. "Where is Mr. Hardman?"</p>
<p>"Upstairs, in his private office."</p>
<p>"Tell him that Miss, Eunice Carroll wishes to see him."</p>
<p>The name was a charm. It was known in the store that she was one of the best customers, and also it was whispered that she and Walter were to be married.</p>
<p>The clerk hastened away, and presently came back with most humble apologies, to say that Mr. Hardman begged her to come up to his office.</p>
<p>So she went up there, and swept proudly into the room where many a heart had grown cold with fear of the hard man who sat there.</p>
<p>"Miss Eunice!" he exclaimed cordially. "What lucky stroke of fortune is it that brings you here? Pray be seated."</p>
<p>"No, I came to see Mr. Harriem about a small matter of business. No one else will do. I only want you to tell me where he is, or when he will return. I am very anxious to see him."</p>
<p>"Peter has left the city, I believe. I received a note from him this afternoon, saying he would not be back
<pb n="120 She Will Do My Work."/>
this evening. If you wish, I will tell him that you desire to see him. He will call the moment he returns."</p>
<p>"You do not know where he has gone?" she queried.</p>
<p>"I have not the least idea, Miss Eunice. You see, he is my confidential man, and I trust him so far that it is not essential that he should tell me just what he does
with his time. Shall I tell him that you wish to see him?"</p>
<p>"If you will be so kind. Good night!"</p>
<p>She went away filled with rage, yet hoping that somehow the absence of her partner in crime meant something in relation to the affair at hand.</p>
<p>That night she was forced to attend a magnificent ball, at which she was the leading beauty.</p>
<p>She retired from the ball early, however, and consumed the remaining hours of the night in a vain effort to sleep.</p>
<p>It was not until morning came that she fell into a troubled slumber, in which her dreams were of the girl she was striving to ruin, and who seemed always to be mocking her with triumphant smiles.</p>
<p>Stella thought she had never known her never-pleasant mistress quite so difficult to get along with.</p>
<p>"If Mr. Harriem calls," said Eunice, when she dismissed her maid at last, "show him up here. Don't wait to announce him."</p>
<p>So at about ten o'clock, the cringing, obsequious
<pb n="121 She Will Do My Work."/>
Peter was ushered into the sumptuous boudoir of the beauty.</p>
<p>He was still bowing and cringing when the door closed in response to an imperious gesture from Eunice to Stella.</p>
<p>"Come nearer and whisper," she said to him fiercely. "I do not know but that girl is at the keyhole. The girl has escaped. Did you know it?"</p>
<p>"I followed her and saw the detective give her money and let her go
free. They did not know I was watching. They do not know that I am as cunning as the best of them."</p>
<p>"Why did you not have her stopped? I wished her to go to prison and be disgraced."</p>
<p>"But I am not so anxious," he said, with a sort of leer, which seemed to indicate that for all his cringing he was not one to be driven. "You know I am to marry her."</p>
<p>"Did you follow her after she left the detective," asked Eunice.</p>
<p>"Yes. That is why I was out of town. I followed her."</p>
<p>"Where is she?" Eunice demanded.</p>
<p>Peter hesitated for a moment, then answered:</p>
<p>"Do you know anybody by the name of Beekman?"</p>
<p>"I know Howard Beekman. He belongs to our set.</p>
<p>"Well, Viola saved his little boy&#x2014;"</p>	
<pb n="122 She Will Do My Work."/>
<p>"Rupert?"</p>
<p>"That is the name. Viola saved his life, and the Beekmans have taken her with them to their home. No doubt they mean to keep her with
them."</p>
<p>"Will you permit it? She might better be here in the store."</p>
<p>Peter shrugged his shoulders.</p>
<p>"What can I do? Now that they are her friends, don't you see that they will be able to help her? It is not as if she were friendless. Besides, you seem to forget the detective.</p>
<p>"What about him?" demanded Eunice quickly.</p>
<p>"Why, don't you see that he suspects somebody or he would not have let her go away. He would not have dared. He is pretty sure that we will not push the matter.</p>
<p>"And you mean," cried Eunice, "that you will let the matter drop just where it is? Why, she is better off than before. And she is sure to write to Walter, telling him what has happened."</p>
<p>Peter turned pale. Simple as the thing was, he had not thought of it.</p>
<p>His subtle brain worked quickly. Suppose she did. the result would be to bring Walter home. That would take at least a month.</p>
<p>In the meantime he should be able to carry out his plans.</p>
<pb n="123 She Will Do My Work."/>
<p>Walter might suspect him, but he would know by Viola's letter that Eunice was the chief factor in the plot against her.</p>
<p>"You are right," he said. "Of course you are right. Why did I not think of it? Walter will be home in a month, and she will explain everything to him.</p>
<p>"They must not meet," hissed Eunice. "Do you think I have begun only to turn back at the first obstacle? Have you a plan that will do?"</p>
<p>"I had a plan, but it is useless," he said gloomily.</p>
<p>"Tell me the plan," she said.</p>
<p>"What is the use? She must have written to him, and he is sure to come home at once. Yes, I had what was a beautiful plan, but it is worthless now. I will give it up."</p>
<p>"Tell me what it was," she said.</p>
<p>"If I tell you I warn you that I will not put it into practice," he said.</p>
<p>"Just as you please about that," she replied. "Tell me the plan."</p>
<p>"Well," he said, "it is certain from the conduct of the detective that it will be idle to think of pressing the charge of theft against her."</p>
<p>"I suppose so. Well?"</p>
<p>"But I thought I could make her believe she was in prison, and the only way of escape was by marrying me."</p>
<pb n="124 She Will Do My Work."/>
<p>"How could you do that?" she asked.</p>
<p>"I was going to find some person who would consent to play the parts of judge and officers. I would give her a mock trial and convict her. I would have sentenced her to prison, and would have found a cell for her. Then I would have gone to her, and I do not doubt she would have married me to escape. Of course, once married to me, Walter would be bound to believe her faithless to him."</p>
<p>"And you give up this plan now?" she asked slowly, as if her thoughts were busy.</p>
<p>"Yes, I give it up now," he answered.</p>
<p>"Well," she said coldly, "if you have given it up, I suppose I might as well do so. Good day!"</p>
<p>He bowed and cringed as he had done on entering, and was gone.</p>
<p>"The cur!" she snapped, when he was gone. "I am well rid of such a coward. Let him go! I will prosecute my plan alone."</p>
<p>"Ha, ha!" Peter chuckled, as he glided down the steps to the sidewalk, "she will do my work for me, and I shall reap the profit. It is the safest way. If she did not hate so bitterly, this could not have been. I am very fortunate."</p>

<pb n="[125]"/>
<div id="gfprice016" type="chapter">
<head rend = "center">CHAPTER XVI.</head>
<byline>A DETECTIVE AGENCY.</byline>
<p>It chanced that this was Eunice's day to receive at home. She dressed in her most becoming gown.</p>
<p>There was one caller she could always depend upon. He was a young lawyer, who was making a specialty of the criminal practice, and who was bent on capturing a rich wife as a fair means of making a good start.</p>
<p>This was Henry Baldwin, one of her most assiduous suitors, whom she alternately snubbed and encouraged.</p>
<p>He came, as she had supposed he would, and began immediately to make himself agreeable to Eunice.</p>
<p>"Oh, Mr. Baldwin," she said earnestly, "we have been talking about detectives. You must know all about them."</p>
<p>It was an interesting subject, and the callers asked him a great many questions. Eunice asked one only once in a while.</p>
<p>Nevertheless, when he was gone she made some notes on a piece of paper, and her black eyes were all aglow with a purpose.</p>
<p>The next morning she went out without the carriage, which was an event, for she disliked to walk on the streets.</p>
<pb n="126 A Detective Agency."/>
<p>"I wonder where she is going?" mused Stella. "She has her last year's cloak on&#x2014;a garment that should have come to me by this time.  I wonder where she is going."</p>
<p>She wondered, but she did not think of trying to find out. She was a luxury-loving young woman, and took as little trouble as possible.</p>
<p>Had she followed her mistress, however, she would have seen her go to a part of the city strange, indeed, for her.</p>
<p>She rode in a Third Avenue street car, too, and reached the Bowery in that way. She studied the street names on the lamp-posts, too, and finally stopped the car and alighted from it.</p>
<p>It was a new experience for the haughty beauty to be in that rude part of the city, and she was angry at being jostled by rough men.</p>
<p>But she did not waver in her purpose, and at last turned in a narrow, dirty hallway, after studying the signs tacked up at the door.</p>
<p>The sign that held her attention longest was one reading:</p>
<p>"Detective Agency. Business of a confidential character transacted promptly and faithfully."</p>
<p>She climbed the rickety stairs to the office of this firm, which Henry Baldwin had said the day before was one of the most unscrupulous
agencies in the city.</p>
<pb n="127 A Detective Agency."/>
<p>There were three men in the office, and their heels came down from their elevation on the desk with a reluctance that showed they paid but scant attention to the requirements of good society.</p>
<p>"I wish to see the manager of this agency!" Eunice said, with a slight shudder at the expressions on the three faces.</p>
<p>"I am the manger," said one, whose pale-blue eyes looked curiously at her out of a pallid, thin face.</p>
<p>"I have some business with you," she said. "I wish to see you alone. What is your name?"</p>
<p>"Sam Purdy. Boys, will you leave us alone?"</p>
<p>The two men winked at him, and stared at her, as they rose from their chairs, and left the room.</p>
<p>"Now madam," Purdy said, motioning to a chair, "what is it? Something in the divorce line?"</p>
<p>"No," she said, "this has nothing to do with a divorce. But before we have any talk, let us understand each other."</p>
<p>"Nothing would suit me better," he replied.</p>
<p>"I have heard of you from a person who knows you thoroughly," she said to him.</p>
<p>"I hope you had a good report," he answered, with indescribable irony.</p>
<p>"It was good for my purpose. He said you were the most unscrupulous
man in New York, and that you would do anything for money.</p>
<pb n="128 A Detective Agency."/>
<p>"And suppose I admit what your friend so flatteringly says of me?" he queried calmly.</p>
<p>"I do not ask you to admit or deny," she replied. "All I wish is that you will understand that any pretense of having scruples will not impose upon me."</p>
<p>"Very well," he said, "we will have that understood; but, really, I do not intend to make any such claim for myself. All I ask is that you will explain your business. If it is something I can do, and you can pay for it, that is all that is necessary to know. What is your business?"</p>
<p>"Let me first assure you that I am able and willing to pay for the service I shall ask of you."</p>
<p>"Very well. What is the service?"</p>
<p>"There is a girl who is in my way," she began.</p>
<p>He interrupted her to say in his calm, even tones:</p>
<p>"Of course, you understand that murder is quite out of my line?"</p>
<p>"I shall ask nothing of the sort of you. The girl is in my way, and I wish her frightened off."</p>
<p>"Is she rich or poor?"</p>
<p>"Poor, and a fugitive from justice. She was accused of stealing a diamond ring, which was found on her person. She was arrested and escaped on the way to prison. I know where she is, and want her, kidnaped. Will you do it?"</p>
<p>"What is to be done with her after that?" he asked.
<pb n="129 A Detective Agency."/>
"I want her to be made to believe that she has simply been arrested. Then a mock trial is to be held, and she is to be sentenced to prison; a cell must be found to put her in, and after that I will take care of her."</p>
<p>Purdy mused a few minutes, and finally said:</p>
<p>"The thing is not so difficult, but it is dangerous. Has she no friends at all?"</p>
<p>"The only friends she has are the people she is now living with, and they have known her but a few days. She saved the life of a little boy, and is now living with his father and grandmother in the country."</p>
<p>"You say you are willing to pay well for this?" he asked.</p>
<p>"You may name your own price for doing it successfully," she answered.</p>
<p>"I will undertake it," he said.</p>

<pb n="[130]"/>
<div id="gfprice017" type="chapter">
<head rend = "center">CHAPTER XVII.</head>
<byline>SHUTTING THE DOOR.</byline>
<p>The thing that had happened to Viola was this:</p>
<p>She had gone with a merry heart from the game in the snow to the lake, intending to skate for a while before entering the house to change her clothes for dinner.</p>
<p>Christmas was almost at hand, and she had been working on some little gifts for her kind friends. She wished to have a little time alone so that she might put some finishing touches to the presents.</p>
<p>She was facing the lake and getting ready to seat herself on a bench so that she might put on her skates, when she was startled by a voice saying in a sharp whisper:</p>
<p>"Miss Redmond! Do not be alarmed. I come from your friend, the detective, who gave you your freedom when you were falsely accused of theft."</p>
<p>She turned with beating heart, alarmed in spite of his reassuring words, and faced a stranger, whose face was none too prepossessing.</p>
<p>"What do you want?" she said, pressing her little hand against her throbbing bosom.</p>
<p>"I only want to tell you that your friend is waiting
<pb n="131 Shutting the Door."/>
at the roadside. He has something important to tell you. But you must come quickly," and the man glanced uneasily toward the house.</p>
<p>"How can I be sure?" she began to say, when he interrupted impatiently:</p>
<p>"Well, I can't stay here, miss. If you don't believe me, I must go. The only thing I will say is that he has something to say about a Mr. Walter Hardman, who is supposed to be in Europe, but who isn't."</p>
<p>"Walter!" she cried. "Oh, can it be that he&#x2014;
<p>"I don't say he is waiting for you," the man interrupted. "I guess if he was around here you'd know it.
But it's something about him. Well, I can't wait. I said you wouldn't come with me."</p>
<p>"Yes, I will, I will," she said nervously. "I will go at once."</p>
<p>She started toward the road, and the man took his place by her side, walking very quickly, so that it was all she could do to keep pace with him.</p>
<p>"Can you tell me nothing of Wal&#x2014; Mr. Hardman?" she asked.</p>
<p>"Don't worry about him, miss. I can tell you he is alI right. You will see him soon enough, too. Could you walk a little faster? I wouldn't like to be seen by anybody from the house."</p>
<p>She quickened her pace almost to a run, and presently was where she could see the road. There was a
<pb n="132 Shutting the Door."/>
sleigh there, with a man sitting in it, all wrapped to the eyes in mufflers.</p>
<p>The sleigh was a large two-seated affair, and was drawn by two powerful bay horses.</p>
<p>All this she noticed mechanically. She wondered if the man in the sleigh was the detective.</p>
<p>She turned to ask such a question of her companion, when suddenly, and with the celerity of lightning, a heavy shawl was thrown over her head, and she was lifted in a powerful pair of arms and carried to the sleigh.</p>
<p>She fought fiercely for her freedom; but in the folds of the shawl were the fumes of a drug, and in a very little while her struggles ceased, and she became unconscious.</p>
<p>And after that she did not know what happened to her; but, as a matter of fact, she was put quickly into the bottom of the sleigh and covered with robes, so that no one could know that she was there.</p>
<p>The driver, meanwhile, had roused himself suddenly, and had put the whip to the horses almost while the men were getting in.</p>
<p>Everything had passed with the utmost quickness, so that the sleigh was far out of sight when Howard Beekman reached the place were it had stood.</p>
<p>It sped on and on, the horses dripping with lather
<pb n="133 Shutting the Door."/>
but the driver plying his whip at the least sign of slackening their pace.</p>
<p>The man who had decoyed Viola, and the one who had thrown the shawl over her head conversed in whispers as they went.</p>
<p>"And it was a good job well done," said the decoy who, in fact, was no other than Purdy himself.</p>
<p>"And a good job well over when it is over," said the other. "I always hate these kidnaping jobs. However, we have a safe place to put her in. The Old Boy himself would not find her there."</p>
<p>"The greater the risk, the greater the pay;" said Purdy. "Did you notice what a beauty she is?"</p>
<p>"I should say so. What a lonely road! I hope Tom knows the way. It is growing dark already in these woods."</p>
<p>"Don't worry about him," said Purdy, "he knows he way. I hope she won't rouse up before we get her there."</p>
<p>It was pitch dark before the horses stopped, and it had long been a wonder to those in the back seat how the driver could find his way, but Purdy had plenty of faith, for he kept reassuring his companion all the time.</p>
<p>"Here we are," said Purdy, in a low tone. "Get out and take her up. I will go ahead and show you the way I can see the house now."</p>
<pb n="134 Shutting the Door."/>
<p>"It's snowing," said the other, as he took Viola in his arms.</p>
<p>"Luck is with us. Our tracks will be so covered that the best detective on the force couldn't find them. Come along. How dark it is. If it wasn't for the snow I couldn't see a step. All right, Tom, you may go now."</p>
<p>He kicked the snow off his feet on the heavy wooden railing of the porch as he spoke, and a moment later fitted a key in the lock of the door, and led the other into a dark, but comfortably warm house.</p>
<p>"Take her right downstairs into the cell we fixed for her," Purdy said. "She might as well wake up in it, I suppose you haven't tried to please the black-eyed tiger cat by giving an overdose, have you?"</p>
<p>"No, I didn't do it, but I was half tempted to. If I'd been dead sure you wouldn't cut up rough, I believe I would have done it."</p>
<p>The man carrying Viola laughed as he went dow the stairs, made light now by Purdy's lighting a lamp in the hall.</p>
<p>"Be sure to give her the antidote, Steve," said Purdy "And be sure to play the jailer in character. You ought to know what the correct thing is."</p>
<p>"Yes," laughed Steve, "I've made his acquaintance often enough."</p>
<p>He took Viola to a room which had been fitted up
<pb n="135 Shutting the Door."/>
exact resemblance of a prison cell. It might have deceived an old inmate of prisons.</p>
<p>He deposited her on the little iron bedstead and lit the lamp which gave light in the cell, but could not be reached by the prisoner.</p>
<p>Then he took from his pocket a box, from which he took some powder, which he held under the nostrils of the insensible girl.</p>
<p>She inhaled the powder by degrees, and presently began to move her limbs.</p>
<p>The instant she did so he stepped outside the door and threw off his outer garments, removing every trace of having just been out in the cold.</p>
<p>Viola shuddered, moaned, and presently started up like one awakening from a nightmare.</p>
<p>"Oh!" she gasped. "Where am I?"</p>
<p>"You are in prison on the charge of having stolen a diamond ring from the room of Miss Eunice Carroll," said the pretended jailer harshly. "Come! I have been waiting long enough for you to wake up. Do
you want any supper? I want to close up and go to bed. All the other prisoners have had their supper long ago. If I wasn't so tender-hearted, I'd have let you go without."</p>
<p>Viola listened in terror.</p>
<p>Was it possible that she was at last the inmate of a
<pb n="136 Shutting the Door."/>
prison? Her heart sank. She shook her head and sobbed:</p>
<p>"I cannot eat. Oh, sir! shall I have to remain here long?"</p>
<p>"Until your trial, and, then, if you are convicted, you will probably be sent to Sing Sing for five or ten years. Well, I must go."</p>
<p>He shut the door with a clang, and she could hear the key turn and
the bolts shoot across the door.</p>
<p>She sank on her knees and sobbed.</p>

<pb n="[137]"/>
<div id="gfprice018" type="chapter">
<head rend = "center">CHAPTER XVII.</head>
<byline>A WOMAN'S LIFE FORESHADOWED.</byline>
<p>If Mr. Howard Beekman had had someting of the detective spirit in his nature, he no doubt would have examined the footprints in the snow
with a care and a purpose which would have resulted in the immediate discovery of some of the facts connected with the abduction of Viola.</p>
<p>As it was, he only returned home in a dejected frame of mind, feeling sure that his beautiful guest had had a clandestine meeting with some man whom he did not doubt she loved.</p>
<p>He avoided his mother for a while; but as the minutes slipped by and over an hour had passed without the return of Viola, He became at once uneasy and indignant, saying to himself:</p>
<p>"It is not right in Viola to do this. Respect for my mother should have restrained her."</p>
<p>He started angrily for his mother's room, but decided first to go out once more to see if perhaps Viola had returned and was skating.</p>
<p>He, therefore, hastened out to the lake, but of course saw no sign of her, and was obliged to return to the house with no better knowledge than he had had before.</p>
<pb n="138 A Woman's Life Foreshadowed."/>
<p>His mother had seen him go out this time and return, alone, and called to him from her room to come to her.</p>
<p>She saw by his face and agitated manner that something was wrong, and she exclaimed at once:</p>
<p>"Howard! what is the matter?"</p>
<p>"Viola," he answered, his voice broken, for it was a terrible blow to him that the young girl he had learned to love should have acted so.</p>
<p>"Viola! What of her? I saw you go out just now as if you had not been with her, and then return at once with something in your manner that filled me with fear. What has happened, Howard?</p>"
<p>"Sit down, mother," he said, trying to calm himself. "It is nothing, only that I have been disappointed."</p>
<p>"What do you mean by disappointed?" she demanded quickly. "Has she refused you? Or is it something else?"</p>
<p>"She has not refused me, mother," he replied. "I almost wish it was that. She has gone for a sleigh ride with some man whom she met near the lake."</p>
<p>"Impossible!" ejaculated Mrs. Beekman, with positive assurance.</p>
<p>"But the proof of it is plain," he said. "There are footprints in the snow&#x2014;hers by the side of his all the way to the roadside, where the sleigh had been waiting."</p>
<p>"There is some explanation of this which will vindi-
<pb n="139 A Woman's Life Foreshadowed."/>
cate her, Howard, even if what you tell me is all true. Tell me everything."</p>
<p>Howard Beekman told his mother everything as he had observed it, and she listened with a keener brain than his.</p>
<p>"How long ago was it?" she demanded, glancing at the clock.</p>
<p>"There is something peculiar in this," she said. "Give me nay hat and cloak and arctics. We will go out together and see if anything will be revealed to us together."</p>
