<p>With a quickness that showed how much in earnest the old lady was, she was ready for the trip, and presently they were both out by the lake, examining the footprints.</p>
<p>"We must learn what we can at once," she said, looking nervously up at the sky. it is going to snow again, and the tracks will be covered up,"</p>
<p>It was surprising to see with what patience and skill the old lady looked over the ground, making her son, however, feel as if she: Were only wasting time there.</p>
<p>But presently she cried out, as she pointed to some tracks which had completely escaped his notice:</p>
<p>"Do you see those footprints? The man, whoever he was, waited for her behind a tree, and did not let his presence be known, until she was ready to put on her skates."</p>
<pb n="140 A Woman's Life Foreshadowed."/>
<p>"I don't see how you can tell that," her son said.</p>
<p>"Ah, my boy!" the old lady said, with a sweet smile, "I often think women have faculties which are denied most men. Why, look! Her footmarks show that she came here and faced the lake without having a thought of anybody being here. Can you not see that?"</p>
<p>"Yes, that is true."</p>
<p>"Then do you not see that she turned hurriedly, as if he had spoken to her and had startled her?"</p>
<p>"I shall begin to think you were born a detective," he said wonderingly.</p>
<p>"My son," she said, "no one ever knows with what faculties a woman is born. It is only a man who can choose; a woman's life is laid out for her by others. But never mind that. Do you see how the man stood off there and talked until he had satisfied Viola? Do you not see how he has made quite a hollow in the snow where he stood and shifted his feet?"</p>
<p>"Mother, it is wonderful!" Howard cried. "Go on. I beg of you!"</p>
<p>"I can make no more out here. Let us follow the footsteps."</p>
<p>Howard supported his mother as they went as quickly along in the track of the footprints as she was able.</p>
<p>When they came near the road, she suddenly stopped and pointed down.</p>
<pb n="141 A Woman's Life Foreshadowed."/>
<p>"There are the footprints of another man. And, look, Howard! can you see her footprints any more? It is lost to me. And see! That other man was hidden behind a tree. Howard! there has been treachery toward our little Viola. Oh! how could you have failed to see this when you were here?"</p>
<p>"Would that I had!" he cried fiercely. "Oh, if this be so, I will pursue and punish the scoundrels. But what could be the motive?"</p>
<p>"I do not know. What does that matter? Oh, Howard, get out the sleigh and go in pursuit. Take help, for there were three men."</p>
<p>"How do you know that?" he demanded.</p>
<p>"Because you can see by the way the snow was pawed up that the horses were restive, and would not have stood without a man in the sleigh to hold the reins. And we know that there were two men out here. They must have picked her tip and put her in the sleigh."</p>
<p>"I will come here and try to follow the sleigh," he said; "though I do not expect to be able to do much. It will be hard to distinguish its tracks in the road."</p>
<p>"True, but you can inquire of everybody you meet about a two-seated sleigh with three men and a young lady in it; it is the only thing you can do, and will be better than sitting at home waiting in idleness."</p>
<p>"Yes," he returned fiercely, "and I may get on their
<pb n="142 A Woman's Life Foreshadowed."/>
track. Let us go home, and I will give the order at once."</p>
<p>Within twenty minutes the horses had been put to the sleigh, and Howard, armed with a pair of revolvers, and accompanied by the most courageous of the men about the place, was following as best they could in pursuit of the abductors of Viola.</p>
<p>But it was a hopeless task; for even if there had been a track which anybody could have followed, the snow came on at last, and effectually covered every trace.</p>
<p>They asked questions of whomever they met, but gained no information whatever, and at last were obliged reluctantly to turn back toward home.</p>
<p>There was a vague hope in the mind of Howard Beekman that he would see the bright, beautiful face of Viola when he reached the house; but the hope was shattered the moment he looked into his mother's face.</p>
<p>"It was useless," he said. "We could hear nothing of her, and the snow obliterated all tracks in the road. What shall we do. now? We must do something, for we cannot leave her to her fate. We are responsible for her."</p>
<p>"I have thought it all out while you were gone," his mother answered soberly. "You must go to the city and see her mother. It will be a shock to her, but it is the only way. She will be able to give us the clew<sic/> to the outrage, perhaps."</p>
<pb n="143 A Woman's Life Foreshadowed."/>
<p>"You are right," Howard said, looking at his watch. "I can catch the six-o'clock express to the city. I will go upstairs and get ready at once."</p>
<p>When he came down his mother had the address of Mrs. Redmond, which she put into his hand.</p>
<p>"Remember that she is ill," she said to him, "and do not forget that she is poor."</p>
<p>"Why do you say that; mother?" he asked reproachfully. "Are you afraid that I shall care less for Viola because of the place I find her mother in?"</p>
<p>"It would have been natural if you had been affected, Howard," she answered.</p>
<p>"Nothing can affect my love for her," he said. "I love her so madly that I would have her at any cost. I did not know until now how much I love her, mother."</p>

<pb n="[144]"/>
<div id="gfprice019" type="chapter">
<head rend = "center">CHAPTER XIX.</head>
<byline>"I LOVE YOUR DAUGHTER."</byline>
<p>Howard Beekman was an older man than Walter, and had led a fashionable life far more years, so that it was not so easy for him to reconcile himself to the thought of Viola's mother living in the poor, shabby tenement in front of which he found himself on the night he left home.</p>
<p>Nevertheless, he was too much in earnest in his passion for Viola to think of hesitating for a moment.</p>
<p>The aristocratic Howard Beekman inquired his way to Mrs. Redmond's apartment of a boy, and then toiled up the long and rickety flights of stairs.</p>
<p>He stopped for a moment at the door of Mrs. Redmond's room, to collect himself for the ordeal of looking upon the woman who was the mother of the girl he loved.</p>
<p>But when, in response to his knock, he heard the sweet, refined voice bid him come in, his apprehensions vanished, and he was certain that he was to enter the presence of a lady.</p>
<p>He opened the door with a respectful air of courtesy, and was charmed to see the face of the invalid.</p>
<p>It was a great deal to him to be sure that she was not
<pb n="145 I Love Your Daughter."/>
coarse and vulgar; and his immediate thought was that she had seen better days.</p>
<p>"Good evening, Mrs. Redmond!" he said courteously.</p>
<p>"Good evening, sir!" she responded, gazing at him with an air of surprise and wonder.</p>
<p>"My name," he said, anxious to relieve any feeling of doubt she might have concerning him, "is Beekman. It was my child your daughter saved from a cruel death."</p>
<p>The widow's face lighted up with pleased expectation, and she looked at him more earnestly than before; but even as she looked, her mother's eye detected the uneasiness and concern depicted on his face, and she cried out:</p>
<p>"Is anything wrong? Has anything happened? Tell me of Viola?"</p>
<p>"I beg you to be calm, Mrs. Redmond," Howard said, seeing that the kindest way would be to deal frankly with her. "Something has happened; but I hope with your assistance to bring everything out right."</p>
<p>Mrs. Redmond half rose in her chair, a look of fear on her delicate face.</p>
<p>"Have her enemies followed and taken her?" she cried.</p>
<p>He started.</p>
<pb n="146 I Love Your Daughter."/>
<p>"Then she had enemies?" he cried. 'You feared for her?"</p>
<p>"Tell me what has happened!" Mrs. Redmond cried. "Let me know everything, and I shall know better what to do, what to say."</p>
<p>Howard Beekman drew a chair near to Mrs. Redmond and sat down. Then he told her succinctly what had happened.</p>
<p>She listened with an agony of mind hard to describe. It seemed to her as if the worst had happened to her darling child, and yet she only suspected that she had been followed by her enemies and arrested.</p>
<p>"Oh, my poor, poor child!" she moaned. "Mr. Beekman, I must tell you the whole truth in order that you may be able to comprehend what has happened. Do you know the firm of Hardman & Son?"</p>
<p>"Very well."</p>
<p>She was employed there. The son of Mr. Hardman saw her and fell in love with her."</p>
<p>"In love with her!" gasped Howard, feeling a shock of jealousy run through him. "And&#x2014;and did she return this love? Was it an honorable love?"</p>
<p>"Perfectly honorable. Walter Hardman is a noble-hearted young man; and Viola&#x2014;</p>
<p>"Do not tell me that she loved him!" he interposed, with pallid lips.</p>
<p>The widow stared at him with amazement.</p>
<pb n="147 I Love Your Daughter."/>
<p>"She did return his love," she replied. "Is there any reason that you know why it should not be?"</p>
<p>"Oh, Heaven!" he cried; "I know the best of reasons. I love your daughter, and have cherished the hope that some day I might win her love and make her my wife. I know that I am much older, but age should be no bar. I would make her rich, and would strive with all my soul to make her happy. My mother loves her dearly, and looks forward to the happy consummation of my hope."</p>
<p>Mrs. Redmond shook her head pityingly.</p>
<p>"I am sorry," she said earnestly. "I am the more sorry because I know that his love for her is the cause of all her misery."</p>
<p>"Then why should she not be weaned from him? Why would it not be well to lead her heart from him, so that I may make her happy?"</p>
<p>The widow shook her head.</p>
<p>"Do you know so little of the passion of love, sir? Ah! you could never cause those two hearts to love any other. But you will understand why I wish she could love you when you hear my story."</p>
<p>With this she told him what had happened to Viola, explaining besides what the detective, who had visited her several times, had told her of the probable nature of the plot against Viola.</p>
<p>"I see it all," Howard said. "They have traced her
<pb n="148 I Love Your Daughter."/>
to my house, and have stolen her away with the aid of an officer of the law. Even now she may be, and probably is, locked up in some prison cell. Oh, I must see this detective who has been so kind! I will tell him what has happened, and it may be that he will know what to suggest."</p>
<p>"Thank you, sir," Mrs. Redmond said.</p>
<p>"And do not fear any more," Mr. Beekman said earnestly. "I am rich, and I will spend every cent I have, if necessary, in order to rescue her. Have no doubt that we will expose this terrible plot against her, and free her fair name. But there is one thing I beg of you."</p>
<p>"What is it? If it is anything in my power I will accede to it."</p>
<p>"You admitted that, it would be better for Viola if she were my wife. In all the world there is no one who would seek to injure her because she was loved by me. If she were mine she would be safe from harm."</p>
<p>"Alas! I see that. But of what avail is it to admit it?"</p>
<p>"Listen to me," he said vehemently. "Walter Hardman is but a boy, dependent on the favor of his father. My wealth is all my own, and at the mercy of no one."</p>
<p>"I realize that. But what of it? She loves him."</p>
<p>"Yes; but if he has deserted her, and is of no use to
<pb n="149 I Love Your Daughter."/>
her in the hour of her dire peril, what right has he to stand between her and me?"</p>
<p>"Ah," said Mrs. Redmond, "I would choose you for her husband, for I am sure you would make her happy, and then all her troubles would be over when she was your wife. But of what avail is it what I prefer? She loves him, and will never give him up."</p>
<p>"But I love her," he said doggedly. "They say everything is fair in love and war. Will you help me to win her?"</p>
<p>"I will do anything I can for you," she said. "Walter has gone away, to be gone a year, and during that time her enemies may ruin her fair young life. I freely say I would rejoice to see her your wife."</p>
<p>"And when the time comes, then," he said eagerly, "you will be ready to add the weight of your word to my pleading?"</p>
<p>"Assuredly I will."</p>
<p>"Then," he cried hopefully, "I shall win her. Already I have a plan in my mind, which seems to me will enable me to find her."</p>
<p>"You will go at once to the detective, will you not?" she asked anxiously.</p>
<p>"No; and I beg you not to say a word to the detective should he chance to call on you. Give me a few hours in which to report. If I do not come to you to-morrow morning, or even by midnight to-night, and
<pb n="150 I Love Your Daughter."/>
say. that I shall set her free, you may then apply to the detective."</p>
<p>"But anything may happen in the meantime," she said anxiously. "Remember she is my child!"</p>
<p>"And she is my love. Better than all the rest of the world, I love her. I swear to you that I will leave no stone unturned to set her free and make her happy."</p>

<pb n="[151]"/>
<div id="gfprice020" type="chapter">
<head rend = "center">CHAPTER XX.</head>
<byline>CUPID AT ANY COST.</byline>
<p>Howard Beekman left the presence of Mrs. Redmond, filled with the idea of separating Viola from Walter. He had spoken no more than the truth when he had said that his not overkeen mind had conceived a scheme which had every promise of yielding good results.</p>
<p>He hastened from the poor tenement to the nearest taxicab stand, and gave the address of one of the most fashionable mansions in the city.</p>
<p>When the house was reached he mounted its steps with a feeling quite different from that which had stirred him on going up the creaking steps to Mrs. Redmond's room.</p>
<p>"Is Miss Carroll at home?" he asked of the servant, and was shown with obsequious attention into the reception room.</p>
<p>Howard paced the floor of the room while the servant was delivering his card. He knew Eunice Carroll, having met her in society, and it was in direct pursuance of his plan that he had come to her, who, he had no doubt, was one of the enemies who was persecuting Viola.</p>
<pb n="152 Cupid at any Cost."/>
<p>Presently he heard the rustling of her silk skirts, as she swept down the staircase. He seated himself and waited with calm face, prepared to meet the haughty beauty.</p>
<p>Eunice wondered why she should be called on by Howard Beekman. She asked herself if it was possible that he suspected her of having had any hand in the abduction of his guest.</p>
<p>But she had the wit to act as if that was the last thing in her mind. She entered the room with a pleasant smile on her face, and with her little hand outstretched.</p>
<p>"I am so pleased to see you, Mr. Beekman," she said. "I had heard you were living in the country this winter."</p>
<p>He smiled and looked fixedly into her dark eyes. "Who was it told you. I was in the country?" he asked.</p>
<p>A faint shade of color tinted her olive cheeks as he spoke in this peculiar manner, but she answered steadily:</p>
<p>"I don't think I could recall. It is generally known, is it not?"</p>
<p>"I think it is, Miss Carroll," he answered. "But never mind that. I have a request to make of you. I wonder if you will grant it?"</p>
<pb n="153 Cupid at any Cost."/>
<p>"Better make the request and find out, then," she said, with a smile.</p>
<p>"It is a serious matter," he said. "I am much older than you, Miss Carroll, but I am not so old that I cannot feel the throbbing of love in my heart. It is in your power to aid me win the object of my love, and I have come to beg you to do it."</p>
<p>"But, Mr. Beekman!" she stammered, gazing at him with a sort of dismay. For it was not possible, from his words, to be sure that she was not the person he referred to.</p>
<p>"I hope you will do as I wish," he said earnestly. "I know that I am appealing to one who knows the power of love. I am sure that you love Walter Hardman. That being so, you should be willing to help me."</p>
<p>She closed her lips tightly together. She could not yet comprehend what he meant, and was only sure that he did not meditate a proposal to her.</p>
<p>"I do not know by what right you make that assertion," she said coldly.</p>
<p>"Do not be angry," he said. "I was forced to approach my point in this way, in order that you might fully understand me. I love Viola Redmond, and I know she is in your way. Will you not be frank with me now? She does not love me, but yet I must win her. We can help each other if you only will."</p>
<p>"Why do you appeal to me in this way?"</p>
<pb n="154 Cupid at any Cost."/>
<p>"Because I know that you have been instrumental in spiriting her away from my house. You see, I am frank with you."</p>
<p>"You are mistaken," Eunice said, her face paling.</p>
<p>"No, I am not mistaken," he said. "I know your whole plot with that man, Harriem. I could have her found and set free. But if I do that, she will go on loving Walter Hardman, and what I wish is to separate those two. So I come to you to ask you to help me win her, while at the same time you win back Walter Hardman. Will you do it?"</p>
<p>"How can I aid you? What am I to do?" she asked.</p>
<p>"You are to reveal all the details of your plot to me, and let me turn the situation to my own advantage."</p>
<p>"If I could only be sure that you would deal fairly with me," she said.</p>
<p>"The best pledge of that," he rejoined, "is the fact that our interests are identical."</p>
<p>"But I," she said, with an outburst of fierceness that startled him, "will have Walter at any cost. Nothing shall stand in my way. I will crush to the earth any human being who comes between him and me. Are you willing to go so far?"</p>
<p>"I will go any lengths," he replied. "My life is bound up in her, and I must have her."</p>
<p>"Very well, then. I will tell you what I have done.
<pb n="155 Cupid at any Cost."/>
But let me warn you, Mr. Beekman, it would be far better for you to put yourself under the claws of an enraged tigress than to attempt to play me false."</p>
<p>"Have no such fear. If I had wished to betray you, why need I have come to you at all? It is Viola I wish, and that is all."</p>
<p>"I believe you," Eunice said, calming herself. "What I have done is simple. Peter Harriem followed Viola and knew you had taken her into your home in the country."</p>
<p>"She has been under surveillance since the first, then?" he cried.</p>
<p>"Since the first moment she encountered you at the station," was the response. "I engaged the services of an unscrupulous detective, who has abducted her, and who is to confine her in a cell made to resemble a prison cell. She is to be tried as if by a real court, and is to be sentenced to prison for ten years. By that time we hope to have her fears so worked on that she will be willing to marry Peter Harriem in order to become free. That was the plan. It only needs to substitute you for him. He knows nothing of her abduction. How does the plan strike you?"</p>
<p>"It will not do. I have a better. Let all be done as you say, though it tears my soul to think of subjecting her to such misery. But instead of asking her to wed me to free herself, it will be better for me to
<pb n="156 Cupid at any Cost."/>
offer myself to free her from prison. Then I will take her to my home secretly, and hide her there in the upper rooms."</p>
<p>"I see. She will be hidden from the world then, and will make no effort to free herself. But how will you win her, then?"</p>
<p>"I will make her believe that Walter is false to her before I utter one word of love."</p>
<p>"The plan is good. I will give you the address of the house where she is imprisoned, and you may consult with the men. Ah, we will separate them yet!"</p>

<pb n="[157]"/>
<div id="gfprice021" type="chapter">
<head rend = "center">CHAPTER XXI.</head>
<byline>"DON'T THROW ME ASIDE."</byline>
<p>Peter Harriem was delighted with himself. He believed he had managed the affair of Viola with a skill that redounded greatly to his credit.</p>
<p>He had put his brains into the scheme, and others did the work and paid the entire expenses of it, besides.</p>
<p>The only trouble he had was that he could not fully trust Eunice.</p>
<p>So he spent much of his spare time in going where he could catch a glimpse of her, in order that he might judge by her expression how the plot was succeeding.</p>
<p>Then, because there was no other way of doing, he took an occasional trip to see Viola herself.</p>
<p>His plan, when in the Beekman grounds, was to hide behind a tree and watch for Viola.</p>
<p>After he had seen her, and had gloated over her fresh young beauty for a while, he would sneak away and take the train home.</p>
<p>But as he knew that Eunice must have agents, he watched for them, too, and one day he detected a fellow stealing through the trees, and hiding.</p>
<p>That man he followed with such success that, although he was left behind the first time, the second
<pb n="158 Don't Throw Me Aside."/>
time he discovered the house which the conspirators were preparing for their unhappy prisoner.</p>
<p>After that Peter's only task was to learn when Viola was abducted. No wonder he felt like the master of the situation.</p>
<p>One night he was sneaking past the Carroll mansion, when a gentleman stepped out of a cab and swept up the steps to the door.</p>
<p>Always on the alert, Peter immediately timed his steps so that he could turn about and walk back in time to see the gentleman when the door opened.</p>
<p>"Howard Beekman!" he muttered. "What is he doing there? I will find out somehow."</p>
<p>He puzzled his brains for an idea until at last he saw Howard Beekman come out of the house again.</p>
<p>A person less shrewd than he was must have recognized the fact that there was a difference between the way Howard had gone into the house and the way
he came out.</p>
<p>"He has been successful in whatever he went there for," was what Peter said to himself.</p>
<p>And as that idea flashed into his brain, another came with it.</p>
<p>"What would Howard Beekman, Viola's employer, have to say to Eunice Carroll that should make him happy? What but some arrangement with her that would give him Viola. He loves Viola."</p>
<pb n="159 Don't Throw Me Aside."/>
<p>He saw how his chances were imperiled, and his face grew white with anger and fear. Nevertheless, he did not think of giving up.</p>
<p>He walked several times around the block to give himself time to recover his self-control and to collect his ideas.</p>
<p>Then he walked up to the door of the Carroll mansion and rang the bell with an assured hand.</p>
<p>The footman's stare at him was supercilious, but the servant recalled that the same sneaking-looking individul had once before been admitted to the house, and he admitted him.</p>
<p>When her maid, Stella, told her that Peter Harriem was waiting for her in the reception room, Eunice frowned, and was on the point of sending word that she could not see him.</p>
<p>But she changed her mind and bade the maid take word that she would see him in a few minutes.</p>
<p>So she took time to make up her mind what she would say to him. Then she went down to the drawing-room and found Peter sitting on the edge of a chair.</p>
<p>"Ah, Mr. Harriem! is it you?" said Eunice languidly.</p>
<p>Peter knew by her tone that she was going to try to deceive him in some way.</p>
<p>"Yes, Miss Carroll," he said fawningly. "I thought
<pb n="160 Don't Throw Me Aside."/>
I would come to ask you if you had decided what you would do about that girl."</p>
<p>"Girl!" repeated Eunice, with a slight raising of the eyebrows. "Oh, you mean that wretched creature who stole my ring?"</p>
<p>"Yes," he said, "the one who stole your ring."</p>
<p>"What was the use of doing anything?" she queried carelessly. "I thought you would do whatever was necessary. She was out of my way already."</p>
<p>"Yes, she was out of your war, it is true," he said.</p>
<p>"But she has been able to write to Walter."</p>
<p>Eunice started.</p>
<p>"To Walter!" she cried, her appearance of indifference disappearing. "How do you know that?"</p>
<p>"If I had no other way of telling than by reasoning, I would be sure of it. But I know it from his father. Walter has cabled."</p>
<p>"Cabled what?"</p>
<p>"That he is coming home."</p>
<p>"Coming home! Walter coming home! Why?"</p>
<p>Peter shrugged his shoulders.</p>
<p>"She has written him about her arrest, I suppose. She may even have told him the whole story of the ring."</p>
<p>Eunice turned pale. She wondered why she had not thought of all that before. She cursed herself for not having taken measures to prevent any correspondence.</p>
<pb n="161 Don't Throw Me Aside."/>
<p>"I will kill her if she has," she hissed. "I will not let her have him!"</p>
<p>"If you have done nothing as yet," Peter said, "I do not see what you can do now. For my part, I shall give her up and try to make terms with Walter. He will never forgive anybody who has injured Viola."</p>
<p>Eunice sprang to her feet, forgetful of the part she had set out to play, and thinking only of the possibility of losing Walter.</p>
<p>"She is in my power!" she cried. "I will destroy her!"</p>
<p>"How can she be in your power when she is not in prison?" he said. "Ah, if we had only carried out that plan, I could see a way by which we could make Walter think her story to him a  pure falsehood. But it is too late. We might as well give up."</p>
<p>"Give up? Never! But I do not see what difference it would make if we had her in our hands. What could you do?"</p>
<p>Peter was quite convinced now that Viola had been abducted, and that Eunice had intended to play him false.</p>
<p>He shrugged his shoulders with his exasperating humility, and whined:</p>
<p>"What is the use of talking about it? Good night, Miss Eunice! I am sorry our pretty little plot should, have miscarried."</p>
<pb n="162 Don't Throw Me Aside."/>
<p>He rose to go. But she glided in front of him and detained him, her lips trembling.</p>
<p>"Tell me what you would have done had we carried out our plan of abducting her?" she said.</p>
<p>"What is the use of wasting time over that?" he said. "Abduct her first, and I will show you how we could make Walter's visit an advantage to you."</p>
<p>"She is abducted!" Eunice cried fiercely. "I have just received word."</p>
<p>He raised his eyebrows, then smiled cunningly.</p>
<p>"And yet you tried to make me believe you had forgotten about her. Oh, Miss Carroll, I am afraid you only think she is abducted."</p>
<p>"I tell you it is true. See! read this letter."</p>
<p>She snatched a letter from her pocket and showed it to Peter. He noticed that it was postmarked in the city and had a special-delivery stamp.</p>
<p>He knew by that that a messenger had been sent from up the river, and that he had posted the letter after his arrival.</p>
<p>He opened the letter and read it carefully, Eunice standing in front of him gnawing her red lips.</p>
<p>"Yes," he said, as he replaced the letter in the envelope, "I see that she is in your hands."</p>
<p>"Now tell me your plan," she said.</p>
<p>He rubbed his hands together for a moment, a peculiar smile in his beady eyes.</p>
<pb n="163 Don't Throw Me Aside."/>
<p>"Tell me yours first," he said.</p>
<p>"My plan! you know it was to try her and then let you win her in marriage by offering to release her."</p>
<p>His smile broadened on his gaunt face, and rubbed his hands harder than ever.</p>
<p>"What was your arrangement with Mr. Howard Beekman?" he asked.</p>
<p>She started. She saw that she had been detected in her plot with Howard. But she cared nothing for Howard. She only cared to win Walter.</p>
<p>"What do you know of that?" she demanded sharply.</p>
<p>"I know that you made some arrangement with him, and that you intended to let me take care of myself," he answered. "I think you would find me a better help than Mr. Beekman."</p>
<p>She thought so herself, now. This fawning wretch was shrewder than she had thought. The only thing was to betray to him what she had agreed on with Howard.</p>
<p>This she did without compunction. Peter listened to the end with a smiling face.</p>
<p>"Now tell me your plan," she said, when she had finished her narration.</p>
<p>He let something in the nature of a grin flit across his face.</p>
<p>"I had no plan. Walter is not coming home. I merely wished to show you that you would be unwise
<pb n="164 Don't Throw Me Aside."/>
to try to throw me aside. You cannot do without me. I have followed everything you have done. You see, I was here at the right time. Give me a letter to your men, saying I am the one to be obeyed. I will look out for the rest."</p>
<p>Eunice's black eyes blazed as they looked on the man who had tricked her into a betrayal of herself. But she realized that he was far too shrewd to be trifled with.</p>
<p>If she would win Walter it must be through him and not through Howard.</p>
<p>"Will you promise not to embroil me with Mr. Beekman?" she asked.</p>
<p>"I shall make use of him just as I have of you," he said slyly.</p>

<pb n="[165]"/>
<div id="gfprice022" type="chapter">
<head rend = "center">CHAPTER XXII.</head>
<byline>INNOCENT OF THE CRIME.</byline>
<p>Poor little Viola! So far as she was concerned the deception was perfect.</p>
<p>She believed she had been arrested for the crime of which she was not guilty; and as she recalled the circumstances of her previous arrest, she had no doubt that she would be convicted.</p>
<p>The troubled night had gone by, and she sat in her little cell trying to compose her mind to the terrible truth which she believed confronted her.</p>
<p>When the man came in the morning with her breakfast, she asked him a number of questions which delighted the conspirators who listened to his report.</p>
<p>They knew positively now that their ruse had been successful. They prepared with a good heart to impose upon her to the end.</p>
<p>About ten o'clock in the morning her jailer came to her to bid her get ready to go with him to the courtroom, where the judge was waiting.</p>
<p>She was taken upstairs into a room which had been made to look somewhat judicial by a few little devices.</p>
<p>"What is this case?" the wretch impersonating the judge demanded of the man playing the part of jailer.</p>
<pb n="166 Innocent of the Crime."/>
<p>"This is the case of the girl who is charged with stealing a diamond ring from Miss Eunice Carroll," was the response.</p>
<p>"Are there any witnesses?" demanded the judge. "Three, your honor," was the response.</p>
<p>"Your honor," said a third man, who had the air of a lawyer, "I move that the trial be postponed so that a jury may be impaneled. This is an important case, and I think it proper that a jury trial be held."</p>
<p>The judge frowned. Viola clasped her hands in terror, and burst forth in a broken voice that should have softened a heart of stone:</p>
<p>"Oh, sir, I am innocent of this crime! Indeed, I never thought of touching the young lady's jewels. I am the victim of a conspiracy."</p>
<p>"There, there ! that will never do, my girl," said. the judge sternly. "If you are going to talk in that way, there will remain nothing for me to do but to impanel a jury. I had hoped you would be reasonable. But see how it is: nothing short of a trial will satisfy you. But let me tell you, my girl, that, with the evidence against you, I do not see how you can hope to escape a sentence of fifteen years, at least, unless you choose to plead guilty; in which. case I will make the sentence ten years."</p>
<p>Poor child! how was she to guess that these men
<pb n="167 Innocent of the Crime."/>
were vile impostors who were making a ridiculous travesty of the law?</p>
<p>"I am innocent, I am innocent," she sobbed.</p>
<p>"Take her away!" roared the pretended judge; "and bring that fellow here from number nineteen, so that I may sentence him to death. Let in the people who are waiting outside."</p>
<p>Viola was led away unresisting. She neither cried nor sobbed now, but maintained a terrible calm.</p>
<p>The talk of sentencing a man to death had put the finishing touch to her terror. There was neither doubt nor hope in her mind.</p>
<p>When she was well out of the way, the men in the room burst into a laugh.</p>
<p>"I call that pretty well done," said the man, who had played the part of lawyer.</p>
<p>"Why, yes," said Purdy, who had impersonated the judge, "I flatter myself we made no mistake there. The trial, however, will be harder work."</p>
<p>"What's the use of a trial?" demanded the other, who was known as Steve Bolton. "Why can't we let it go as it is? We can't very well frighten her any more, and we may spoil what we have done. Ah! there is a rap at the door. Shall I go?"</p>
<p>"I will," said Purdy. "You never can tell what is going to happen."</p>
<pb n="168 Innocent of the Crime."/>
<p>He went to the door and opened it. He started back at the sight of one whom he had every reason to fear. Howard Beekman stood there.</p>
<p>"Well," demanded Purdy, "what d'you want?"</p>
<p>"I want to talk to you about Miss Viola Redmond," was the response.</p>
<p>A dangerous gleam came into the 'eyes of the scoundrel.</p>
<p>"I don't know anything about any such person," he said.</p>
<p>"Yes, you do. Don't be afraid of me. I come with a letter from 'Miss Carroll. It will tell you that I am to be trusted."</p>
<p>He handed the letter to Purdy and waited patiently while he read it. As soon as the latter had finished it, he said suspiciously</p>
<p>"I don't know about it."</p>
<p>"Pshaw!" said Mr. Beekman impatiently. "Even if you choose to doubt me after reading the letter, I suppose it is only a matter of money. I assure you the letter is genuine. I know all about the plan to abduct and try the young lady. Nevertheless, I will pay you liberally if you will assist me as. the letter demands."</p>
<p>"It seems kind o' funny for you to be coming when the&#x2014;	
<p>"When you just took her from my house," inter-
<pb n="169 Innocent of the Crime."/>
jected Mr. Beekman. "Yes,, I understand that. But it is a fact,
nevertheless. Will you do as I wish?"</p>
<p>Purdy considered for a moment.</p>
<p>"If Iam well paid I will do anything not too dangerous," he answered.</p>
<p>"Then let us go inside," Mr. Beekman said.</p>
<p>The door closed on them, and Purdy's accomplices" came up to them.
<p>Howard Beekman shuddered inwardly at the looks of the men he was plotting with against the girl he declared he loved.</p>
<p>But the thought of Walter hardened his heart against every thought of a better course, and he said doggedly:</p>
<p>"Tell me what you have done thus far."</p>
<p>Purdy told him circumstantially, and a twinge of sharp pain darted through Howard Beekman's heart as he listened.</p>
<p>"She is horribly frightened now," Bolton said, with a coarse laugh, "and I have been saying we might just as well let it rest where it is as to take the chances of playing the rest of the game."</p>
<p>"I agree with you," said Howard.</p>
<p>"And how will you carry out the rest of the plot?" demanded Purdy.</p>
<p>I have thought of that," replied Howard. "She must have some money with her. Why not let the
<pb n="170 Innocent of the Crime."/>
man who plays the part of jailer offer to give her a chance to escape if she will give him what she has?"</p>
<p>"That is all right. Then what?"</p>
<p>"Then I will meet her outside some distance from here, and will not let her suspect that I know anything about what has happened. If she tells me, I will pretend to be frightened for her, and will offer to hide her in my house. If she agrees she will be practically out of the world, and no one will know where she is."</p>
<p>"The thing will work," said Purdy, after a few moments of reflection. "She is so frightened now that she will be ready to do anything to escape from here. It shall be done. How about your little contribution?"</p>
<p>"How much do you want?" he asked.</p>
<p>"A thousand apiece will be little enough, I think," was the answer.</p>
<p>"Here are two thousand dollars," Mr. Beekman responded, producing the money. "I will give you the remainder any time you may set."</p>
<p>"I will meet you near the lake in your grounds tonight," Purdy answered.</p>
<p>"Do not injure Miss Redmond," Howard said. "'Do not be rough in order to play your parts too well."</p>
<p>Purdy laughed.</p>
<p>"I suppose you want to have the monopoly of all that yourself. All right. You have paid for it, and she hall be treated right. You may go away with a com-
<pb n="171 Innocent of the Crime."/>
plete assurance that she will be released within two hours."</p>
<p>"I suppose she will go out by the back door?" queried Howard."</p>
<p>"Yes, that will be the best way. Good day, sir."</p>

<pb n="[172]"/>
<div id="gfprice023" type="chapter">
<head rend = "center">CHAPTER XXIII.</head>
<byline>SEEMINGLY GUILTY.</byline>
<p>Howard Beekman had lived all his life an honorable man, yet he was now plotting against the peace and happiness of the woman he professed to love.</p>
<p>He left the men with a shudder because of the evil that was in them. And yet he was worse than they. For he would go from them and pass in the world as a good man.</p>
<p>But while there was remorse in his breast for what he was doing against Viola, there was no hesitation.</p>
<p>"She will learn to love me better than ever she did Walter," he said to himself, as he walked away from the house, and found shelter from the winds in the woods not far away.</p>
<p>Purdy, in the meantime, had turned to his associates, when they were alone, and had said:</p>
<p>"Well, it doesn't matter what happens, we have made a good bit of business out of this. Now, Mr. Jailer, do your part of the play, and we shall soon be rid of the girl, and will, moreover, be some few thousands in pocket now, to say nothing of several thousands more in the way of blackmail later on."</p>
<p>Steve Bolton laughed.</p>
<pb n="173 Seemingly Guilty."/>
<p>"Oh!" he cried, "to think of the rich picking there will be in the days to come, when our good friends are safely married. We can bleed Beekman and Miss Carroll until they will wish they had never heard of us."</p>
<p>All three laughed boisterously, and the jailer went down to Viola's cell, to play his nefarious role.</p>
<p>He opened the door and looked in on her, saying with a gruff sort of sympathy:</p>
<p>"Is there anything I can do. for you, miss? I'm a father and have a daughter not far from your age, and I wouldn't like to see her in such a scrape."</p>
<p>"Oh, sir," cried Viola, "if I were guilty it would be a different matter. But I am innocent, as innocent as your own daughter!"</p>
<p>"If I only thought so," the man said, "I would risk a good deal to help you."</p>
<p>"Help me! Can any one help me now?" she cried eagerly. "Oh, if you will but aid me I will promise you&#x2014; But, alas! how can I promise anything? I have some money with me. Will you take that? Oh, if I could only escape!"</p>
<p>"Do you think I would take your money, miss? If you are innocent&#x2014;and I can't help thinking you are, somehow&#x2014;I will try to let you escape, if it costs me my place. But no, there is Bill, the turnkey; he would have to have money to get him to keep out of the way."</p>
<pb n="174 Seemingly Guilty."/>
<p>Viola drew her money from her pocket and thrust it eagerly into his hand, saying:</p>
<p>"Take it; give it to him. Oh, sir, aid me to escape and I will never
forget you! I am innocent. It was a plot to ruin me. But never mind that. Give this money to the other man, and help me, for your daughter's sake."</p>
<p>"Well, well," he said gruffly, "I can make it seem as if you had worked your way out. I will let you go. But, mind you, I wouldn't touch a cent of this money, if it was not to give it to Bill."</p>
<p>"I wish it was a large sum," Viola said.</p>
<p>"Well, it isn't much," the wretch said, shaking his head. "And Bill is such a shark! Perhaps if you had any jewels of any sort?"</p>
<p>The only jewel of value she had was the ring Walter had given her when he went away, and that she could not bear to part with.</p>
<p>"I&#x2014;I have only a&#x2014;a ring," she stammered, turning the stone inward. "It is very dear to me. Don't you think he will take the money if you tell him I will send him more?"</p>
<p>"Oh, you couldn't catch him that way, miss. He's too sharp for that. He'll take all he can now, and not trust to the future. Well, I'll tell him about it, and will let you know in a few minutes."</p>
<p>The fellow went away, and returned in a little while
<pb n="175 Seemingly Guilty."/>
with a long face to say that Bill would not think of letting her go unless she would give him something more.</p>
<p>"If I was you, miss, though I don't want to suggest anything, I would let him have that ring. You see, you can always explain how you came to let it go."</p>
<p>The color completely faded out of Viola's cheeks, and the iron of despair entered her soul. Must she give up that precious ring? And yet it must be that or the loss of freedom&#x2014;the horror of prison&#x2014;worse than death.</p>
<p>"Give it to him!" she cried, as if the words were torn from her heart.</p>
<p>The scoundrel took the ring and thrust it into his pocket.</p>
<p>"Count on getting out of here in something less than two hours," he said. "At that time there will be nobody here excepting Bill and me, and you will have no difficulty, in escaping."</p>
<p>Viola would have pleaded to be taken away at once, but the man gave her no opportunity to do so. He closed the door and went up to his companions, to whom he showed the proceeds of his shameful trickery.<p>
<p>"The ring is worth two hundred, anyhow," he said.</p>
<p>"It is worth two thousand at least," Purdy said coolly, as he held the ring in his hand.</p>
<pb n="176 Seemingly Guilty."/>
<p>"You are crazy, Sam" said Bolton. "The ring isn't worth a cent over
two hundred and fifty, anyhow."</p>
<p>"I'll bet you a cool hundred that I will sell it for two thousand," Purdy said.</p>
<p>"Done!" ejaculated Bolton.</p>
<p>"Wait here for me for a few minutes, and you shall see," said Purdy.</p>
<p>The others stared.</p>
<p>"What are you going to do?" they demanded.</p>
<p>"Take it to our friend Beekman. If he understands his business, he will pay two thousand for the engagement ring of his rival."</p>
<p>"Well, you have a long head, Purdy," said Bolton, and the other wretch nodded his head admiringly.</p>
<p>Purdy went out and presently returned with Howard, having told him of the ring.</p>
<p>"It will enable you to prove to him that she is faithless&#x2014;don't you see? It is well worth all I ask for it."</p>
<p>Howard Beekman knew that, in buying that ring, he was as guilty as the men who had tricked it from Viola. But he readily saw how it could be used to separate the lovers, and he did not hesitate.</p>
<p>"I have not the money," he said. "But I always carry my check book of my private account. Will a check do?"</p>
<p>"Certainly. And you might as well make it for three thousand, so as to include everything."</p>
<pb n="177 Seemingly Guilty."/>
<p>So the check was made out in that way, and Howard went away again, this time bearing with him the ring which was the sign of the betrothal of Walter and Viola.</p>
<p>Meanwhile the poor victim was waiting with throbbing heart for the man to come to set her free.</p>
<p>The time came at last when the man came to her with her hat and coat, together with a thick shawl, which he told her to wrap around her head, so as to avoid recognition.</p>
<p>And to be sure that she was sufficiently enfolded in the shawl, he wound it about her himself.</p>
<p>Then, with an appearance of great stealth and secrecy, he led her forth from the cell, and dragged her swiftly to the back door, which he opened and through which he dragged her so quickly that she would have had no time to look about, even if she had had the inclination.</p>
<p>But she was too glad to escape to think of studying her surroundings. She followed her leader across the open space into the woods.</p>
<p>On and on he led her until they had reached the road, where, with a hurried whisper to her to keep right on, he left her.</p>
<p>She needed no urging, but sped along the side of the road, and did not slacken her pace for an hour. Then it was the sound of jingling sleigh bells that made her
<pb n="178 Seemingly Guilty."/>
stop and look around, her bosom heaving from her exertions.</p>
<p>A sleigh was flying toward her over the smooth road, and her first impulse was to dart aside into the woods to escape observation.</p>
<p>Then something familiar in the horse, next in the driver, claimed her attention, and with a wild cry of, "Mr. Beekman!" she sank swooning in the snow.</p>

<pb n="[179]"/>
<div id="gfprice024" type="chapter">
<head rend = "center">CHAPTER XXIV.</head>
<byline>DEEP REGRETS.</byline>
<p>No one would have known the sedate, calm man of the world in the Howard Beekman who sprang from the sleigh and caught up the fainting form of Viola in his arms.</p>
<p>His intense, mad passion had transformed him. He hugged her inanimate form to his breast and rained kisses on her cold cheeks.</p>
<p>If Viola had but come to consciousness while he was so dishonorably stealing the sweets of her dewy lips she would have known her peril from him.</p>
<p>But her swoon continued until after he had placed her in the sleigh and had urged his horses on again.</p>
<p>He had wrapped her up well in the robes so that she could not suffer from the cold, and his eyes were more on her than on his flying animals.</p>
<p>He saw the color returning to the pale cheeks, and watched eagerly for the first glance of the lovely eyes when they should open.</p>
<p>He would have given all he possessed if he could have been sure that, when the long lashes lifted from the round cheeks, the brown eyes would have in them a look of love for him.</p>
<pb n="180 Deep Regrets."/>
<p>But he knew very well that they would not. And the thought made him hate Walter, as if the latter were the one at fault.</p>
<p>The brown eyes opened, and in them was a look of terror that should have won him to remorse for the misery he was causing her.</p>
<p>A cry of fear rose to her lips, but before it could escape, she recognized her companion, and cried out joyfully:</p>
<p>"Oh, Mr. Beekman! it is you. Oh, save me! save me!"</p>
<p>"I will save you; I will. Trust in me!"</p>
<p>He was bending over her, with his passion flaming in his eyes, when he recalled to himself that he must put a guard over his madness, and he added:</p>
<p>"From what must I save you, Viola? Why did you leave us?"</p>
<p>"I was stolen away," she answered, with a shudder of fear. "I&#x2014;I was&#x2014;arrested. Oh, do not turn from me because of that. But save me, for I am innocent."</p>
<p>"Arrested!" he cried, with an easily simulated laugh of incredulity. "Of what did any one accuse you? Of course you are innocent. I will take you home with me and no one shall touch you."</p>
<p>"I dare not go there," she said despairingly. "Oh, take me to the railroad station and let me go hide myself in the great city.</p>
<pb n="181 Deep Regrets."/>
<p>"No, no!" he said. "You shall go to my mother. I have wealth and can protect you. Trust in me!"</p>
<p>"You are very kind, but you do not understand," she moaned. "I was accused of stealing a diamond ring when I was in the city. I should have told you at first, but I had not the courage. I thought I would be safe with you. But they found me and took me to prison."</p>
<p>"To prison!" he exclaimed. "How, then, do you come to be free now?"</p>
<p>"I bribed the jailer to set me free, and he did so. So now I must hide in some safe place until my innocence can be proven."</p>
<p>"What safer place can there be than my house, Viola?" he asked, making a strenuous effort to hide his passion.</p>
<p>"It would be known that I was there, and I should be dragged back to prison. I think I should go mad if I were to be taken there again."</p>
<p>"Who would know that you were there?" he demanded. "Who would tell the miscreants?"</p>
<p>"The servants would tell. They would see me go into the house, and would not know that they should not tell."</p>
<p>"But what if I could get you into the house unknown to them? Would not that do?" he asked.</p>
<p>"How could you do it?" she asked, so eagerly that
<pb n="182 Deep Regrets."/>
he knew she would fall a ready victim to his nefarious plot against her happiness.</p>
<p>"I could drive about until it was dark, and then could easily smuggle you into the house. I could give you a room in the wing that is never used. You could live there for months without a suspicion on the part of anybody."</p>
<p>"It would be such a trouble to, you," she said faintly.</p> 
<p>"We care too much for you to think of the trouble," he answered, his eyes almost betraying him.</p>
<p>"But your mother?" queried Viola. "What will she think when she knows that I am accused of theft, and have been arrested?"</p>
<p>"She will think exactly as I do: that you cannot be guilty of so absurd a charge. How can you doubt our love for you, Viola?"</p>
<p>She started a little at the fervor of his tone, and cast an involuntary look into his eyes. He saw his mistake, and said hastily:</p>
<p>"My mother loves you for yourself, and I can never be grateful enough for saving my darling boy."</p>
<p>His words dissipated any faint suspicion that might have entered her brain, and she thought no more of the matter.</p>
<p>"If I were sure she would not care," Viola murmured, "I should be so grateful to you."</p>
<p>"I know she will not care," he said.</p>
<pb n="183 Deep Regrets."/>
<p>"But it will be terrible to go driving about until dark," she said, with a last faint scruple.</p>
<p>"If I had not found you, I should have driven about all day with despair and grief in my heart," he said. "I came out provided with everything to enable me to be gone all day. There is a camping stove in the sleigh, and I have food. You will not suffer."</p>
<p>"I was not thinking of myself," she said; "I was thinking only of you."</p>
<p>"I am so happy at having found you," he said, "that I shall never think of anything but that. You do not know how wretched we were when we learned that you were gone. I was out all last evening and all to-day. I would never have given up until I had found you."</p>
<p>"How good you are to me!" she murmured gratefully.</p>
<p>"It is easy to be good to you," he said. "But tell me how it happened to you. Let me know everything, so that I may know how to act."</p>
<p>So she told him the whole story just as he already knew it, and he listened to her sweet voice without paying much heed to her words.</p>
<p>It was plain, even to him, that her love for Walter occupied her whole soul. But with the infatuation of one who will see but one side, he persuaded himself that he could wean her from her love.</p>
<pb n="184 Deep Regrets."/>
<p>And now he knew that he must begin his course of deception toward her if he would hope to part her from Walter.</p>
<p>"You love him very much," he said, when she had finished.</p>
<p>"I love him with all my heart," she answered, with a vivid blush that made her face look divinely beautiful.</p>
<p>"Have you written to him about your trouble?" he asked.</p>
<p>"Yes. I have told him everything.''</p>
<p>"Then he will be sure to return home and come here to my house to seek you, I should think?" he said.</p>
<p>"I hope he will," she replied, in a low tone.</p>
<p>She was, in fact, living on that hope,<sic corr="."/>
<p>"Of course he will," he said slowly. "He certainly will if he loves you as you love him, and as you deserve to be loved. I almost wonder that he ever consented to leave you. It does not seem the act of a fervent lover."</p>
<p>"Oh! I begged him to go," she said, eager to take Walter's part.</p>
<p>"It certainly was the wisest thing to do," Mr. Beekman said spitefully. "I was only thinking that it would seem as if a true lover would think more of the one he loved than of wealth. Still, he was worldly wise in thinking of his father's wealth."</p>
<p>Viola smiled serenely.</p>
<pb n="185 Deep Regrets."/>
<p>"He neither thought of it, nor cared for it," she said. "If I had been willing, he would have defied his father. But my mother would not give her consent, and not for the world would I have come between a father and his only son. I loved him too well to cause him any pain. We could wait, however hard it might be to do so.</p>
<p>Howard Beekman bit his lip to restrain his anger at her confidence in her lover.</p>
<p>It maddened him to think that no insinuation of his could shake her trust.</p>
<p>"But I will bring her such proofs of her lover's perfidy and falseness," he thought to himself, "that she will be obliged to distrust him. Then she shall be mine."</p>

<pb n="[186]"/>
<div id="gfprice025" type="chapter">
<head rend = "center">CHAPTER XXV.</head>
<byline>SMALL, BEADY EYES.</byline>
<p>Viola was cold and miserable in body when at last it was dark enough for her to be taken to the house, so that she could be smuggled in without being observed by any of the servants.</p>
<p>Mr. Beekman had her get out of the sleigh in the road near the spot where she had met her abductors, and from there she made her way to a place near the house, and waited until he should summon her to enter.</p>
<p>He drove up to the house and had the sleigh taken to the stable at once.</p>
<p>Then he ran into the house, and was met by his mother in the hall.</p>
<p>"What news, Howard?" she cried; then caught a glimpse of his face by the hall lamp, and added eagerly: "Good news, I know. Have you found her?"</p>
<p>"Hush, mother!" he said in a whisper, as he looked cautiously about to see that no one was listening. "I have found her and brought her home. It is a strange, pitiful story. She is the victim of a terrible persecution, and must be hidden for a while. I know you will be her friend."</p>
<p>"Of course I will, Howard. Where is she?"</p>
<pb n="187 Small, Beady Eyes."/>
<p>"She is waiting out of doors until I can arrange to get her into the house unobserved. I thought of getting her into the unused wing, where she could be hidden, and could be made comfortable for a long time."</p>
<p>"What would you have me do?" she asked.</p>
<p>"Be sure that the servants are all downstairs while I call Viola in."</p>
<p>"They are downstairs now without exception," she said. "I will go down and contrive to keep them there in case any of them should seek to come up. You hasten and get the poor child in out of the cold. By the way, why not take her to my room. until we can make one of the other rooms comfortable? I will give Mary permission to go out this evening. No one else will go to my room."</p>
<p>So it was arranged. Before many minutes had passed, Viola was in the cheery room of good Mrs. Beekman. And, soon afterward, she was sobbing in very contentment on the ample bosom of the dear old lady.</p>
<p>In the meantime, Howard Beekman was uneasily pacing the floor of the sitting room, waiting for the coming of his mother.</p>
<p>He knew he must have her for an ally, and yet he knew that she would never be a party to the wickedness he contemplated.</p>
<p>"I must deceive her," he said to himself. "I must
<pb n="188 Small, Beady Eyes."/>
concoct a story which will tally with anything Viola may say, and which yet will dispose mother to take my part against Walter Hardman."</p>
<p>He could not recall the time when he had lied to his mother. It was with an inward shudder of shame that he contemplated doing it now.</p>
<p>It startled him to think how easy he found it to concoct a falsehood now. He at last hit upon the right story to tell.</p>
<p>He was ready now for his mother to come. He would tell her such a story as would make her strive hard to turn Viola against Walter.</p>
<p>He had heard the rustle of her gown on the stairs.</p>
<p>"Poor. little Viola!" said Mrs. Beekman, as she entered the room.</p>
<p>"How is she feeling?" he asked.</p>
<p>"Quite well now; and so sweetly grateful to us!"</p>
<p>"Did she tell you her story?" he asked anxiously.</p>
<p>"Only a part of it. She said she had told you everything, and that you would tell me."</p>
<p>"Yes," he said, "I will tell you her story, and then I will tell you what she does not know, and what I hope she need never know. You know she was a salesgirl in Hardman & Son's?"</p>
<p>"Yes."</p>
<p>"Well, her story is that Walter Hardman fell in
<pb n="189 Small, Beady Eyes."/>
love with her, and she with him, and that they would have been married but for the opposition of the latter's father. She says that Walter was sent to Europe, to be gone for a year, with the understanding that, if he still loved her on his return, he should wed her. She was promoted in the store, so that she was receiving a better salary, and everything was going nicely, when she was accused of the theft of a diamond ring by Eunice Carroll, who is the woman selected by Mr. Hardman as a suitable wife for his son."</p>
<p>"It looks as if there were some vile plot to ruin the poor girl, in the absence of her lover, so as to break off the engagement," said Mrs. Beekman, her keen intuition leaping at once to the truth.</p>
<p>"That is precisely what it did seem like," he said. "And when I first knew the circumstances I felt like sending a cable message to Paris to Walter Hardman. For, though I loved her, mother, I felt that it was my duty to her to do what would make her happiest."</p>
<p>"That was like you, my noble boy!" Mrs. Beekman said proudly. "Why did you not do it?"</p>
<p>He winced at her undeserved praise of him.</p>
<p>"I would have done it, but an interview with Mrs. Redmond, who is a lovely woman, and just what you, would suppose Viola's mother to be&#x2014;an inteiview with her induced me to go to the detective who had arrested Viola for the theft."</p>
<pb n="190 Small, Beady Eyes."/>
<p>"Arrested! Poor child!" interrupted Mrs. Beekman.</p>
<p>"He told me everything as soon as he learned what my relation to Viola was; he told me he had connived at her escape on the way to the prison because it had been so clear to him that she was innocent."</p>
<p>"Who could doubt it?" ejaculated Mrs. Beekman.</p>
<p>"He told me, too," Howard went on, turning away so that his mother could not by any possibility read his deceit in his face, "that he had carefully investigated the affair, and that there was no doubt in his mind that the whole affair was a terrible plot to ruin Viola and make it impossible for her to hope to be Walter's wife."</p>
<p>"Scoundrels!" cried Mrs. Beekman.</p>
<p>"And the worst of it was," Howard went on implacably, for there was no retreat for him, "that Walter was a party to the infamy."</p>
<p>"Incredible!" murmured his mother, in a tone of horror.</p>
<p>"So I said," her son went on. "But the detective told me such things that I could no longer doubt."</p>
<p>"He is unfit to live," cried the indignant old lady. "Any one who would plot against the happiness of that girl deserves the worst punishment that can be inflicted."</p>
<p>"The worst that can happen to him," he said, in a
<pb n="191 Small, Beady Eyes."/>
hollow voice, "is that he should lose even the chance of making her his wife."</p>
<p>"Never, never must he wed that lovely creature, Howard," his mother said. "I was regretting that she was lost to you. But now I feel that she must not be lost to you. We must save her from that scoundrel."</p>
<p>"You will help me then to win her?" he said eagerly.</p>
<p>"I will, indeed, both for your sake and for hers?" was the ready response.</p>
<p>"But what shall we do?" he asked. "I would not have the courage to go to her with such a story."</p>
<p>"Of course not. She is far too loyal a little soul to believe you. There is but one thing to do, painful as it will be, and that is to bring his perfidy home to her in such a way that she cannot doubt. It will be a terrible blow to her, but it is better that she should suffer a little to escape a worse blow."</p>
<p>"I will work in harmony with the detective, then," he said
eagerly. "And when something occurs which will make it impossible for
her to doubt, you will present it to her. If I were to do it, she might not turn to me afterward. I would like her to have only pleasant thoughts connected with me."</p>
<p>"I understand, Howard. It shall be my task to open her eyes to the truth."</p>
<p>He had gained his point, and was filled with tri-
<pb n="192 Small, Beady Eyes."/>
umph, despite the sense of shame at his own double dealing.</p>
<p>Perhaps had he looked out of the window at that moment there would have been still another sensation mingled with his triumph; for glaring in on the mother and son was a pair of small, beady eyes, set in a gaunt, sallow face.</p>
<p>"The fool!" muttered Peter Harriem, as he turned away with an evil grimace. "He thinks he plots for himself, but he is only my tool!"</p>

<pb n="[193]"/>
<div id="gfprice026" type="chapter">
<head rend = "center">CHAPTER XXVI.</head>
<byline>"WHY PERMIT IT?"</byline>
<p>What, meanwhile, was Walter doing? Did he, indeed, think so little of his love that he felt no qualms about leaving her alone and unprotected?</p>
<p>In truth it had not occurred to him to fear anything definite for her, but he was too much in love not to feel on leaving her as if his heart had been torn from his breast.</p>
<p>On the way over on the steamer he was tortured by all sorts of vague fears of what might happen to him or to Viola during the long year that was to intervene.</p>
<p>But when he reached Paris, he plunged into his work as if determined to keep his mind so occupied that there would be no room for repining.</p>
<p>Then came Viola's first letters, in which she told him enthusiastically of her promotion.</p>
<p>"Father is very good, after all," Walter murmured. "I can see that he intends to take care of her."</p>
<p>He soon discovered that there was really little work for him to do in the Paris agency, and he also discovered that he was gifted with no little business ability.</p>
<p>This led him to insist upon making a thorough ac-
<pb n="194 Why Permit It?"/>
quaintance with every detail of the business; and ended in his starting on a tour of the several agencies in other parts of Europe.</p>
<p>He had left orders that his letters should be forwarded to him; but, owing to some mistake, they were kept at the Paris office.</p>
<p>This fact hastened his return, and he reached Paris again in about two weeks, to find four letters in the precious handwriting which he had learned to know.</p>
<p>He arranged the letters in the order of their arrival, as shown by the postmark, and sat down in his cozy parlor to enjoy the feast which he anticipated.</p>
<p>One by one the letters were read and let fall, the first look of horror growing into an expression of fierce, furious determination.</p>
<p>He muttered angry words as he read, but he hardly stirred from his seat until every word was read and reread.</p>
<p>"It is a plot of that wretch Harriem," he murmured at last. "I can see it. And it would seem as if Eunice must be a party to it. Can it be that father has had a hand in it? Oh, I must not believe that. It would be too infamous for belief."</p>
<p>He leaped up and paced the floor.</p>
<p>Fierce and wrathful as he was, he yet had the good sense to hold his anger in check while he studied the situation.</p>
<pb n="195 Why Permit It?"/>
<p>"Heaven bless those good people who have befriended her!" he murmured. "I will take the first steamer home. But, lest my father may have had a hand in the affair, I will pretend to the agent that I am going back on my tour of the other agencies so that he will not notify father that I am on my way home."</p>
<p>"I will see Viola. I will cheer her up, and will persuade her to marry me. Then let father do what he will. If he wishes to turn me adrift to earn my own living, I know now that I can do it."</p>
<p>He carried out his plan exatly as he had laid out, and within three days was on his way to the United States.</p>
<p>He hesitated for a while as to whether he should go to see Mrs. Redmond before going up to Viola. He decided to see Viola first.</p>
<p>When the steamer drew up to the pier, he was one of the first to step on the gangplank and hasten ashore.</p>
<p>He had not taken passage under his own name, and he now drew his coat collar up as if he felt the cold, and pulled his soft hat down so as to be unrecognizable to any acquaintance chancing to be there.</p>
<p>He never thought of glancing around on the sea of faces upturned toward the steamer to watch the passengers come down, and probably it would not have mattered if he had.</p>
<p>But in truth there was a young man in the crowd
<pb n="196 Why Permit It?"/>
who was a clerk in the store of Hardman & Son, and who knew Walter well by sight.</p>
<p>He eyed Walter eagerly and gave a start of joy at recognizing him.</p>
<p>He stole quietly behind him until he saw him take a taxicab and give the direction:</p>
<p>"Grand Central Depot."</p>
<p>Then the young man darted away and did not cease running until he had climbed the stairs of the Sixth Avenue elevated station nearest the pier.</p>
<p>In less than half an hour he stood before the desk of Peter Harriem, out of breath, but with his face filled with triumph.</p>
<p>"Well?" queried Peter, with an eagerness he could not conceal.</p>
<p>"Mr. Walter landed half an hour ago."</p>
<p>The beady eyes twinkled. The cold voice murmured:</p>
<p>"Where did he go?"</p>
<p>"He took a taxicab to the Grand Central Depot."</p>
<p>"That will do. You have earned your promotion. I will go see Mr. Hardman, and tell him how well you have done. He will have a good joke on Mr. Walter."</p>
<p>The young man went away with a smile, muttering:</p>
<p>"All very well, sneaking Peter! you don't fool me like that. But it's none of my business as long as I get my promotion."</p>
<pb n="197 Why Permit It?"/>
<p>Walter, meanwhile, had reached the station, and had learned that he would have to wait an hour before he could get a train that would take him to the station for the Beekman place.</p>
<p>Peter glided out of the store and for once was forced to take a taxicab.</p>
<p>He was in a great hurry to reach the house of Eunice Carroll, and trembled with anxiety lest she should not be in when he reached the house.</p>
<p>He paid the chauffeur his charge, though he made a wry face over doing it, and then rang the bell.</p>
<p>"Tell Miss Carroll," he said, "that Mr. Peter Harriem wishes to see
her on very important business."</p>
<p>The man remembered the sallow, mean face of the visitor, and recalled that his mistress had already seen him before, so he ushered Peter into the reception room and went away.</p>
<p>Eunice was down with him in so short a time as to convey to him the fact that she had been waiting anxiously for some news.</p>
<p>"What has happened?" she demanded breathlessly.</p>
<p>"Walter is home, and is on his way to see Viola."</p>
<p>"Why do you permit it?" she demanded furiously.</p>
<p>"I trusted to you to prevent any such thing happening."</p>
<p>"I am doing this in my own way," Peter said, with, minous glitter in his little eyes. "Please go at
<pb n="198 Why Permit It?"/>
once and write a letter to Howard Beekman, telling him that Walter will be at his house immediately after the receipt by him of the letter."</p>
<p>"Will you be sure to get it to him on time?" she demanded.</p>
<p>"I am going to take it myself," he answered.</p>
<p>"Supposing Walter should see you? Everything would then be spoiled."</p>
<p>"Walter will not see me," he said, in a low tone. "Do not waste time."</p>
<p>Eunice went into another room and wrote a hasty note, telling Howard what Peter had told her to. She returned with it to Peter, and gave it to him.</p>
<p>He placed it in his pocket, after swiftly glancing over it, and glided away, saying:</p>
<p>"He could not catch a train that will take him there before me."</p>
<p>And when he was outside of the house he said, as he darted swiftly up the street:</p>
<p>"Soon I shall have Walter and Howard at each other's throats. Then I shall step in and carry off the prize."</p>

<pb n="[199]"/>
<div id="gfprice027" type="chapter">
<head rend = "center">CHAPTER XXVII.</head>
<byline>"STRIKE HIM DOWN."</byline>
<p>It was an incredible joy to the sly, subtle nature of Peter Harriem that he was able to carry out his plot in the involved, secret manner in which he did.</p>
<p>It seemed to him that he could not fail of his purpose as long as he controlled his puppets.</p>
<p>He rubbed his hands with an infinite relish as he walked away from the house of Eunice Carroll.</p>
<p>His hurry was over now. He had only been afraid he would not find her at home, and would have to seek for her elsewhere. It was another reason for his secret satisfaction that he had known at once that Walter would not be able to catch a train right away to take him up the river.</p>
<p>He had been up there so often that he had learned the trains by heart, and had known the instant the young man had told him of Walter's action, that he would have to cool his impatience in the station.</p>
<p>"How they all have to do just what the despised Peter tells them,"
he muttered. "Even Walter, who comes from Europe and thinks to have his own will of things, has to be my creature. Aha! he little thinks I
shall be on the same train with him, with my web
<pb n="200 Strike Him Down"/>
woven to catch the girl he loves, but who must be mine."</p>
<p>He glided stealthily into the station and peered around it until his keen eyes fell upon Walter, pacing uneasily up and down at one end.</p>
<p>"He can't wait until he sees her," he murmured. "Well, he will have to learn patience. I wonder why I hate him so. I always did hate him. I think it is because he has always had what I never did have. Curse him for his good looks and his insolence! He always despised me, but in the end I shall triumph."</p>
<p>He sneaked stealthily up to the office and bought his ticket, always keeping his eye on Walter, to be sure that he was not recognized.</p>
<p>When the gates were opened to let the passengers through into the cars, he waited until Walter had passed through and then followed him.</p>
<p>He could not help noticing the assured, strong step of the young man whom he hated, and whose affianced wife he was trying to steal, but he did not lay it to the right cause.</p>
<p>"He is so sure of getting her," he said to himself, with'' a sneer of hate and malice. "He walks as if he owned the whole earth."</p>
<p>He could not know how Walter had changed in the short time of his absence, for he had never understood
<pb n="201 Strike Him Down"/>
the qualities that lay dormant in him, wanting but little to bring them forth.</p>
<p>He took the car behind Walter, and during the ride up the river kept watching him with a growing hate.</p>
<p>When the cars stopped at the station he was waiting on the step, and was away in the darkening day before Walter was off the car.</p>
<p>He knew there was no need for him to follow Walter now.</p>
<p>Viola was the loadstone that attracted them both, and Walter would now follow him.</p>
<p>Peter had gone over the road so many times that it was very familiar to him, and yet he paused once in his rapid, gliding run and looked about him.</p>
<p>It was a spot where the road ran by the side of a precipice, down the side of which grew a mass of tangled brush, and at the bottom of which was a darksome pool.</p>
<p>Peter could see nothing of this now, for it was already too dark for that, but he knew how it was, and as he paused there he muttered:</p>
<p>"I could wait here; I could leap upon him from behind and strike him down. Then I could throw him over there, and no one would, ever know. Curse him! how I would like to do it. But no, he can serve me best alive, now."</p>
<p>It was a murderer's thought and impulse, and the
<pb n="202 Strike Him Down"/>
picture of the handsome young man lying dead haunted him. He gloated over the mere idea of it.</p>
<p>He accelerated his pace and ran on until he reached the house of Howard Beekman.</p>
<p>It was lighted up in its usual cheery fashion, and Peter had much ado to steal up to the front door and reach the bell without exposing himself to the flood of light that streamed from the windows.</p>
<p>Not that it would have mattered at all if he had exposed himself, but he loved stealth and secrecy for its own sake.</p>
<p>He kept as much as possible in the shadow when he heard the step of the servant in the hall, and when she opened the door he said:</p>
<p>"Tell Mr. Beekman there is a man to see him on important business. I won't come in."</p>
<p>He reached out and pulled the door shut in her face, much to her alarm and amazement, but his action had the desired effect. She ran quickly to her master, and said:</p>
<p>"There is a strange man at the door to see you, sir. He wouldn't come in. He said important business."</p>
<p>Howard Beekman lived a life of inward but intense excitement in those days. He fancied all sorts of things, and waited for something to happen which would guide him to his right course.</p>
<p>He started up at the girl's words, and ran to the
<pb n="203 Strike Him Down"/>
door, which he snatched open impetuously. He peered out, and saw the dimly outlined figure of a man in the vestibule.</p>
<p>"Who are you&#x2014;what do you want?" he demanded.</p>
<p>"A letter from Miss Carroll," said Peter. "Read it quickly. There is no time to lose."</p>
<p>Howard snatched the letter from the long fingers, and forgot the man in his interest in the letter.</p>
<p>"Come in!" he said mechanically, as he retreated toward the hall lamp.</p>
<p>"No. Be quick. The man mentioned in the letter is on his way here now," said Peter, pulling the door shut, and gliding out to the porch, that he might watch for the coming of Walter.</p>
<p>"He is sure to be late," he muttered. "He would not come without asking directions, and that would take time."</p>
<p>The door of the house opened, and Howard stepped out, saying huskily:</p>
<p>"The letter says you are to be trusted."</p>
<p>"I am," said Peter, sinking lower into his upturned coat collar.</p>
<p>"You know everything?" asked Howard.</p>
<p>"Everything. Walter Hardman is not five minutes away from here."</p>
<p>"She&#x2014;she advises nothing," said Howard, his own
<pb n="204 Strike Him Down"/>
wits hard at work to no purpose in the face of the peril menacing him.</p>
<p>"She told me to tell you that you must make him think the young lady has gone."</p>
<p>"That part is plain. I had determined on that. What else?"</p>
<p>"Then you must go to her mother and persuade her not to reveal the truth.</p>
<p>"I have already persuaded her of that. She is my ally."</p>
<p>"Ah, is it so? Then go to her, and if you can get her to give Walter back his engagement ring, telling him that the girl wishes to break the engagement, you will be all right."</p>
<p>"What do you know of the ring?" demanded Howard, startled.</p>
<p>"Miss Carroll knows everything that happened. Will you do what I say?"</p>
<p>"Who are you?" demanded Howard, with sudden suspicion.</p>
<p>"What does it matter? I am bound to do Miss Carroll a service. She told me the facts and asked my advice. I told her what I have told you."</p>
<p>"But why should she&#x2014;"
<p>Peter laid his hand on the other's arm and hushed him.</p>
<p>"Hark! he is coming. I must get out of the way.
<pb n="205 Strike Him Down"/>
I have given you all the advice I have to give.	You must use your own judgment about taking it. You will have to play your part well. Good night!"</p>
<p>He escaped down the steps, and glided away into the shrubbery, where he crouched. Howard went in, after a moment of hesitation, and softly closed the door.</p>
<p>He stood for a moment in the hall in indecision. What should he do before meeting Walter! What sort of man would he turn out to be? Who was the messenger?</p>
<p>Then he darted up the stairs and went into his mother's little parlor, where the old lady spent a great deal of her time, because later in the evening Viola would come down and sit with her.</p>
<p>"Mother," he said abruptly, "I must have a few words with you."</p>
<p>"Something has happened," she said quickly. "Tell me what it is."</p>
<p>"Walter Hardman has come home."</p>
<p>"Ah, he will see Viola, then," she said at once.</p>
<p>"He is on his way here now. I have received word from my detective that he is coming. He will be here any minute. There is his ring now. What shall we do?"</p>
<p>"He must not see Viola," Mrs. Beekman said, with
<pb n="206 Strike Him Down"/>
decision. "The girl would believe in him, and go with him."</p>
<p>"That is so," said Howard eagerly. "But what can we do? I feel that he would not believe me if I were to tell him she was not here."</p>
<p>"He might not believe you," she said. "He is sure to believe me."</p>
<p>"You, mother! would you tell him a falsehood?" he demanded joyously.</p>
<p>He had hoped for it, but had not believed that anything would persuade his mother to say what was not true.</p>
<p>She turned to him and placed her hand upon his arm; her sweet face was troubled but firm.</p>
<p>"Howard," she said, "I must save Viola at any cost. If he discovers that she is here, he will find means to see her. He has not returned from Europe to see her unless he is terribly infatuated. I will mislead him. Leave it to me."</p>
<p>"But I should be present at the interview," he said.</p>
<p>He did not dare to remain away, although, he would have been glad to escape meeting Walter.</p>
<p>"Yes," she replied, "you should be present. We will greet him courteously and give him a welcome as if we did not suspect him in any way. We will even urge him to remain overnight. It will be easy to keep
<pb n="207 Strike Him Down"/>
Viola in the wing. She will never know that he has been here, and he will have any possible suspicion set at rest."</p>
<p>"What would I do without you, mother? Here comes Mary to tell us he is here!"</p>

<pb n="[208]"/>
<div id="gfprice028" type="chapter">
<head rend = "center">CHAPTER XXVIII.</head>
<byline>GOOD INTENTIONS</byline>
<p>Walter walked into the sitting room of the Beekman house, his whole being vibrating with hope.</p>
<p>He had asked for Mr. or Mrs. Beekman rather than for Viola, because he had wished to be courteous. He knew she was a sort of companion to Mrs. Beekman, and he thought it would be better to ask to see either the old lady or her son.</p>
<p>He could not sit down restfully, for his eagerness to see his darling was too great to permit of repose of manner.</p>
<p>All the way to the house he had dwelt on her image, and had reveled in the thought of how he would sweep away every scruple she had against a hasty and secret marriage.</p>
<p>He thought how yielding he had been before going away, and smiled happily as he realized how he had changed.</p>
<p>"Perhaps," he murmured, "she did not think me manly enough. She may have feared to trust herself to me, in spite of her great love. For I know she did love me."</p>
<p>He paused in front of the blazing fire on the hearth
<pb n="209 Good Intentions."/>
and listened eagerly for some sound to indicate the coming of some one<sic corr="someone"/></p>
