<p>"How good these kind friends have been to her!" he immured. "I must think of some way of proving my gratitude. I hear the rustle of a dress."</p>
<p>He stopped and turned toward the door, his handsome face lighted up by a smile of expectation Mrs. Beekman entered the room.</p>
<p>Walter had no doubt it was she the moment his eyes fell upon her, for Viola had described her to him in more than one letter.</p>
<p>"I know this is Mrs. Beekman," he said, stepping forward quickly and putting out his hand. "My name is Walter Hardman. Viola has told you of me, I know."</p>
<p>Mrs. Beekman took the outstretched hand and pressed it cordially.</p>
<p>She studied the handsome face earnestly. She told herself instantly that no scoundrel ever had eyes like his.</p>
<p>She felt in her heart that he was worthy of Viola; and yet there was the direct evidence given her son that Walter was base and unworthy.</p>
<p>"Yes," she said, "Viola has spoken of you many times. I did not know you were home. Have you seen Viola?"</p>
<p>"No," he replied, with a bright, eager smile. "I have not met her yet. I asked for you first."</p>
<pb n="210 Good Intentions."/>
<p>Howard Beekman entered the room at this point, and Mrs. Beekman turned and introduced him to Walter.</p>
<p>"You have heard Viola speak of Mr. Hardman, I am sure, Howard," she said.</p>
<p>"Why, no, mother," he said, "I must confess that I never have. But if Mr. Hardman is a friend of hers he is very welcome here. Miss Redmond saved my little boy from a horrible death, Mr. Hardman. Have you seen her lately, by the way?"</p>
<p>Walter turned from Howard to his mother, a strange, perplexed expression creeping into his eyes.</p>
<p>"Seen her lately!" he repeated. "I have just returned from Europe, and came here at once. I am impatient to see her. She&#x2014;is to be my wife, you know."</p>
<p>"Your wife!" exclaimed Howard, looking at his mother with pretended amazement. "Did she ever tell you so, mother?" he asked.</p>
<p>Walter stared with growing anxiety. A vague fear was forming in his heart, though he could not give it expression.</p>
<p>Mrs. Beekman shook her head at the question. It happened that she could truthfully answer:</p>
<p>"No, she never spoke of such a thing while she was here. But it does not matter," she added, with a pleasant smile. "If you and she are to be married, I can
<pb n="211 Good Intentions."/>
most heartily congratulate you, for a sweeter girl I never met. I hope you hear good news of her?"</p>
<p>"Good news of her!" repeated Walter slowly.</p>
<p>"Yes," replied Mrs. Beekman. "We have worried greatly over her since she left us so suddenly."</p>
<p>"Left you suddenly!" gasped Walter. "What do you mean?"</p>
<p>"Perhaps you did not know," said Howard. "Of course she could not have written to you in time for you to get the letter before leaving the other side."</p>
<p>"In mercy," cried Walter, "will you tell me what you mean? I thought Viola was here. Is she not here? If not, where then?"</p>
<p>"Then you do not know!" cried Mrs. Beekman. "Oh! I hope nothing has happened to her. She left us before Christmas."</p>
<p>"Left you before Christmas!" cried Walter. "Where did she go? Why did she go?"</p>
<p>Mrs. Beekman shook her head.</p>
<p>"She left us suddenly and without any warning. She did not even say good-by to us."</p>
<p>"Merciful Heaven!" cried Walter. "Her enemies have found her!"</p>
<p>"Her enemies!" said Mrs. Beekman and her son together. "What enemies had she?"</p>
<p>"Ah! I remember," said Walter, his mind working quickly. "She did not tell you her sad story I must
<pb n="212 Good Intentions."/>
not take time to repeat it all. She was accused of theft. You must know that she could not be guilty. Tell me when she went away, and all
about it."</p>
<p>"It was one afternoon," Howard said. "She had been playing with my little boy in the snow. When he was tired of the play he came into the house, and she went out to a little lake, apparently to skate. When I went out a little later, she was gone, and the footprints showed that she had been joined by some man, and had walked with him to the road, where she got into a sleigh with him. We have not seen her since."</p>
<p>"And you did nothing about it?" cried Walter indignantly.</p>
<p>"Did nothing!" exclaimed Mrs. Beekman. "My son jumped into a sleigh and tried to find her. He was not home until after midnight that night. And the next day he was out all day long. We gave the alarm and had the whole country searched. Then when we could not find her we decided that she must have intended to leave us."</p>
<p>"Did the footprints tell you nothing?" Walter asked, a prey to agony, but maintaining his composure with marvelous strength of will.</p>
<p>"They only told us that she must have gone willingly, for there was no evidence of a struggle, and it was plain that she had gone for a long distance by the side of her companion."</p>
<pb n="213 Good Intentions."/>
<p>Walter placed his hands to his temples as if to subdue the throbbing, that he might think more clearly.</p>
<p>"What can have happened to her?" he murmured. "But I shall discover soon. And if harm has come to her, woe to them who have done it! My darling! my darling!"</p>
<p>An expression of pain and doubt crept into the sweet face of Mrs. Beekman. It was impossible to look at and hear Walter without believing in him.</p>
<p>Howard saw the expression, and feared that his mother would be carried away by her feelings, and betray the truth to his rival.</p>
<p>It was not difficult for him to comprehend that if Walter were to see Viola, all hope for him would be gone.</p>
<p>He saw in Walter just such a man as any woman might love, and who, if loved, would prove irresistible.</p>
<p>"We shall only be too glad to aid you in any way in our power, Mr. Hardman," he said. "If we had doubted that she was in safety, we would never have ceased our efforts to find her. I cannot think why she did not tell us her story. We were too fond of her to have believed such a thing of her."</p>
<p>"The shame of the accusation and of her arrest had stayed her tongue," said Walter absently.</p>
<p>He was settling in his mind the proper course of action. One thing showed itself clearly to him. He
<pb n="214 Good Intentions."/>
must see his father and Peter Harriem. Then there was Eunice.</p>
<p>"I must return to the city," he said, with decision. "You will have supper first," Mrs. Beekman said. "I could not eat with this terrible load on my heart," he answered. "You will forgive me if I go at once."</p>
<p>He took his hat and was going out of the room, when Mrs. Beekman stopped him, saying:</p>
<p>"There is no train for you to take, Mr. Hardman. Eat supper with us, and we will send you down to the first train. Besides, we may, by talking it over, see some way by which we can help you. You may depend upon us."</p>
<p>Howard Beekman viewed his mother with uneasiness. He saw that she had a design in trying to keep Walter.</p>
<p>"I feel as if I must be doing something," Walter answered, his face showing the agony he felt.</p>
<p>"I can understand the feeling," Mrs. Beekman said kindly, as she placed her hand on his arm in a motherly way. "If you love Viola truly, the loss of her would be madness."</p>
<p>"If I love her truly!" Walter said, with vehement passion. "I love her better than life. I would give up all else for her. Love her! Words are too weak. I shall go mad if I do not do something. But, no, I will not give way. I must be calm in order that I
<pb n="215 Good Intentions."/>
may hunt down her persecutors. And that I will do, though my own father be one of them."</p>
<p>"Your father!" cried Mrs. Beekman. "Do you suspect him?"</p>
<p>Howard saw that his mother was rapidly being won by the candor and frank honesty of the young man; he trembled lest she should tell the truth and snatch Viola from his power.</p>
<p>"I have reason to suspect everybody," Walter said. "Viola and I were in love. My father wished me to marry a wealthier girl, and I refused. He persuaded me to consent to a year's probation with the understanding that I was to marry Viola if I still wished at the end of a year. As if one who had ever known her would forget her or love her less."</p>
<p>"And if you find her now, then," Mrs. Beekman said eagerly, "you would wish to make her your wife?"</p>
<p>"I would insist upon it in order that I might secure her from the machinations of her enemies," he answered.</p>
<p>"You must remain to supper," Mrs. Beekman said, in a decided tone. "I tell you we can help you. You might as well remain, for you could not get a train by going. Excuse me and I will order supper at once. Howard, will you come with me and give your orders yourself about the carriage. You will lose nothing by remaining, Mr. Hardman."</p>

<pb n="[216]"/>
<div id="gfprice029" type="chapter">
<head rend = "center">CHAPTER XXIX.</head>
<byline>"DON'T MARRY VIOLA."</byline>
<p>"Howard," said Mrs. Beekman, in a tone of firm conviction, the moment they were alone, "I am sure that young man is the soul of honor. There has been a strange mistake."</p>
<p>"He is a good actor, rather," Howard said sullenly, his brain busy with a determination to discover some way to keep his mother from telling Walter the truth.</p>
<p>"He is not acting, Howard," she responded firmly. "I know truth when I see it, and I purpose to bring those two lovers together. Think of the happiness of Viola in meeting him."</p>
<p>Howard closed his teeth to keep back a fierce imprecation.</p>
<p>"I do not believe in him," he said. "My information cannot be wrong. The truth probably is that he has learned through one of his agents that Viola is here, and has hurried over from Europe to lay claim to her."</p>
<p>"There was truth in his tones, my son, when he told me he loved her, and would make her his wife. Take care that you do not blind yourself with your own passion. Surely you would not do anything remotely dishonorable."</p>
<pb n="217 Don't Marry Viola."/>
<p>"Is it dishonorable," he demanded, "to save her from a scoundrel? Because I love her, I am more careful than you."</p>
<p>"You have nothing to fear," his mother said gravely. "I tell you he is a highminded, noble young man. My intuition cannot be false."</p>
<p>A sudden thought came to Howard in the midst of his despair.</p>
<p>"You may be right, mother," he said. "Give me time to think it over. You know I am not as quick as you. Let me consider it, and if I have no better plan, I will accede to yours."</p>
<p>"Spoken like my son!" said the good old lady, stroking his cheek.</p>
<p>He pushed her hand away impatiently and strode out of the room, saying:</p>
<p>"I will go order the carriage. I must get into the fresh air to think."</p>
<p>"Poor Howard!" murmured the old lady compassionately. "He loves her so madly that it is hard for him to give her up. But his own noble nature will conquer. When he sees, as I do, that Walter Hardman is worthy of Viola, he will be quick to place her hand in his."</p>
<p>But there was little prospect of such a change in the spirit of Howard Beekman.</p>
<p>He threw on his hat and coat, and hastened out of
<pb n="218 Don't Marry Viola."/>
the house. But instead of going out to the stable, he sought the front of the house, where he crept along, whistling softly.</p>
<p>Presently his whistle was answered, and he was joined in the shadow
of one of the trees by the shivering form of Peter Harriem.</p>
<p>"Ugh! how cold it is!" chattered Peter. "What does he say?"</p>
<p>"What he says is nothing. He has persuaded my mother that he is true and honorable, and unless I can think of some excuse to prevent it, she will tell him that Viola is in the house, and this very evening they will be united. Help me out of the difficulty if your brains are better than mine."</p>
<p>Anger warmed the thin body of the sly wretch. It seemed as if there could be no way to prevent the catastrophe.</p>
<p>"It must not be," he hissed. "He must not marry her."</p>
<p>He spoke with such passion that Howard's first suspicions of him were reawakened.</p>
<p>"How does it concern you so closely?" he asked sharply.</p>
<p>"Concern me!" cried Peter, recollecting himself suddenly. "It does not concern me directly, but it concerns Miss Carroll, and if I do not serve her well, she
<pb n="219 Don't Marry Viola."/>
has it in her power to ruin me forever. I have good reason to work strenuously for her."</p>
<p>"Now I can comprehend your earnestness," Howard said. "Concoct some scheme, then, that will keep my mother from telling him."</p>
<p>"First tell me everything you can remember," said Peter.</p>
<p>Howard went over the conversation that had taken place in the house, and Peter asked questions until he knew all that had been said, and was able to form an idea of the situation.</p>
<p>He saw just how easy it would be for Walter to learn the whole truth now, with Mrs. Beekman so kindly disposed toward him.</p>
<p>Swiftly into his brain flashed again that picture of Walter struck from behind and falling over the cliff.</p>
<p>"If Walter Hardman were only dead," he whispered.</p>
<p>Howard shuddered and drew away. But Peter followed him closely, and murmured:</p>
<p>"We may put off the revelation by your mother. But a woman once disposed as she is will soon ferret out the truth. And then you will certainly lose your love."</p>
<p>"But he will not die because we wish it," Howard gasped.</p>
<pb n="220 Don't Marry Viola."/>
<p>"Who knows?" hissed the serpentlike voice of Peter. "Other men have died conveniently."</p>
<p>"Are you talking of murder?" panted Howard, the idea becoming less abhorrent to him as he thought more of it.</p>
<p>"Murder! what an ugly word! It would not be murder if he fell over that cliff on the way to the station."</p>
<p>"No, but how can we expect him to fall there?" asked Howard, in a low voice.</p>
<p>"Suppose you walked with him to the station," said Peter, in his most insinuating tones, "and he should go too near the edge, and trip?"</p>
<p>"I could not make him do it," Howard said.</p>
<p>"But you would not mind having him fall, I suppose?" Peter sneered.</p>
<p>"No. I would be glad if he were dead, but I could not do it. I would forever see his face before me. I wish him dead, but I will not kill him."</p>
<p>In the silence of his heart, Peter cursed his companion. He had hoped to persuade him to do the foul deed which would rid him of a stumblingblock, and would put the curse of Cain on Howard himself.</p>
<p>If it had been a question of facing Walter or any one much less able to defend himself, Peter would never have considered the matter for a moment. As it was, he whispered:</p>
<pb n="221 Don't Marry Viola."/>
<p>"Will you say to your mother that if she will postpone her revelation until you can have time to see Viola's mother, you will consent?"</p>
<p>"Of course I will say that. But what is your purpose?"</p>
<p>"You shall see. Then will you persuade Walter to walk to the station?"</p>
<p>"Yes. I will say that the horses are sick."</p>
<p>"That will do. Then you must give him a glass of wine before he leaves, and into the wine you must put a few drops of some drug. Have you such a thing in the house?"</p>
<p>Howard shuddered.</p>
<p>"Yes; in the medicine closet are all such things."</p>
<p>"And you will do as I say?" asked Peter, his beady eyes glittering in the dark, as he realized how easily Howard was falling into the trap set for him.</p>
<p>"I will do it," Howard said.</p>
<p>"And then you have only to accompany him as far as the entrance to the part of the road that leads by the cliff. By that time he will begin to feel the effects of the drug, and will&#x2014;well, never mind the rest."</p>
<p>"But&#x2014;but&#x2014;" stammered Howard shudderingly.</p>
<p>"Pshaw!" said Peter. "It seems to me you have no reason to utter a word of doubt. Your part is easy. You would not have me, who has no interest in the
<pb n="222 Don't Marry Viola."/>
matter, risk my life in an encounter with a man so much superior in strength?"</p>
<p>"But to drug him and&#x2014;and&#x2014;"</p>
<p>Peter sneered.</p>
"Well, I have advised you. You may do as you please. I can return to Miss Carroll and assure her that I did all I could. I am sure I have no wish to do the thing."
<p>"She&#x2014;she wishes it, I suppose," said Howard, trying to palter with himself by trying to believe that the crime would be of her instigation and not his.</p>
<p>Peter emitted his noiseless chuckle.</p>
<p>"Of course she wishes it. She has told me a thousand times that she would rather see him dead than married to Viola. Oh, yes, she as good as advised it herself."</p>
<p>"I don't see how any harm could come to me," Howard murmured.</p>
<p>"Of course not."</p>
<p>"I would make her a better husband than he would," said Howard, with a miserable attempt to justify the horror.</p>
<p>"As for that," sneered the subtle demon at his side, "there can be no guessing. So you will do as you say? Well, I shall be in the neighborhood and see that no harm befalls your guest."</p>
<pb n="223 Don't Marry Viola."/>
<p>"You will freeze to death," said Howard, his teeth chattering.</p>
<p>"Is there any building around here where I could hide?" asked Peter.</p>
<p>"Here is the key to the tool house. It is out of the way, and is warmer than almost any other of the buildings."</p>
<p>"Bring me out something to eat if you can, will you?"</p>
<p>"Yes, I will bring you out something to eat, and to drink, too," Howard said.</p>
<p>"All right. I'll go in there and wait for you. I suppose you won't change your mind after all?"</p>
<p>"No, I do not care what becomes of him; it will not be my doing."</p>

<pb n="[224]"/>
<div id="gfprice030" type="chapter">
<head rend = "center">CHAPTER XXX.</head>
<byline>WAS HE WORTHY OF HER?</byline>
<p>During Howard's absence from the house, his mother had thought more carefully about Walter, and was thoroughly convinced that he was all he seemed to be.</p>
<p>She felt that it would be wronging Viola not to give her the opportunity to see her lover, from whom she had been separated, and she had quite decided that she should see him when Howard came into the room.</p>
<p>She glanced up at him, and was startled by the deathly pallor of his face.</p>
<p>Immediately she jumped to a conclusion which expressed itself in her pitying thought:</p>
<p>"He loves her so madly that it is like death to him to give her up.<sic/></p>
<p>"My poor boy!" she said aloud.</p>
<p>"Why do you say that?" he asked, with a guilty start.</p>
<p>"Do you think," she said sorrowfully, "that I cannot read your thoughts? Ah! I am so sorry."</p>
<p>He cursed his nervousness, which for the moment had made him think she suspected the terrible plot he had entered into.</p>
<pb n="225 Was He Worthy of Her?"/>
<p>"What do you read?" he asked evasively.</p>
<p>"I read that you love Viola so much that you cannot bear to give her up."</p>
<p>"I do love her," he said, "with a passion I never dreamed myself capable of. I never before knew what love was. It will kill me to give her up."</p>
<p>"Ah! no," she said soothingly, "it will not kill you to do right."</p>
<p>"Well," he answered, "whether it kills me or not, if he be true to her I must give her up."</p>
<p>"That was my noble boy who spoke then," she murmured.</p>
<p>"But first," he went on doggedly, "I must have a better proof of his good intentions than your mere intuition."</p>
<p>"What proof, Howard?" she asked gently.</p>
<p>"I must have him followed by my detective in New York. He will know how true his words are as to his readiness to quarrel with his father."</p>
<p>"And not let poor Viola see him?" she queried regretfully.</p>
<p>"Not yet," he answered sharply. "You know that if they meet she will believe anything he tells her. Be just to me as well as generous to him."</p>
<p>"Howard, dear," she remonstrated, in a hurt tone, "you know I would never sacrifice you except to the right."</p>
<pb n="226 Was He Worthy of Her?"/>
<p>"You mean, mother," he answered irritably, "that you are willing to sacrifice me to your own notion of right. I claim that it is no more than fair that he should be tested. If he is worthy of her I must give way, but if he is not I have a right to ask that I have the chance to win her and make her happy."</p>
<p>"And you wish me to let him depart from the house to-night in order that your detective may follow him in the city?"</p>
<p>"Yes, mother."</p>
<p>"Well, I will accede; but I tell you frankly, Howard, that you are not doing what is right."</p>
<p>"I must take the responsibility of that, mother," he replied, rejoiced that he had won his battle so easily.</p>
<p>"Have you ordered the carriage?" she asked, in a tone that showed how little she liked yielding her point.</p>
<p>"I find that the horses are not fit to go out to-night. He will not mind walking. I will go part of the distance with him in order to show him the way."</p>
<p>"Very well," Mrs. Beekman said, and dismissed that part of the conversation from her mind.</p>
<p>She was determined that, if she could not give Walter the happiness of meeting Viola, she would at least bestow on him such happiness as there might be in conversation about her.</p>
<p>So she returned at once to the room where she had
<pb n="227 Was He Worthy of Her?"/>
left Walter, while Howard glided into the lower part of the house and contrived, unobserved, to purloin some food.</p>
<p>This, with a bottle of light wine he took out to the tool house, where Peter was ensconced, and where he found it possible to be comfortable.</p>
<p>All that troubled Peter's mind was that his good clothes would be soiled by the tools he was forced to come in contact with, which might entail the expenditure of some money.</p>
<p>"I'll get that out of Beekman," he said to himself, as he waited.</p>
<p>And so, after Howard had given him the food and wine, and had whispered that his mother had been won over, Peter whined:</p>
<p>"I shall be out of pocket by this trip, and I am a poor man. Miss Carroll never pays me for what I do."</p>
<p>"I will willingly pay you," said Howard. "I have some money with me. How much would satisfy you?"</p>
<p>Peter could never bear to set a price, lest it should be lower than the other would be willing to give.</p>
<p>"I'd rather leave that to you, Mr. Beekman. All I say is that I'm a very poor man, and that nothing will come amiss."</p>
<p>"I think there are one hundred dollars there," How-
<pb n="228 Was He Worthy of Her?"/>
ard said quickly, as he thrust a roll of bills into the band of the other. "Take it!"</p>
<p>He could not help thinking with a shudder that he was paying blood money, and there was a terribly heavy feeling in his heart.</p>
<p>"I think," chuckled Peter, in a tone of horrible purpose, "that one of these hammers would be a good thing for the deed."</p>
<p>"My Heaven! Don't talk in that way!" gasped Howard. "And don't remove any of these tools, or suspicion might fall on me."</p>
<p>The same thought had instantly leaped into the brain of Peter, and he cursed himself for his ill-timed levity.</p>
<p>"If I hadn't said anything," he thought, "I could have taken a hammer and left it where it would be found. Then I would only need to prove an alibi, and I would be all right.</p>
<p>"Of course I won't take any of them," he said. "I am not a fool."</p>
<p>"You will be cold in here, I'm afraid," Howard said. "Don't worry about me," Peter answered. "What time will you leave the house for the station?"</p>
<p>"At precisely eight o'clock."</p>
<p>"All right. I shall get out of here by quarter of. Don't take too long in getting there, for it is cold waiting out in the night air."</p>
<p>"I will hurry," Howard said, in a low tone.</p>
<pb n="229 Was He Worthy of Her?"/>
<p>Mrs. Beekman, meanwhile, had found Walter agitatedly pacing the floor of the sitting room.</p>
<p>"You are distressing yourself about Viola?" she said kindly.</p>
<p>"How can I help it?" he demanded. "If you could only know how I love her, you would understand. I loved her the first moment I ever saw her, and never since has there been any woman in the world for me."</p>
<p>"She is worthy of your great devotion, Mr. Hardman," the old lady said. "I have learned to love her since she has been with me."</p>
<p>"She has written me how good you have been to her," he answered. "I was so sure I would find her here. It all seems so strange. If it were not that I can see truth and honesty in your eyes, Mrs. Beekman, I could find it in my heart to doubt you."</p>
<p>The good old lady flushed crimson.</p>
<p>"Do not doubt me," she said earnestly. "I give you my word that I have in my heart nothing but love for Viola, and good will for you. My dearest wish is that she will be truly mated."</p>
<p>From this the conversation branched to other things, though always filled with thoughts of Viola.</p>
<p>Mrs. Beekman told Walter all the things she could remember of Viola, and described how bravely the latter had rescued little Rupert.</p>
<pb n="230 Was He Worthy of Her?"/>
<p>"My son and I are her everlasting debtors for that deed. And even if we did not love her for herself, we would cherish her for that noble act," she said.</p>
<p>How could Walter doubt the absolute good faith of his hosts, when Mrs. Beekman discoursed to him so earnestly of the love she bore Viola.</p>
<p>There had been a vague uneasiness in his mind, hardly amounting to a doubt, before; but by the time Howard returned to the room, Walter was fain to admit that he was with good friends of Viola's.</p>
<p>Howard did his best to maintain this illusion, but while Walter did not again revert to his uneasiness regarding him, he found it much more agreeable to him to converse with Mrs. Beekman.</p>
<p>The supper passed off in a rather constrained way, for there could be little cheerful intercourse under the circumstances.</p>
<p>Walter's mind was full of misery over Viola's mysterious absence, and Howard's was filled with terrible thoughts that he did his best to combat.</p>
<p>But all through the supper the idea presented itself to him that he was talking to a doomed man.</p>
<p>Now and again the picture came into his mind of the stealthy assassin stealing upon his victim, crushing his head in, and then throwing him off the cliff.</p>
<p>Sometimes it seemed as if he could not bear it any
<pb n="231 Was He Worthy of Her?"/>
longer, and as if he must go out and warn Peter that he must not do it.</p>
<p>Then the specious argument would present itself that it was none of his business, since it was not a deed of his doing.</p>
<p>"I shall have to ask your pardon for not taking you to the station in the carriage," he said, as supper neared its end.</p>
<p>"It will not matter to me, so that I get there in time," replied Walter. "I beg of you not to let me be late."</p>
<p>"I will take care of that," Howard said; "and to be sure that you do not go wrong I will accompany you part of the way."</p>
<p>When supper was over, and it was time to start, Howard, with a white face which he concealed by turning away, said carelessly:</p>
<p>"It is so cold out I think I will take a drop of brandy before starting. I will bring you a glass."</p>
<p>"I seldom touch anything, thank you," Walter said.</p>
<p>Nevertheless Howard entered the room a few moments later, a tiny glass of brandy in his hand, saying:</p>
<p>"Drink it! it will do you good. You have been under a considerable strain to-day, and will need a little stimulant."</p>
<p>It seemed true enough to Walter, and the quantity seemed so little, that he took the glass and tossed the contents down his throat.</p>
<pb n="232 Was He Worthy of Her?"/>
<p>He thought the taste rather queer; but, as he was no judge of brandy, he gave it no further thought.</p>
<p>"Good night!" he said to Mrs. Beekman. "Heaven bless you for your kindness to my darling!"</p>
<p>"Both you and she will always be welcome guests in this house," Mrs. Beekman responded.</p>

<pb n="[233]"/>
<div id="gfprice031" type="chapter">
<head rend = "center">CHAPTER XXXI.</head>
<byline>LURING HIS VICTIM.</byline>
<p>There was hidden in the nature of Peter Harriem the ferocity of the wild beast. If he had had the courage of the brute he would have been a terrible man.</p>
<p>The deed he had elected to do did not trouble him, except as it presented the possibility of danger.</p>
<p>In fact, he almost gloated over the part he had to play. He rehearsed in his evil brain just how he would do it.</p>
<p>And when the time came for him to steal forth from his hiding place, he lighted a match and looked quickly about him.</p>
<p>He would not take one of the tools that would be missed; but he would trust to any stick he might be able to find in the darkness.</p>
<p>The handle of a mattock caught his eye, and he took it in his hand and weighed it to see if it was something he would be likely to find too heavy for him to wield.</p>
<p>"It will do," he murmured. "A blow from that will forever silence him. How I hate him! I always did hate him. I hated him when he was a lad and used to come to the store all decked out in fine clothes, the price of which would have paid for my clothing for ten years.</p>
<pb n="234 Luring His Victim."/>
<p>"Then he came between me and Viola. That is what I am doing this for. Bah! no one will ever find it out. I would not have let that fool Beekman know if it had not been that I wish to have him under my thumb. I shall soon be done with him.<sic/></p>
<p>"Now, let me get out of here. I will take the key as a bit of evidence against Beekman. Ah-a! Peter Harriem is too cunning and subtle for these rich swells. Little by little he is climbing his way to the head.</p>
<p>"One of these days he will be one of the richest men in New York, and he will have the prettiest wife in New York, too. She shall wear good clothes to do me honor. I will dress her well, but she must be careful of her dresses, and not wear them out, as the silly creatures do who come to us to buy. That will do for others, but my wife must be careful. It is wrong to waste money, for money is power."</p>
<p>He crept like a snake away from the dismal little tool house, and soon was gliding along the road toward the cliff.</p>
<p>Walter and Howard, meanwhile, were walking along briskly after him. They were some distance behind, but the air was biting, and Walter was impatient, so they moved on rapidly.</p>
<p>"I am sorry you should have had to walk," Howard said, simply to maintain a conversation; for somehow the could not bear to walk in silence.</p>
<pb n="235 Luring His Victim."/>
<p>"It does not matter," Walter said. "I am glad to walk. Something seems the matter with me, anyhow. My head feels heavy. I am glad to walk."</p>
<p>"You have been on such a strain, I suppose," said Howard, with a little catch in his voice. It seemed to him as if he were delivering his companion, bound and helpless, to an assassin.</p>
<p>"Perhaps that is it. I did not feel it until I came out into the open air. No doubt it will soon pass away."</p>
<p>He passed his hand over his head as he spoke, and the next moment his foot struck against something, and he stumbled.</p>
<p>"That was careless," he said. "I did not pick my feet up."</p>
<p>"Do you feel ill?" asked Howard, in a low tone.</p>
<p>"Only this heaviness. Let us walk faster. Perhaps I can throw it off. I suppose I must be feeling the change from the ocean to the steady land. Some persons do, I am told."</p>
<p>"That is it, no doubt," Howard said huskily. "Would you like to turn back?"</p>
<p>If Walter had only taken up the invitation, perhaps the end would have been different. But these things go as they are ordered. He refused.</p>
<p>"Thank you, no. It is nothing. I shall be all right when I get on the cars. I must not lose any time in
<pb n="236 Luring His Victim."/>
hunting for Viola. Ugh! what a miserable twinge that was. Stop a moment!"</p>
<p>He rested for a second, and then moved rapidly on again, fearing he would not otherwise be able to make his way to the station.</p>
<p>He leaned unconsciously on Howard, and the latter dared not push him away, but the contact with the man he was leading to his death made the beads of perspiration roll off his forehead.</p>
<p>"How far is it?" Walter asked.</p>
<p>"About half a mile from here. Can you get on?"</p>
<p>"I must," was the response.</p>
<p>It was not far now to the place where he should abandon Walter to his fate. But Howard hesitated; it seemed to him like committing the murder. And yet, if he did not, was it not likely that Walter would take Viola from him?</p>
<p>But to say to his companion that he would leave him just when he was feeling so wretched. It was horrible!</p>
<p>"I&#x2014;I was going to leave you just beyond here," he said. "But since you feel as you do, I think&#x2014;I think that you had better return with me. What do you say?"</p>
<p>"No, I will go on. This is nothing. Do not mind me."</p>
<pb n="237 Luring His Victim."/>
<p>"Will you be able to reach the station?" asked Howard. "Are you sure you can find the way?"</p>
<p>Walter stopped again and pressed his hands to his head.</p>
<p>"It seems as if I were going to sleep," he murmured. "What can it be? Can I really be ill?"</p>
<p>"Perhaps it will pass away better if you move on," Howard suggested, fearing now that Walter would become unconscious before the right place was reached.</p>
<p>"Yes," said Walter, with a violent effort, "I will move on. I&#x2014;I feel better now."</p>
<p>He did seem to, and went on again with more vigor, though constantly passing his hands over his eyes as if to dispel a cloud that had gathered over them.</p>
<p>"Don't go farther than you should," he said. "I feel better now."</p>
<p>"Really?" demanded Howard. "I am afraid you say that only to be polite."</p>
<p>"No, it is true. Is this the place?"</p>
<p>"I was going to leave you here; but under the circumstances&#x2014;"</p>
<p>"Good-by!" Walter held out his hand, which Howard took with a sort of horror. "I hope to get news of Viola when I am in the city. Anyhow, I shall find her eventually, and I will make those who have persecuted her rue it. Good-by! thank you for your many kind-
<pb n="238 Luring His Victim."/>
nesses to Viola. She has told me many of the kind things you have done for her."</p>
<p>He shook Howard's hand and let it go.</p>
<p>"Good-by!" Howard said huskily. "I hope you will be successful."</p>
<p>He turned away and walked swiftly back on the road. Walter stood watching, and trying to collect himself more.</p>
<p>"I cannot make it out," he murmured. "I never felt anything like it before. It must be the change from sea to shore. It is passing away."</p>
<p>He drew himself up as if he would drive the sensation of drowsiness away by mere force of will.</p>
<p>"Now I am better. I thought I would drop a few moments ago. I am glad he went back. Somehow I do not like him. I had to thank him for his kindness, but I can't help feeling that his mother is truer. I don't believe he cares much for me, either. But he is so much older. Older men often dislike younger ones just because they are younger."</p>
<p>He stood and talked to himself in this way merely to give himself time to collect his forces.</p>
<p>He knew there was not much time to lose, so he forced himself to start on toward the station.</p>
<p>"I am better all the time. I will be well when I reach the station."</p>
<p>He said this as he strode forward and entered the
<pb n="239 Luring His Victim."/>
gloom of the spot where the trees began, and near which Peter lurked.</p>
<p>His senses were too enthralled by the drug that had been administered to him to enable him to hear the faint rustling of the leaves that followed the stealthy steps of Peter as he nervously changed his position.</p>
<p>But it might have been the unconscious perception of evil that made Walter start and stop when he had gone a few paces into the dark woods.</p>
<p>"How gloomy!" he muttered. "I wonder they do not put lamps at such dark places on country roads."</p>
<p>He started on again.</p>
<p>Peter changed his hold on the mattock handle. His hands were clammy with cold perspiration. He had to wipe his hands dry on his coat.</p>
<p>Walter reeled a little as he walked, for his head was light; but he was much better than he had been. The effects of the drug were working off.</p>
<p>"A queer place to leave a stranger," he muttered, as he strained his eyes in a vain attempt to see some light ahead. "I suppose he forgot about it, or is so used to it that he thinks it nothing."</p>
<p>He had come to the tree behind which Peter lurked.</p>
<p>He stopped there to try once more to clear his brain.</p>
<p>Peter hardly breathed. For a moment he fancied Walter had heard some sound to make him suspicious.</p>
<p>He had been able to detect that Walter was not
<pb n="240 Luring His Victim."/>
walking steadily, but he had not been able to tell what the extent of his condition was.</p>
<p>He waited with bated breath for Walter to move on two steps farther. He felt a sort of ferocious joy in the thought that, in a second more, perhaps, the deadly club he held in his hands would be crushing into Walter's skull.</p>
<p>Walter took the step. He took one more. Then he heard a light, rushing sound behind him and turned half round.</p>
<p>Then something struck him on the head, and he sunk on one knee. He heard a strangled cry of fierce joy, and the club fell again.</p>
<p>He tried to rise to his feet, holding his hands above his head to shield it.</p>
<p>"Help! help! help!" he cried.</p>
<p>He remembered that Howard Beekman could not be far away, and it seemed possible he might hear and return.</p>
<p>Peter did not utter a word; but the cries of Walter accelerated his movements; and once more the club fell.</p>

<pb n="[241]"/>
<div id="gfprice032" type="chapter">
<head rend = "center">CHAPTER XXXII.</head>
<byline>THE BODY BY THE ROADSIDE.</byline>
<p>Perhaps it was the blows he had received, perhaps the drug had already worked off its effects.</p>
<p>None of the blows struck him fairly on the head. His derby hat had protected him somewhat, and Peter's lack of muscle had had something to do with it.</p>
<p>The third blow struck him on the fleshy part of the arm, and for a moment he thought his arm was broken.</p>
<p>The pain did him good, however, for it brought back his scattered wits, and the consciousness came to him that if he would grapple with his assailant he might conquer him.</p>
<p>But he must put him off his guard, if possible. These thoughts flew like lightning through his brain, and ere Peter could strike another blow, Walter had fallen like a log in the road.</p>
<p>A panting cry of rage and delight came from the lips of Peter. He believed he had stunned Walter.</p>
<p>He wanted to see if his victim would move. If he had done so, the club would have been brought down mercilessly on his head again.</p>
<p>But Walter lay like one dead. Peter threw his club far away, and it could be heard crashing down through
<pb n="242 The Body by the Roadside."/>
the branches of the scrubby growth that lined the face of the cliff.</p>
<p>Peter had thrown it away, because he could not hold it and drag Walter to the edge of the precipice.</p>
<p>He leaped on Walter almost as he threw the club away. His hands closed on Walter's shoulders. Then he felt the hands of Walter leap up out of the darkness and clutch him by the throat.</p>
<p>Walter was at a disadvantage, being prostrate. But Peter was taken by surprise, and before he could think what to do, Walter had thrown him off, and was on his knees.</p>
<p>Then Peter realized that it was a fight for life, or more than life.</p>
<p>Therefore he summoned all his strength, and endeavored to force Walter back again to the earth, with the wild hope of strangling him.</p>
<p>Walter was gripping the throat of his assailant with a hand that seemed to Peter to be of steel.</p>
<p>It flashed through his brain then that this was another of the ways in which Walter had been more favored than he&#x2014;Walter was stronger.</p>
<p>And Walter had gained his feet now. His head was aching, but his brain was clear, and the ache he did not think of then.</p>
<p>Peter was losing consciousness; he had only sense enough not to betray himself by crying out. He knew
<pb n="243 The Body by the Roadside."/>
instinctively that it would be useless for him to try to bring Howard back now. Howard would think only of his own safety.</p>
<p>He was like a child now in the hands of Walter, and the gurgling cry of a strangling man rose to his lips.</p>
<p>"Mercy, do not kill me!"</p>
<p>Walter did not recognize the voice and did not heed the appeal. He dared not. He did not know what weapons the wretch might have at his command if once he escaped.</p>
<p>So he pressed the bony throat until the body of Peter sunk limp in his hands. Then he let him drop slowly to the earth, but watched him warily in the light, which would barely enable him to see at all.</p>
<p>Peter did not move, and Walter could tell by his feebly beating heart that there was not much life left in his still body.</p>
<p>"I hope I have not killed him," he murmured, in an awe-stricken tone. "He tried to take my life, but I don't wish his. I suppose he is some miserable thief. What shall I do with him?"</p>
<p>He knelt there for a few moments, trying to think what disposal he should make of him, when it occurred to him that if he did not hasten he would lose his train.</p>
<p>"I will drag him to the roadside," he said to himself. "Perhaps he is a poor wretch who was driven to his
<pb n="244 The Body by the Roadside."/>
crime. He had but little strength. I will look at his face. If it is that of a thief, I will speak of it at the station. If not, I will let him revive as he can, and will say nothing about it."</p>
<p>He picked the body up and carried it to the roadside, where he laid it down as carefully as the darkness, would permit.</p>
<p>Then he fumbled in his pocket and drew out his match safe.</p>
<p>"Once in a while," he muttered, "there is an advantage in being a smoker."</p>
<p>He struck a match as he spoke, and bent over the man. He started in dumb incredulity for a moment, then gasped:</p>
<p>"Peter Harriem! He sought my life. The scoundrel!"</p>
<p>The match went out and dropped from his fingers. The thoughts were coursing each other in his brain.</p>
<p>Why had Peter Harriem ventured on so desperate a deed? Peter Harriem was one of those who had con spired against the peace and happiness of Viola.</p>
<p>No doubt he wished Viola for himself. The wretch, to aspire so high! But how came he there at that hour of the night?</p>
<p>How came he to know that Walter would be passing there? It was not credible that it was a mere accident.</p>
<pb n="245 The Body by the Roadside."/>
<p>Peter moved, and Walter bent with a fierce impulse to seize him again by the throat and finish the work he had begun.</p>
<p>"He is not fit to live," he muttered. Then he added contemptuously: "But I shall not play the executioner for him. Let him live. He shall answer to me for this."</p>
<p>He drew his handkerchief out of his pocket&#x2014;it was a large silk one&#x2014;and with it tied the hands of the prostrate man together behind him.</p>
<p>Then Walter went on with his attempt to unravel the mystery. He struck another match and looked at Peter's face. It was plain that Peter was not near consciousness.</p>
<p>Walter thought of his train. And almost at the same moment he heard the sound of a distant whistle.</p>
<p>"Lost it," he muttered, and resigned himself to studying out the riddle of Peter's action.</p>
<p>"If he tried to get me out of the way he must have some knowledge of where Viola is. He may dislike me, but it is on account of her that he has tried to do this. I wish he would come to his senses. I would make him talk."</p>
<p>He stood still thinking, thinking. Suddenly he made up his mind.</p>
<p>"Yes," he cried, "I will do it I will return with him the Beekmans'. We can revive him, make him
<pb n="246 The Body by the Roadside."/>
tell the truth on penalty of being turned over to the law, and then I shall know where Viola is. I suppose this wretch had a spy there at the pier when I landed. He must have followed me, and has been watching me ever since. And he certainly knows where Viola is."</p>
<p>By this time Walter was conscious of the pain in his head from the blows he had received:</p>
<p>But nothing mattered in comparison with the fact that he had thought of a way of solving the mystery about Viola.</p>
<p>He leaned over and picked Peter up. There was no other way but to throw him over his shoulder as he might a sack of grain, and that he did.</p>
<p>It was fortunate now that Peter was so gaunt. If he had fed himself better he would have been heavier for Walter to carry.</p>
<p>He was weight enough as it was, and more than once Walter was forced to stop to take breath.</p>
<p>But there was no thought of doing anything but carrying out his original design. He would have done that at any cost now.</p>
<p>At last the lights of the Beekman mansion shone out, and he felt that he would soon know the truth.</p>
<p>His heart beat high with hope. He believed that he was on the eve of a discovery which should carry joy and happiness to the heart of Viola, as well as to his own.</p>
<pb n="247 The Body by the Roadside."/>
<p>He paused at the foot of the steps to take breath, then slowly mounted the steps with his burden and rang the bell.</p>
<p>He thought of nothing but that he would soon learn where his darling was, and he did not realize what a picture he made with that uncanny load over his shoulder, and the blood from his wounds staining his face.</p>
<p>What fate was it that brought Howard Beekman to the door?</p>
<p>Was it that he had been starting at every sound since he had reached home, and had been unable to bear the suspense of letting the maid go to the door?</p>
<p>It was he who stood there and stared at Walter as if madness were visiting his brain.</p>
<p>Then a horrified scream broke from his lips, and he reeled back, fainting.</p>

<pb n="[248]"/>
<div id="gfprice033" type="chapter">
<head rend = "center">CHAPTER XXXIII.</head>
<byline>REELING IN HORROR.</byline>
<p>Men may cry out at the horror of a sight, but they seldom faint unless a terrible shock is received.</p>
<p>Howard Beekman had received a shock which he would never get over as long as he lived.</p>
<p>He had passed through all the horrors and misery which his imagination could inflict upon him already. He had suffered tortures of conscience; for twist and turn as he would, he could not blind himself to the fact that he was the accomplice of a murderer.</p>
<p>It had required all his self-control to keep his face from betraying to his mother the agony of his soul. He seemed to see blood on everything, and every noise was but the echo of the blows which the assassin was dealing out to the innocent, defenseless victim.</p>
<p>He had gone to the door only because he was unable to bear the suspense of waiting to know from the maid who it was that came there.</p>
<p>Then he had been confronted by the awful spectacle of Walter, with bloodstained face and disordered apparel, bearing on his shoulder the limp body of a man.</p>
<p>In his breathlessness from his exertions, Walter did not speak as he stood there, and to the troubled imagi-
<pb n="249 Reeling in Horror."/>
nation of Howard Beekman what he saw was but the wraith of the murdered man.</p>
<p>It was that horror that sent him reeling in a swoon against the wall and made his wild cry sound weirdly through the house.</p>
<p>His trembling limbs did not sink under him, however, and when his mother ran into the hall to ascertain the cause of his frightful cry, he was opening his eyes to stare once again at the picture framed in the doorway.</p>
<p>By this time Walter had recovered his breath and was able to pant:</p>
<p>"Am I so frightful to look at? This man tried to murder me, and I did not know what else to do but to bring him here. It was necessary to take him somewhere."</p>
<p>Mrs. Beekman was speechless with horror, but the words of Walter restored her presence of mind and enabled her to say:</p>
<p>"Your face is covered with blood. Come in and let us do something for you. Howard, help Mr. Hardman!"</p>
<p>Howard forced himself to move forward, hardly able to get over his first shock, yet knowing that his very salvation depended on his doing something to avert discovery.
"Let me take the&#x2014;the wretch," he said. "I will
<pb n="250 Reeling in Horror."/>
put him in a safe place while mother does what she can for you. Are you much hurt?"</p>
<p>"My head is bruised, but that is the worst. Do not let him escape from you. He must explain many things."</p>
<p>"I will put him in the little storeroom upstairs, mother," said Howard, staggering under the burden as he approached the stairs.</p>
<p>"I will send one of the men to assist you," Mrs. Beekman said.</p>
<p>"No, no!" Howard cried hastily. "I think it will be better not to let the servants know of this unless it is necessary. It might interfere with Mr. Hardman's plans in regard to the wretch."</p>
<p>"I do not know that it will make any difference in that respect," Walter said. "Do as you think best."</p>
<p>Mrs. Beekman led him to a room upstairs, and then, with the skill of a woman who has lived long and been useful, she examined the wounds on his head.</p>
<p>"Your scalp is bruised," she said at last, "but I have lotions which will relieve the pain and keep away the ache to-morrow. You should go to bed at once, however."</p>
<p>"Not until I have talked with the miscreant who tried to take my life," Walter said firmly.</p>
<p>"Well," said the old lady dubiously, "I will bathe and bind up your wounds, and will let you talk a little
<pb n="251 Reeling in Horror."/>
with the man, but after that you must take a sleeping potion and retire."</p>
<p>"I will do as you say when I have learned what he has to tell me&#x2014;what he must tell me."</p>
<p>"You know him, then?" Mrs. Beekman said inquiringly.</p>
<p>"Yes, I know him," Walter answered.</p>
<p>"Is he some person who has reason to do you harm?" she asked.</p>
<p>She had a natural curiosity to know what the relations of the two were, yet did not like to ask outright.</p>
<p>"I suppose he thinks that by getting rid of me he will be better able to carry out his foul plot against Viola."</p>
<p>"Viola!" cried the old lady. "What has she to do with this?"</p>
<p>"She is the victim of his wiles," Walter replied. "He knows where she is."</p>
<p>Mrs. Beekman started and stared at Walter.</p>
<p>"Why do you think he knows?" she asked.</p>
<p>She was moving about, waiting on Walter as they talked.</p>
<p>"I will tell you, Mrs. Beekman, for I know that you are true and good. The truth is, that that scoundrel is my father's right-hand man."</p>
<p>"Surely you do not suspect your father of any complicity in this horrible attempt?" she cried.</p>
<pb n="252 Reeling in Horror."/>
<p>Walter smiled.</p>
<p>"Certainly not. My father adores me, and would as soon think of taking his own life as of injuring me in the slightest. But I suspect this fellow of working against Viola because my father wishes to prevent the marriage. And as he loves Viola, I do not doubt that he has tried to murder me to get me out of the way, knowing that while I live, Viola will not listen to him."</p>
<p>"Nor would she afterward!" Mrs. Beekman said, with horror.</p>
<p>"He probably does not believe that. Anyhow, he tried to murder me, and I reason from it that he knows where Viola is."</p>
<p>It was on Mrs. Beekman's tongue to reveal the truth about Viola to Walter; but something she could not then understand restrained her.</p>
<p>"How did it happen?" she asked.</p>
<p>She believed she could learn from the circumstances facts that might be hidden from Walter.</p>
<p>"He was waiting for me in a dense, thick wood just beyond where your son left me."</p>
<p>"And you did not hear him approach you?" she asked.</p>
<p>"Not until he was near me, and then I was so stupid from a strange attack of drowsiness that it needed his first blow to restore my senses.</p>
<pb n="253 Reeling in Horror."/>
<p>"Drowsiness!" she exclaimed curiously, staring at him. "Did you feel it when you left here?"</p>
<p>"No, it came on after I was outside for a while. At one time I thought I should sink to the road. It probably was due to the change from shipboard to land."</p>
<p>"I wonder that Howard left you," she said, rather to herself than to him. "Did he know you felt so?"</p>
<p>"I felt better when he left me," Walter answered.</p>
<p>"Drowsy!" murmured Mrs. Beekman. "That is not the way one is affected after a voyage. You are dizzy, perhaps, and you feel the motion as of the vessel under your feet. But that is all I ever heard of. You drank no wine at dinner?"</p>
<p>"No, I drank no wine," he answered. "Though I did take a glass of brandy at your son's urgent request just before I went out. Do you remember?"</p>
<p>"I was not in the room at the time," she replied, in a low tone.</p>
<p>Her brain was busy analyzing what Walter had told her. It has already been seen what a keen mind she had for sifting evidences.</p>
<p>She puzzled over this, hardly knowing what she was doing, until a white, distressed expression crept over her face.</p>
<p>"I must speak with Howard," she said to herself. "Of course he will explain how it happened. But it haunts me."</p>
<pb n="254 Reeling in Horror."/>
<p>"Did you speak to me?" Walter asked.</p>
<p>She started.</p>
<p>"No, no! I was thinking of the strangeness of the case. So you think this man knows where Viola is, Why do you feel so sure of it?"</p>
<p>"It was through him, in the first place, that the accusation of theft came. I am sure of that&#x2014;just as sure as if I had known his thoughts. He and Eunice Carroll have done it between them."</p>
<p>"I think there is no doubt of that," Mrs. Beekman said.</p>
<p>"Then Viola disappeared suddenly and mysteriously from your house. It was very unlike her. She would have told you she was going if she had had the chance."</p>
<p>"You think she was abducted?" said the old lady, wondering how far his reasoning would lead him toward the truth.</p>
<p>The thing she was certain of was that whether his reasoning led him or not, he should know that Viola was under the same roof as he that night.</p>
<p>"I do not doubt it. And now I am sure that she was abducted at the instigation of Peter Harriem. I am sure, too, that she is still somewhere in this neighborhood."</p>
<p>"Why do you think that?" she asked.</p>
<p>"Because this wretch was here to prevent me seeing
<pb n="255 Reeling in Horror."/>
her. He had followed me here, and feared I would discover the truth, hence the attack on me."</p>
<p>It flashed through Mrs. Beekman's mind that his reasoning, if correct, must imply that the scoundrel knew that Viola was underneath her roof.</p>
<p>And if he knew that, how did he learn it? What must she think? She turned away and pressed her hands to her heart.</p>
<p>It seemed as if she must relieve herself of her trouble by saying at once to Walter that Viola was in the house.</p>
<p>She turned with the words on her tongue, but hesitated when she saw the pallor of his face.</p>
<p>"He is not fit to bear it yet," she murmured. "Besides he is keen, and the same thoughts might flit through his head as have seared their way through mine. I will wait until later. I will give him something now to revive him, so that he will have strength for his interview with his would-be murderer."</p>
<p>She went over to the medicine chest, which contained the cordials and drugs, and lifted the lid.</p>
<p>"Somebody has been at this," she murmured, and began to run her eye over the vials with keen scrutiny.
A deathly pallor suddenly leaped into her face, and she tottered back. A low moan fell from her lips, and she took one vial out of its place.</p>
<pb n="256 Reeling in Horror."/>
<p>"Is anything the matter?" Walter asked, with quick apprehension.</p>
<p>"A pain in my heart," she answered, with trembling lips. "It&#x2014;it sometimes comes. It&#x2014;it is gone now."</p>
<p>He accepted her explanation and was silent. She examined the vial with close scrutiny.</p>
<p>"This has been used from," she murmured miserably. "A few drops of this in a glass of brandy would cause one to act just as our guest did. Oh, merciful Heaven! can it be that my boy in his mad infatuation for Viola has been guilty of this terrible crime? Heaven help me! What shall I do?"</p>
<p>"I am feeling better now," Walter said. "I think I will go see my prisoner and extort the truth from him."</p>
<p>"You&#x2014;you are not strong enough," she stammered. "He may not have revived yet."</p>
<p>"As you please," Walter acquiesced, sinking back in his seat. "But if he has recovered I must see him to-night. I could not rest knowing that I am so near discovery."</p>
<p>"You shall see him to-night," she said, and hastened from the room.</p>


<pb n="[257]"/>
<div id="gfprice034" type="chapter">
<head rend = "center">CHAPTER XXXIV.</head>
<byline>MISERY IN HIS BREAST.</byline>
<p>While Mrs. Beekman, with growing horror and misery, was groping her way to the awful truth about her son, the latter had been busted with Peter.</p>
<p>He had felt some signs of life in him when he took him on his shoulder, so he quickly deposited him on a long box in the storeroom, and hastened to his own room for a bottle of brandy.</p>
<p>When he returned, he poured as much as he could of the liquor down the throat of the unconscious man.</p>
<p>The effect was almost immediate. Peter started up as if liquid fire had coursed through his veins.</p>
<p>His first thought was that he was still in the dark woods with the hands of Walter pressing his throat, and he began a strangled scream.</p>
<p>Howard clapped his hand over the other's lips and begged him to desist.</p>
<p>"In the name of Heaven, make no noise," he said. "We have but a few moments to consider a means of escape from the awful plight you have gotten us into. Do you understand me? It is I, Howard Beekman, who is speaking."</p>
<p>"Ugh!" murmured Peter, feeling of his throat with
<pb n="258 Misery in His Breast."/>
his long, bony fingers. "I thought it was Walter. What have you done with him? Did you throw him over the cliff?"</p>
<p>"Fool!" groaned Howard, in a tremor of fear. "You are his prisoner. He brought you to this house to keep you a prisoner."</p>
<p>"A prisoner!" gasped Peter. "Let me go. He is not here now.  Let me escape! If he gets me into the hands of the law, I am lost, and you are, too," he added, with a savage snarl.</p>
<p>"I cannot let you go," Howard said. "I should be suspected. We must think of some other way. Don't you understand? He is going to interrogate you about Viola. You must bear with it and think of some way of deceiving him."</p>
<p>"Stay here to go to prison?" snarled Peter, trying to push past Howard. "Do you think I am a fool? If I am taken, I swear I will implicate you."</p>
<p>"As well that as to be suspected by letting you escape," Howard said doggedly. "Can't you listen to reason? We have but a few minutes."</p>
<p>Peter steadied his nerves by an effort and reviewed the situation quickly. It looked as if exposure was certain to overtake him, in any event. The only thing to do was to escape with as little punishment as possible.</p>
<p>He was not troubled by scruples about his compan-
<pb n="259 Misery in His Breast."/>
ion in crime. He considered only how to get himself out of trouble.</p>
<p>He asked himself what good it would do him to run away then. He saw that he would be an outcast at once, and would be unable to accomplish any of his ends.</p>
<p>To save something, and as much as possible, from the wreck was his thought. A subtle, cunning plan flashed into his brain suddenly.</p>
<p>"I will remain and bear his examination," he said quickly, "but during the night I must escape. Can you arrange that? There must be some way that will not implicate you."</p>
<p>I will bring you a screw driver," Howard said. "With it you can unscrew the lock, which, you see, is on this side of the door. Then you can get out when you will."</p>
<p>"That is right. I will protect you in my story. He cannot suspect you."</p>
<p>"Yet we have lost our game," Howard said gloomily.</p>
<p>"Why? you are as well off as ever."</p>  
<p>"No," answered Howard. "My mother will surely tell him now that Viola is in the house."</p>
<p>"She must not," Peter said. "If he meets Viola, and learns from her what I have done to her, he will kill me. If he meets her before I have escaped, I swear
<pb n="260 Misery in His Breast."/>
I will betray you. You must persuade your mother to keep the secret until the morning."
<p>"I will if I can," Howard murmured, his face ghostly at the thought of being exposed.</p>
<p>"It is not if you can," hissed Peter venomously, "you must."</p>
<p>"I will do my utmost," Howard said.</p>
<p>"Now get me the screw driver," said Peter.</p>
<p>Howard glided swiftly away and returned in a few moments with the tool in question.</p>
<p>"Put it where I can get it, and then tie my hands again," Peter said. "If you tie them I shall have no difficulty in releasing myself. Be sure to prevail on your mother to keep the secret until the morning. Perhaps then I may be able to think of some way by which you can obtain the beautiful Viola after all."</p>
<p>"I will plot no more," Howard said, with a shudder. "Heaven forgive me for what I have already done. I will do no more evil. I think I was mad when I yielded."</p>
<p>Peter smiled inwardly as he thought how useless a crime it would have been for his dupe.</p>
<p>"Just as you please about that," he said. "Somebody is coming. It is your mother, I think. I do not hear his step."</p>
<p>Mrs. Beekman, pale but stern and with burning eyes, entered the room, and looked at the prisoner.</p>
<pb n="261 Misery in His Breast."/>
<p>She studied his sneaking, furtive manner, and noticed his whole mean and vicious appearance. Then she turned to Howard and said:</p>
<p>"He is recovered. Shut the door and come with me. I have something to say to you."</p>
<p>It seemed to the guilty man that his mother had discovered everything, and his soul quailed within him, as guilt will quail before honesty and high purpose.</p>
<p>He did as she bade him, exchanging one furtive glance of dismay with Peter, whose eyes conveyed a threat.</p>
<p>"The old lady suspects," was the thought that flashed through the brain of the cunning wretch. "He will have a hard time to persuade her to keep the secret. Well, I will not be outwitted without a struggle. I only hope some one will come with Walter to keep him from violence."</p>
<p>Howard followed his mother into his own room, which she selected, because it was the nearest. She closed the door after he had entered.</p>
<p>He did not speak.</p>
<p>It was plain from her manner that some suspicion was in her mind, but he could not tell how much.</p>
<p>Oh, how he wished in that moment of suspense that he had never fallen to the depths of sin and crime, from which he now saw no way of escaping.</p>
<p>"Howard," said the good old lady, her pride for-
<pb n="262 Misery in His Breast."/>
saking her then, "what have you done? Oh, my boy! you who have been my pride, my stay, and comfort alt these years! tell me that you are not guilty. Tell me that I am misled. by a hideous appearance of complicity that does not in reality exist. Howard! speak!"</p>
<p>He turned away with such misery in his breast as had never been there before. He had sinned and plotted, and the end was that his mother had found him out, and would go down to her grave despising him.</p>
<p>"I know&#x2014;I know," she faltered. "Love will make a man do many things. But this was murder, Howard! Oh, Heaven, that I should live to say it!"</p>
<p>He saw that she either knew or suspected everything.</p>
<p>"It did not succeed," he said miserably.</p>
<p>"But it was in your heart," she moaned.</p>
<p>"I was insane," he said. "Mother, I am as thankful as you that it did not succeed. I should have gone mad had it been done. That man tempted me. I cannot shift the burden of my sin on him, but it is all I have to say,"</p>
<p>"He tempted you, that he might make use of you. Had he succeeded in his awful purpose, Howard, he would have laughed. at you, and would have gone on plotting that he might win Viola himself."</p>
<pb n="263 Misery in His Breast."/>
<p>"He!" cried Howard, with a start. "Why, he is but the agent of Eunice Carroll."</p>
<p>"The agent of Eunice Carroll!" repeated the old lady sadly. "Oh, how you have been beguiled! Do you imagine that Eunice Carroll would wish to murder the man she loves and has plotted crime to win?"</p>
<p>"Who is he, then?" Howard cried.</p>
<p>"He is Peter Harriem, who in everything seeks only to get that poor girl in his clutches. He has been using you. Oh, Howard!"</p>
<p>"Peter Harriem!" murmured Howard, realizing at once how he had been duped and played with.</p>
<p>"No other than he," she said. "Now, Howard, help to undo all that you have done. Frankly and humbly give up your hope of winning her from her true lover, and if there be anything to tell that I do not know, let me hear it from your lips."</p>
<p>Howard reflected on his compact with Peter. It was plain enough that to the last Peter was plotting further ruin for him.</p>
<p>"I will tell you everything, mother," he said. "Try to forgive me for what I have done, and on my part I will spend my life in atonement."</p>
<p>"My poor boy!" faltered the mother, ever ready to forgive the child of her bosom.</p>
<p>"I will give up Viola," he said. "I had already resolved on that. My passion is cured by the awful sin
<pb n="264 Misery in His Breast."/>
that has been brought home to me. I hope she will be restored to him who loves her and deserves the reward of her love."</p>
<p>"Ah! it atones for much to say these things, Howard. Is there anything I should know?"</p>
<p>"But one thing: Peter Harriem threatened to expose me if I did not persuade you to keep the presence of Viola a secret from Walter Hardman until morning."</p>
<p>"Why did he wish that?" she demanded quickly.</p>
<p>"I do not know."</p>
<p>"I wonder he did not demand to be released," she said.</p>
<p>"I was afraid to let him go lest I should be suspected of complicity. So he agreed to remain on condition that Viola's presence here was kept from Walter until morning."</p>
<p>The old lady pondered that a while, and then said abruptly:</p>
<p>"And he was willing to remain a prisoner and go to jail in the morning if you agreed to that?"</p>
<p>"Oh, no! I was to release him during the night. Or, rather, I have given him a screw driver so that he may let himself out and escape."</p>
<p>Again the old lady's keen brain pondered the matter, and something like the truth came to her.</p>
<p>"I think I understand," she said. "But I will spoil
<pb n="265 Misery in His Breast."/>
his plot. And yet, for your sake, he must go free. There is no need that Mr. Hardman should know what you have done."</p>
<p>"I had hoped so," he said humbly.</p>
<p>"No, there is no need," she said.</p>

<pb n="[266]"/>
<div id="gfprice035" type="chapter">
<head rend = "center">CHAPTER XXXV.</head>
<byline>"HE HAS STOLEN HER."</byline>
<p>"What will you do, mother?" Howard asked.</p>
<p>"I will go to Mr. Hardman and tell him frankly that we had had reason to suspect his intentions toward Viola, and that for that reason we had not told him the truth in regard to her."</p>
<p>"Perhaps," said Howard, with a look of shame, "I should tell you something more."</p>
<p>"Tell me everything that will be of service," she said.</p>
<p>"When Viola disappeared from here she was not arrested and taken to prison, as she supposed. That was but a pretense. The men were vile wretches, who impersonated officers of the law. The place they took her to was only a private house they had hired for the purpose."</p>
<p>"And you knew this, Howard?" she said reproachfully.</p>
<p>"I did not know it until I had been to New York and had seen Eunice Carroll, whose scheme it was. She told me of it in order that I might make use of the opportunity to win Viola."</p>
<p>"And you have been playing a part ever since Viola came back?" she said.</p>
<pb n="267 He Has Stolen Her."/>
<p>"I have been playing a part," he answered sorrowfully.</p>
<p>"Ah, well!" she murmured, "we will make a happy ending for it to-night. Do not go near that wretch again. I will go to Walter Hardman and tell him the good news."</p>
<p>"You will not implicate me, mother?"</p>
<p>"He shall know nothing about your part in it."</p>
<p>Walter had been waiting impatiently for the return of Mrs. Beekman, and started up when she entered the room.</p>
<p>"I am ready now," he said, "to interview that wretch. I feel quite like myself."</p>
<p>"I wish to say a few words to you first," she said. "Please sit down."</p>
<p>He reseated himself reluctantly, looking impatiently at her for an explanation of her words.</p>
<p>"Is it something important?" he asked.</p>
<p>"Very important," she answered, with a smile, for it rejoiced her to think of reuniting and making happy two such true lovers. "Viola has told me of you."</p>
<p>"Then Viola has, after all, talked of me to you?" he queried.</p>
<p>"She has done little else since a certain event, of which I will now tell you. And I must say again that what I am going to tell you now I would have told you before you left the house this evening, but for a
<pb n="268 He Has Stolen Her."/>
desire to be overcautious. Viola is dear to me&#x2014;as dear as a child of my own."</p>
<p>Walter glanced quickly at the old lady, and a sudden color swept up into his cheeks, while his lips parted as if eager to ask a question.</p>
<p>"Question me, then," the old lady said, with a certain glad ring in
her voice that made him cry out:</p>
<p>"You know where she is?"</p>
<p>"I do."</p>
<p>"Is she&#x2014;is she&#x2014;oh, relieve my suspense! is she in this house?"</p>
<p>Mrs. Beekman moved quickly over to his side, and answered with an outburst of joy:</p>
<p>"She is in this house."</p>
<p>He leaped to his feet, his face radiant with happiness.</p>
<p>"She knows I am here?"</p>
<p>"She knows nothing about it. She is hidden here because we feared for her safety. Even the servants in the house are ignorant of her presence here."</p>
<p>"How good you are! Oh, take me to her! I cannot wait an instant longer. Ah! now I understand why that miscreant tried to murder me. Perhaps he believed I had seen her and was only going away for a little while. But take me to her."</p>
<p>"Would it not be better to prepare her?" the old lady asked doubtfully.</p>
<pb n="269 He Has Stolen Her."/>
<p>"If you think it best, though they say joy never kills. Go to her, but hasten. I will stand at the door and await your call."</p>
<p>"Yes, yes; I will go."</p>
<p>"Warn her of the bandages on my head. May I not take off the hideous things? My head is so much better."</p>
<p>"Such vanity!" she laughed. "Well, I do not see why you should not. And there! make yourself look a little more presentable while I go. You will be able to hear my call. How happy I am!"</p>
<p>"Heaven bless you!" cried Walter.</p>
<p>She went quickly away, and he turned to the mirror to adjust his collar and tie, and to brush his hair.</p>
<p>All had been in disorder before, but he had not cared. Now it seemed trivial, but it was something to do.</p>
<p>He was in the act of brushing his hair as well as he could, when there fell on his ears a cry of terror and dismay.</p>
<p>He ran to the door and heard it repeated, coupled with hurrying footsteps. He saw Mrs. Beekman hurrying toward him and ran to meet her.</p>
<p>"She is gone!" she said. "She is not here!"</p>
<p>As she spoke she snatched open the door of the storeroom and looked in.</p>
<p>"He is gone, too. He has stolen her away!"</p>

<pb n="[270]"/>
<div id="gfprice036" type="chapter">
<head rend = "center">CHAPTER XXXVI.</head>
<byline>A BOLD DEED.</byline>
<p>Keen-witted and always alert for the worst, Peter Harriem had seen in the face of Mrs. Beekman that she suspected much.</p>
<p>A glance at her stern, troubled, yet noble face, had told him that she might be merciful to one repentant, but inexorable to one who persevered in villainy.</p>
<p>"She will get him away, and he will be putty in her hands," he muttered to himself, gnawing his lip as she spoke.</p>
<p>He knew that if Howard Beekman revealed what he knew there would be short shrift for him.</p>
<p>In the consciousness of his danger he did not stop to reason it out, but instantly cast off the handkerchief with which his hands were tied.</p>
<p>"I must think and act quickly," he muttered. "They think they have me cornered. They forget that a cornered rat is always the most dangerous. I have everything to gain, and nothing to lose. But what&#x2014;what?"</p>
<p>He caught up the screw driver Howard had given him and attacked the door, his mind working as his hands did.</p>
<p>Like lightning his thought flew all around the subject
<pb n="271 A Bold Deed."/>
of his danger, and now and again it would take a leap off to one side.</p>
<p>In one of these leaps aside, so to speak, he recalled that Mrs. Beekman had had something in her hand when she entered, and that she had mechanically laid it on one of the boxes.</p>
<p>He turned his head to see what it was, but could not, and murmured to himself:</p>
<p>"I will look when this is done. The first thing is to make my escape easy. I must not wait for Walter to find me here."</p>
<p>So he worked with astonishing celerity at the lock and presently had it off the door.</p>
<p>"Now I can go. I do not know how much time I have, but I can surely escape. Fools! they shall not trap me this time. What was that she brought in here?"</p>
<p>It seemed strange that his mind should revert to an apparently unimportant matter like that, but his was a mind that neglected nothing.</p>
<p>He sprang over to the box where he had seen her deposit the article in question. It was a small vial.</p>
<p>He snatched it up and held it so that he could read what was on it. As he did so, he uttered an oath of anger.</p>
<p>"This is how she caught him. This is what he used to put in the liquor he gave Walter. Yes, I must
<pb n="272 A Bold Deed."/>
go, indeed, I suppose he can turn State's evidence and go free while I am sent up for years. No, no! I won't take that chance."</p>
<p>He opened the door softly and peered out into the hall. It was deserted and offered him a clear way to escape.</p>
<p>He was stealing out, when a sudden idea struck him and made him pause, while a hideous, malignant scowl distorted his features.</p>
<p>"Why not?" he muttered. "As well for an old sheep as for a young lamb. In this case, however, it is the young lamb. I will try, though I get my death in doing it."</p>
<p>He glided back into the storeroom and snatched up the vial.</p>
<p>Then, with the swiftness of a well-defined purpose, he stole along the hall and up a flight of stairs.</p>
<p>"I am sure this is the way," he muttered.</p>
<p>At the head of the stairs he stopped and listened and peered about him.</p>
<p>"Straight ahead and then to the right," he muttered. "There can be no mistake. Aha, Walter Hardman! it may be my turn, after all. And I have some wires to pull yet."</p>
<p>He went swiftly in the direction he had laid out for himself, and presently had the satisfaction of see-
<pb n="273 A Bold Deed."/>
ing a faint ray of light streaming under a door near him.</p>
<p>He glided like a snake toward the door and listened. He could hear a faint movement within. Softly he tried the door. It was locked on the inside.</p>
<p>He knew that every moment was precious.</p>
<p>How to bring Viola to the door the most quickly and with the least noise.</p>
<p>He glanced at the vial, which he held in his hand, as if it were a precious thing. Then he gave a soft but assured tap.</p>
<p>"Is that you, Mrs. Beekman?" said the soft, sweet voice of Viola; and then, without waiting for a response, she began to unlock the door.</p>
<p>Peter stepped back and waited. Everything depended on the quickness of his movements now.</p>
<p>The door opened and Viola looked wonderingly out. The light she had been in prevented her seeing in the dark where Peter had withdrawn himself.</p>
<p>"I must have been mistaken," she murmured. "I suppose I am so anxious to have her come I imagined she knocked."</p>
<p>She turned as she closed the door, a sigh of weariness escaping her lips.</p>
<p>Then was the moment for Peter to act, and he did so.</p>
<pb n="274 A Bold Deed."/>
<p>He leaped through the door and caught her around the neck, so that nothing but a choking cry escaped her.</p>
<p>He closed the door behind him with a movement of his foot, and with his teeth uncorked the vial he held in his hand.</p>
<p>In another moment he had drawn her head back, and reckless of the danger to her, poured the contents of the vial on her lips.</p>
<p>Fortunately for her life, but little of the drug really entered her mouth and trickled down her throat, or her death would have been instantaneous.</p>
<p>She swallowed enough, however, for it to take effect, and, in spite of all her efforts to retain her consciousness, she sunk lifeless in his arms.</p>
<p>Pale and with panting breath, Peter now opened the door and listened.</p>
<p>He could not hear a sound.</p>
<p>"I must take the chance," said he, under his breath. "I have risked so much and been successful, perhaps fate will see me through. Aha! who would think Peter would do so bold a deed? But I have been driven to it. Now to get her away; I am not strong like Walter, but I have endurance."</p>
<p>He took her up in his arms, and, having disposed her over his shoulder, he crept warily from the room.</p>
<p>It needed no one to tell him the risk he ran if he should be caught.</p>
<pb n="275 A Bold Deed."/>
<p>He was sure if either Walter or Howard were to catch him in the act of abducting Viola his life would hardly be worth a moment's purchase.</p>
<p>But Howard and his mother were engaged in the former's room, and Walter was impatiently waiting for the return of his hostess.</p>
<p>If he had but known that the faint noise he heard in the hall was the miscreant who had attempted his murder, now stealing away with the treasure of his heart.</p>
<p>But Peter passed the door and was down the stairs in so short a space of time that Walter forgot in a moment that he had heard the noise.</p>
<p>Peter closed the front door after him softly, and then, with a freer heart, glided as rapidly as his burden would allow down the path and out into the road.</p>
<p>The weather was sharp and cold, and Viola was illy prepared for it, but she was unconscious, and Peter thought of nothing but safety.</p>
<p>He had not even determined where he would take her. It had flashed into his brain that, rather than surrender her to Walter, he would throw her over the very cliff which he had selected for our hero.</p>
<p>But he was not disposed to lose her, after having taken such pains to find and possess her. So, as he sped along the road, he cudgeled his brains, and at last uttered a low cry of triumph.</p>
<pb n="276 A Bold Deed."/>
<p>"There is no better place. If I but had a horse!"</p>
<p>As he spoke a short laugh escaped him, and he muttered:</p>
<p>"Why not? Perhaps it will never be found out. I will try it. With a horse and carriage, and that house to hide her in, I may defy pursuit until I have made myself secure.
