Now, the time is coming when the paper given me by Silas Hardman will protect me. He thought I wanted it only as a security for the share in business. He will learn that Peter Harriem can make more than one use of a document like that."</p>
<p>He exerted himself to move at a greater speed now, and, after having gone along the road to the station for a short distance, turned aside and took a road which would lead him to the village, not more than half a mile distant from that point.</p>
<p>He reached the village, panting and almost ready to drop, but, after a moment's rest in the shadow of a building, he glided toward the hotel sheds, where a number of horses and carriages were standing, waiting for their owners to come out.</p>
<p>He waited for a few moments, to be sure that no one was around to see him, and then stole into the sheds, and as well as he could selected the best horse and buggy.</p>
<p>He placed Viola in the carriage, untied the horse and
<pb n="277 A Bold Deed."/>
backed it out with as little noise as possible, muttering as he did so:</p>
<p>"If I had not been everybody's drudge when I was a boy, I would not have known how to choose the horse. He looks like a good one."</p>
<p>As soon as the animal was out, Peter leaped into the carriage and clucked to the horse, which started away at a quick trot, as if glad of the chance to get home.</p>
<p>And Peter, so far from discouraging the animal's haste, accelerated it by occasional strokes of the whip, so that in a little while the road echoed with the sharp beat of the hoofs on its frozen surface.</p>
<p>Peter had been in the neighborhood so many times that he knew the way well, and needed only an occasional glance at the roadside to enable him to know where he was.</p>
<p>He was merciless to the poor horse, and flogged it when it would have been ready to stop.</p>
<p>But it was a matter of more than life and death to him.</p>
<p>He was battling for triumph over those he hated, and for possession of her for whom he felt a fierce passion.</p>
<p>On and on the poor beast flew, until at last Peter drew it up with a sharp pull and searched the objects along the road for a guide to his whereabouts.</p>
<pb n="278 A Bold Deed."/>
<p>"This is the place," he cried exultantly.</p>
<p>He climbed out, lifted Viola out, and then turned the horse's head in the direction whence it had come, and with a sharp slap on the loins started it on its homeward journey.</p>
<p>"I call that a brilliant performance," he chuckled. "No one will be able to trace me. Aha! Peter Harriem deserves to succeed. Now for the house. I reckon there are nothing but ghosts there to disturb me, and I am not afraid of them. They don't scare me."</p>
<p>He had placed Viola, still unconscious, on the ground. He now took her up and carried her swiftly through a tangled path, at the end of which a dark mass loomed up.</p>
<p>"What a place a haunted house is for hiding a treasure!" he murmured, as he neared it. "I only hope no one else feels as I do about it."</p>
<p>He paused when he reached the house, and looked it over and listened. He did not really fear that anybody was in it, but he was naturally cautious.</p>
<p>"How they will storm when they discover that both birds have flown!" he laughed, as he opened the door of the house and entered. "You have been here before, my dear," he muttered, as he groped along the hall, "and I know there is a nice little cell all fitted up for your use."</p>
<pb n="279 A Bold Deed."/>
<p>He had brought her to the very place from which she had escaped but a short time before.</p>
<p>He took her down the stairs, found his way to the cell, proving that it was not his first visit there, and deposited her on the little bed, her heavy breathing proclaiming that she was still under the influence of the drug.</p>
<p>He lighted a lamp, and when his eyes were accustomed to the light, gazed down at his unconscious victim.</p>
<p>"She is lovely enough to risk a great deal for," he muttered. "Ah, my beautiful Viola! you shall never leave here until you have become my wife. Then I can claim a share in the business of Hardman & Son, and can defy the world."</p>

<pb n="[280]"/>
<div id="gfprice037" type="chapter">
<head rend = "center">CHAPTER XXXVII.</head>
<byline>A DESPAIRING MAN.</byline>
<p>For a little while the most intense and incoherent excitement prevailed in the Beekman house.</p>
<p>Howard rushed madly to the wing where Viola had lived in seclusion, and when he discovered that she really was not there tried vainly to conjecture how she had been stolen away.</p>
<p>Walter was thunderstruck, and ran from the storeroom to the wing, trying to make out something from the appearance of the two places.</p>
<p>Even Mrs. Beekman was so unstrung by the occurrence that for a while she could do nothing but wring her hands and bemoan the occurrence.</p>
<p>But she was the first to recover herself, and to cry out to her son:</p>
<p>"Howard, the scoundrel cannot have gone far with her for a burden. Run out and see if you cannot find him. Mr. Hardman, go with him. While you are gone I will consider what else can be done."</p>
<p>Her advice was so manifestly the best under the circumstances, that both men darted down the stairs and ran out of the house.</p>
<p>Howard hastened in one direction, and Walter in the other. Both searched the road thoroughly.</p>
<pb n="281 A Despairing Man."/>
<p>Walter took the road to the station as being the only one he was acquainted with, and followed it at a swift pace all the way.</p>
<p>He saw nothing of the fugitive, of course, and was assured at the station that it was impossible that anybody such as he described could have boarded the cars on the last train.</p>
<p>In despair Walter returned as quickly as he could to the house, after ascertaining when the next train would leave for the city.</p>
<p>In the meantime Mrs. Beekman had gone carefully over the house, using her extraordinary faculty of constructive analysis to the best of her ability.</p>
<p>She studied the storeroom with careful scrutiny, and then examined the room occupied by Viola. She jumped to no conclusions, but weighed everything deliberately.</p>
Howard was the first to return. His looks proclaimed his failure. His mother placed her hand kindly on his arm.
<p>"Do not despair, Howard!" she said. "As sure as there is justice in heaven, we shall find her. I know you are being true to yourself, and that is comfort to me."</p>
<p>"How can you look at me so kindly, mother?" he cried vehemently. "How can you speak a word of comfort to me? I curse myself. I see myself now as
<pb n="282 A Despairing Man."/>
if I were another being. How could I have so forgotten my manhood?"</p>
<p>"Hush, Howard! it may be your good fortune to atone by finding her and restoring her to her lover."</p>
<p>"And I will do it, mother, if I can. I swear I will redeem myself, if it can be done, by earnest effort to undo what I am responsible for having happened."</p>
<p>Later Walter, quite fagged out with his rapid walk, entered the house. When he had had a few minutes' rest, Mrs. Beekman said to him:</p>
<p>"Let me show you how it happened. It may be that the knowledge of how it was done will enable you to find some conclusion that is hidden from me."</p>
<p>"How it happened!" Walter repeated. "Can you tell me that?"</p>
<p>"I can nearly tell you. Come!"</p>
<p>She led him to the storeroom and pointed to his handkerchief, which lay on the floor.</p>
<p>"He easily got rid of his bonds," she said. "Then with that screw driver, which must have been left in here, he easily procured an exit. Come upstairs!"</p>
<p>He followed her up and stood at the door of the room, while she pointed to the floor, and said:</p>
<p>"Do you see those stains? I know by the odor that they were caused by the spilling of a stupefying drug. So it is certain that he came up here and summoned
<pb n="283 A Despairing Man."/>
her to the door, surprised her and drugged her. Then he must have carried her away."</p>
<p>"Yes, it is quite clear," Walter said. "So it would seem that he was already provided with the means for carrying her off&#x2014;the drug, I mean."</p>
<p>Howard glanced at his mother with a look of misery. He was quick enough to see that the drug used was the one which had helped his mother to discover his share in the previous wrongdoing.</p>
<p>"He was plainly provided with a drug," was all she said.</p>
<p>"But the real question is," said Walter, in agony, "where has he taken her to? I shall not rest until I have rescued her. He is capable of the worst acts."</p>
<p>"I have no means of guessing," she replied.</p>
<p>"I know that they did not go on the last train, because I am sure it must have passed before he could have reached the station, and because the man in charge was certain that no such person boarded the train."</p>
<p>"I do not believe he could have gone the other way," Howard said. "I do not think he would have dared. That road leads up into the mountains, and is very rough and rocky."</p>
<p>"And yet," said Mrs. Beekman, "he must have gone somewhere. Have you any suggestion, Mr. Hardman?"</p>
<pb n="284 A Despairing Man."/>
<p>"I had thought of going to the city at once. I could go to my father and tax him with complicity. He may know something that will aid us, though I confess my doubts. Then I would go to Eunice Carroll, and then to Viola's mother. Can you suggest anything else?"</p>
<p>"I would find the detective who was so kind to her, and lay the case before him. He may be able to help you."</p>
<p>"Yes, that is what I will do," Walter said; "though I cannot bear to leave the place where she certainly must be."</p>
<p>"What do you think of the plan, Howard?" asked Mrs. Beekman of her son, who had stood by in silence.</p>
<p>"I think the plan is a good one," he answered. "Some one should go to the city and make inquiries. The only suggestion I would make is that Mr. Hardman go first, and perhaps only to the detective. Miss Carroll would hardly be persuaded to tell him anything if she knew it; and to go to Mrs. Redmond would only alarm her."</p>
<p>"I will accept your suggestion, at least so far as to go first to the detective and take his advice."</p>
<p>"In the meantime," said Howard, "I will continue my search up here in the hope of finding her."</p>
<p>"I am inclined to think he will get her to the city as soon as possible," Mrs. Beekman said.</p>
<p>"Mr. Hardman can work to good advantage there,"
<pb n="285 A Despairing Man."/>
Howard said; "and I can do better than any one else up here. I am going out again at once."</p>
<p>He donned his hat and coat, and was going out, when Walter advanced toward him and held out his hand, saying:</p>
<p>"I can never thank you sufficiently for your good offices, Mr. Beekman."</p>
<p>"Thank me when I have returned Viola to you," Howard said, with his emotion betraying itself on his face.</p>
<p>He went out, and Walter followed not long after, and Mrs. Beekman was left alone.</p>
<p>"Howard will retrieve himself," she murmured. "It is a terrible madness that was upon him. I feel sure by the expression of his face that he has a fixed idea in going out again."</p>
<p>Yes, Howard had a fixed idea, but it was not of very great value so far as he could see. It had merely occurred to him that it would have been possible for Peter to take the road to the village, and thus escape Walter's pursuit.</p>
<p>He had simply determined to follow down that road until he came to the village and there inquire for such a stranger.</p>
<p>He had not the keen intuition of his mother, or the fiery energy of Walter, but he was animated now by a fierce, dogged determination to run down the man who
<pb n="286 A Despairing Man."/>
had so nearly made a murderer of him, and who at the last had duped him and stolen away Viola.</p>
<p>So he kept right on to the village without seeing anybody to ask any questions of, and without seeing anything to give him a certainty that Peter had come that way.</p>
<p>As he passed the hotel with the intention of continuing on down until he met somebody, he noticed a commotion in the horse sheds.</p>
<p>He stopped, wondering if he should go over and inquire of anybody there, though doubtful of obtaining any information from a hotel crowd on a winter's night.</p>
<p>"I tell ye," he heard a shrill, angry voice say, "I tied him right here. I guess I know what I'm talkin' 'bout. 'Tain't likely I walked down here and then thought I'd rid down. My new buggy, too."</p>
<p>"Better get out an alarm right off," said one of the bystanders.</p>
<p>"What is the matter?" he asked.</p>
<p>The lights of numerous lanterns were flashed on his face, and several voices exclaimed:</p>
<p>"It's Mr. Beekman!"</p>
<p>"Somebody's took Si Walton's horse an' buggy," volunteered some person in the crowd.</p>
<p>"When?" demanded Howard quickly.</p>
<p>"Must 'a' took it within half an hour, anyhow," the
<pb n="287 A Despairing Man."/>
victim said angrily. "I hain't been in thar more'n that time, hev I, Rube?"</p>
<p>"No, ye hain't," was the response.</p>
<p>"Is there no way of determining which way the wagon went?" Howard asked.</p>
<p>"Waal," said one wiseacre, "he must 'a' gone either up or down when he left here, for ther' ain't only them two ways to go."</p>
<p>"Are you not going to do anything about it?" Howard asked sharply. "It seems to me that every man who owns a horse should do something right away."</p>
<p>"True for you, Mr. Beekman!" cried a voice.</p>
<p>"What do you say," Howard went on, "if those of us who are here make a search party? The fellow cannot have gone far, and, if we go over every road leading out of here, we are sure to hear something of him."</p>
<p>"Oh, let's wait till morning," grumbled one.</p>
<p>"Not at all," said Howard peremptorily. "Who will set out on the search? I, for one, will take my share of the trouble."</p>
<p>A number of others joined them rather for the fun and excitement of the affair than for anything else, and within a short time seven carriages were filled and had set out.</p>
<p>Howard took his place in a buggy with the owner of the stolen horse.</p>
<pb n="288 A Despairing Man."/>
<p>"We ought to find that horse and buggy," he said.</p>
<p>They followed along the road selected for them, and were gone for over an hour before they could persuade themselves to give up the chase until morning.</p>
<p>"Say, Mr. Beekman," said the man suddenly, "I've got an idee."</p>
<p>"Well?"</p>
<p>"I say we go to my house an' see if the blamed critter has gone thar. If she has, you kin bet yer pile thet my wife is mournin' me a dead-and-gone sinner."</p>
<p>They drove to the house and went out to the stable. With its head against the stable door the patient horse stood. Everything was in good order excepting that the reins had dragged and been broken.</p>
<p>"Great Jehosophat?" exclaimed the farmer; "I'll bet a dollar I fergot to tie the critter."</p>
<p>Howard's heart sunk. He had been sure that he was on the trail of the abductor of Viola.</p>
<p>"But look what a lather she's in!" the farmer went on. "It jest don't seem as if she'd got that way comin' home; especially as there ain't nothin' broke to show she'd been tearin' along."</p>
<p>He was holding the lantern up, looking the horse and wagon over critically, when something white in the bottom of the buggy attracted Howard's attention, and he picked it up.</p>
<p>It was a soft, white handkerchief. His heart
<pb n="289 A Despairing Man."/>
throbbed violently. He thrust the handkerchief into his pocket and waited until the man went into the stable to see that the stall was all right. Then he snatched the handkerchief out and took it over to the light on the buggy he had been riding in.</p>
<p>"V. R." were the letters embroidered in one corner. It was the handkerchief of Viola.</p>
<p>She had been taken somewhere in that carriage. Oh, if the horse could only speak!</p>
<p>"Merciful Heaven!" cried Howard suddenly; "what if it should be the same house!"</p>

<pb n="[290]"/>
<div id="gfprice038" type="chapter">
<head rend = "center">CHAPTER XXXVIII.</head>
<byline>THE NEED FOR CAUTION.</byline>
<p>"Have you a revolver in the house?" Howard demanded of the man who had come out of the stable.</p>
<p>"No, I haven't," was the response. "My wife is so 't'rnal feared on 'em thet she won't let me hev one in the house. I've told her many's the time&#x2014;"</p>
<p>Howard cared nothing for what the man had said to his wife on the subject, so he unceremoniously said:</p>
<p>"Well, I fancy I can be of no more use to you, so I will go back and give up the horse to the person who loaned it to us."</p>
<p>"Much obleeged to you, Mr. Beekman," said the man. "I take it right kind on ye to ha' been so interested."</p>
<p>"That is all right. Good night."</p>
<p>Howard was out of the yard while the man was still talking about something remotely connected with horse stealing.</p>
<p>He drove through the village and said that the missing horse had been found; then drove home and had one of the stablemen take the horse back to its owner.</p>
<p>Then he ordered a horse hitched to a carriage for his
<pb n="291 The Need for Caution."/>
immediate use, and went in to his mother, who was waiting up for him.</p>
<p>He told her everything that had happened, and showed her the handkerchief. He asked for her opinion and advice.</p>
<p>"There is no doubt," she said, "that the wretch carried Viola off in that buggy, and then set the horse face homeward and let it go."</p>
<p>"Yes, I am sure of it," Howard answered.</p>
<p>"Then," said his mother, with an air of decision, "it only remains for us to scour the country and find her. Howard you must find her. If you do this&#x2014;if you can return her to Walter this time&#x2014;it will be an atonement for some part of the past."</p>
<p>"That is the one thing I am striving for, mother," he answered, in a low tone. "If I can do anything that will relieve me of the terrible feeling of shame and remorse, I shall be only too glad."</p>
<p>"Then set parties out in pursuit. Give a description of her, and offer a large reward to the successful person," Mrs. Beekman said energetically.</p>
<p>"But, mother, I have reason to believe I know the house she has been taken to," he said.</p>
<p>She looked at him for a moment; then her quick brain solved the riddle.</p>
<p>"You think it is the same place she was confined in
<pb n="292 The Need for Caution."/>
before, when she believed herself in prison? That is probable. And you know the house?"</p>
<p>"Yes, I have been there. I would have gone there, the instant I thought of it, but I felt that I ought to take a revolver. He is likely to be a desperate man, you know."</p>
<p>"Yes, he will be desperate. Well, get the revolver; and while you are getting it I will order a horse and carriage for you."</p>
<p>"I have done that already," he said.</p>
<p>When he returned downstairs, ready to go, he was surprised to see his mother dressed as if to go out.</p>
<p>"Where are you going, mother?" he asked.</p>
<p>"I am going with you, Howard. If Viola is where you think, it will be better for me to be with you, for she will not feel well after taking that drug. She will be glad to see me."</p>
<p>Howard paused as he was moving toward the door and went back to his mother.</p>
<p>He laid his hand upon her shoulder.</p>
<p>"Mother," he said, while his voice trembled with emotion, "you surely do not mistrust me? You do not doubt that I will be true and honorable now?</p>
<p>"Oh, no, Howard!" she answered quickly. "I know that you are your old self now, and that you will treat Viola as if she were your daughter. I mean only what
<pb n="293 The Need for Caution."/>
I say. I will go with you for Viola's sake. Do you not believe me?"</p>
<p>He took her in his arms and murmured:</p>
<p>"I do believe you, mother. I have no right to be sensitive; but I am trying so hard to atone."</p>
<p>"And you will atone, Howard," she said, pressing a kiss upon his brow. "Come let us go. Something tells me that we shall have good news for Walter when he comes again.</p>
<p>The servants were informed of their departure and Howard assisted his mother to enter the carriage.</p>
<p>"We may be a little crowded," he said, "but she will not mind that. Oh, if we can only find her!"</p>
<p>"We shall find her, Howard; I know we shall. There can be no doubt that he has taken shelter in the house; it would be almost the only thing he could do. You see, he probably does not know many places in the country. It was shrewd of you to think of it, Howard."</p>
<p>The horse was a good one and carried them over the frozen roads as quickly as it was safe to go.</p>
<p>Howard remembered well where the house was. He recalled with shame how he had played his wicked part that day in the deception of poor Viola.</p>
<p>"There is the house, mother," he said at last, pointing out the indistinct shape to her. "I will leave you
<pb n="294 The Need for Caution."/>
here, so as not to arouse suspicion of the scoundrel if he really be here."</p>
<p>"Be cautious, Howard," she murmured. "Do not risk your own life, nor take his except in self-defense."</p>
<p>Howard set his lips in a hard line, but answered:</p>
<p>"I will be careful, mother."</p>

<pb n="[295]"/>
<div id="gfprice039" type="chapter">
<head rend = "center">CHAPTER XXXIX.</head>
<byline>LOOKING LIKE DEATH.</byline>
<p>Words cannot describe the frame of mind in which Walter went to New York. His heart was filled with rage and despair.</p>
<p>He was turning his back on the place where Viola had been stolen from under his very eyes, and yet he knew that he was doing what was best.</p>
<p>He believed that a solution of the mystery that had surrounded her was to be found in New York better than anywhere else, and so he went there, but he went mourning.</p>
<p>Before leaving the Beekmans, he had procured the address of the detective who had befriended Viola, and, late as it was when he reached the city, he went thither at once.</p>
<p>The detective was in bed, but he came downstairs when told that Mr. Walter Hardman wished to see him.</p>
<p>"I owe you an apology, I know," Walter began, "for rousing you at this time of night, but I am in terrible distress, and it seems as if no one could assist me as well as you. I wish to employ you, and will pay you anything for your services."</p>
<pb n="296 Looking Like Death."/>
<p>"Why do you come to me?" asked the detective, as if he had no suspicion of the reason.</p>
<p>"Because I know that you once befriended my betrothed wife, when she was foully accused of a crime of which she was not guilty. It is in relation to Viola Redmond that I come to you now."</p>
<p>"I will do all I can for you," the detective said quickly. "Tell me everything you know about her. I have been unable to do anything about it for a variety of reasons, but I have not failed to think of her and to stand ready at any time to assist her. Besides, I have done something, too."</p>
<p>Walter started in at the beginning and told the detective everything he had learned from every source.</p>
<p>The detective listened to the end, only interrupting to ask a question now and then.</p>
<p>When Walter had finished he said in his quick, decisive way:</p>
<p>"Wait here until I have finished dressing properly, and we will go out together. I see a chance to end this affair."</p>
<p>As soon as he was ready he and Walter left the house. The latter inquired:</p>
<p>"Where are we going now?"</p>
<p>"To search the rooms of Peter Harriem. I have kept a watch on him, and have had a proceeding of this
<pb n="297 Looking Like Death."/>
sort in view. I have no warrant for the search, but a detective must take risks to accomplish his ends."</p>
<p>There was no one on watch at the house where Peter's economy had led him to live, so that the detective had no difficulty in forcing the door leading into his room.</p>
<p>"Rather a burglarious proceeding," he laughed, as the door gave way; "but I think it necessary."</p>
<p>He closed the door after him and lighted a lamp which stood on the table in the room.</p>
<p>Walter was now amazed to see with what swiftness the detective prosecuted his task of searching the place.</p>
<p>He did not even know what the object of the search was.</p>
<p>Presently the detective unearthed a pocketbook, which he took to the light and opened, uttering an exclamation as he did so.</p>
<p>"We will find what we are after in here if anywhere," he said to Walter.</p>
<p>"What do you seek?" Walter asked.</p>
<p>"I am looking for the address of some house out of the city, and up in that neighborhood," he answered.
Walter was mystified.</p>
<p>"Why do you look for such a thing?" he asked.</p>
<p>"Because it is plain that there is some place up in that neighborhood which has been used by these scoun-
<pb n="298 Looking Like Death."/>
drels for their pretended prison, for, of course, there was no real arrest, no real prison."</p>
<p>"And you think&#x2014;" began Walter.</p>
<p>"I think that if there is such a place this fellow will have something to tell him where it is. Another man would carry the fact in his head, but this is one of your sly ones who is always trying to incriminate others. Ten to one he has the address or description in the writing of some one else. There!" he cried triumphantly, as he unfolded a slip of paper and glanced over it. "Just look at that!"</p>
<p>Walter read it eagerly.</p>
<p>It was the description of a house, and he could see that it must be somewhere near where the Beekmans lived.</p>
<p>"Do you happen to know the writing?" the detective asked, his eyes meanwhile running over another piece of paper which he had taken from the pocketbook.</p>
<p>It had not occurred to Walter to think of examining the handwriting. He did so now; at first doubtfully, and at last with horror in his expression.</p>
<p>"It is the writing of Eunice Carroll, I believe."</p>
<p>The detective coolly took it from Walter and put it in his pocket, saying:</p>
<p>"I did not know her handwriting, but I would have sworn it was hers. I do not believe she will give us any trouble, but the paper will be useful in some other way,
<pb n="299 Looking Like Death."/>
maybe. Mr. Harriem did us a service in preserving it, to say nothing of this other document."</p>
<p>"What is it?" demanded Walter.</p>
<p>"It does not matter now" the detective answered. "If necessary I will show it to you. The question now is to get up to the house described in this address. Are you prepared to go?"</p>
<p>"I am," answered Walter. "I cannot rest until I have rescued my darling from the clutches of that scoundrel. He would not hesitate to murder her if he could not bend her to his will; and I know he will never succeed in doing that."</p>
<p>"Well, he isn't likely to attempt it to-night, anyhow," said the detective, who had the faculty of seeming to be perfectly sure of everything he said.</p>
<p>It gave Walter a feeling of confidence in him, and rendered it possible for him to preserve some outward show of composure.</p>
<p>By going into a hotel the detective was enabled to get a time-table and learn about the trains.</p>
<p>"There is a late express, which stops at the station farther on," he said. "It would do just as well for us. We can get a conveyance and reach the house from there as well as from the other station."</p>
<p>"What if she should not be there?" Walter said.</p>
<p>The detective smiled as if there was no manner of doubt in his mind as to that.</p>
<pb n="300 Looking Like Death."/>
<p>"She will be there," he said; and he was as satisfied of that as if he had seen her at that moment.</p>
<p>Yes, she was there, indeed; but, although there were signs of life within her breast, she remained fast locked in the slumber induced by the treacherous drug.</p>
<p>After placing her on the cot and throwing something over her to keep her warm, Peter stood for a while gloating over her beauty.</p>
<p>"Mine or nobody's," he muttered. "Oh, how beautiful!"</p>
<p>He took up the little hand and held it in his great, bony claw for a moment, then bent over her to ascertain how much under the influence of the drug she was.</p>
<p>"I hope I did not give her too much," he murmured. "And yet I would rather see her lying there dead than his bride."</p>
<p>He tried to revive her by gently shaking her, and by slapping her hands as he had heard was done. But the stupor could not be dispelled in that way.</p>
<p>"I will leave her for a while," he said uneasily. "I will go upstairs and make a fire. I know there were the materials there. It won't cost me anything, and I always believe in being comfortable when some one else pays for it."</p>
<p>He chuckled in his hideous way and went upstairs, where he lighted a fire and sat down to enjoy its warmth.</p>
<pb n="301 Looking Like Death."/>
<p>"No one will think of looking for us here," he muttered. "If I could only frighten her into becoming my wife. Then I could return to the city and defy them all. That paper of agreement with the old man would save me from Walter, I fancy. Even Walter would not do anything to mix his father up in the attempt at murder."</p>
<p>He crouched over the fire and rubbed his hands in a way that was uncanny to see.</p>
<p>"Sneaking Peter they call me at the store; and they think I do not know it. As if I have not put down the name of every one who has jeered me behind my back. Some day I shall pay each one for his insolence. Sneaking Peter I may be. I know I am a very sly Peter. Very sly and cunning. Too cunning for most people.</p>
<p>"Look at this, now! They thought they had me safe under lock and key and quite beaten. But I escaped and carried away their treasure. Aha! they reckon without their host when they leave me out of their calculations. I have her in my power, and I will keep her, or&#x2014;or&#x2014; Well, there is no use in settling that yet. At any rate, he shall never fold her in his arms and call her wife.</p>
<p>"I shall see to that. She shall be mine, or no one's. I loved her first, and I deserve her. What it she did love him! She will be better off with me than with
<pb n="302 Looking Like Death."/>
such a spendthrift. And some day I shall be worth more than any of them. I wonder if she is reviving?"</p>
<p>He got up as he spoke and went down to the little room, where she lay in a slumber so quiet and still as to be awesome.</p>
<p>"It looks so horribly like death," he muttered. "I would not like to look at her if I had done it."</p>
<p>He tried to rouse her again, and when he failed in every measure he tried, he would have been alarmed but for the fact that her breathing was strong and regular, if a little heavy.</p>
<p>"If he had given a little more of the stuff to Walter," he snarled, "nothing of this need have happened. He was afraid to do it and Walter recovered from the dose."</p>
<p>He went upstairs again and crouched once more over the fire, listening now and again with startled gaze as the sudden scampering of rats in the empty rooms roused him.</p>
<p>And by and by his eyes closed, and he fell into a fitful, disturbed slumber in which he alternately fought like a cornered brute with his pursuers, and sneered at them.</p>

<pb n="[303]"/>
<div id="gfprice040" type="chapter">
<head rend = "center">CHAPTER XL.</head>
<byline>IN A TERRIBLE RAGE.</byline>
<p>Mrs. Beekman listened to the footfall of her son until it was lost, and then waited for some sound to tell her that he had acted.</p>
<p>Presently he returned, and in a whisper told her that he had contrived to peer through the shutters of one of the rooms, and had seen Peter sitting over the fire.</p>
<p>A glad cry escaped from his mother's lips as she listened, for it seemed to her that Fate had led her son there, that he might purge himself of the sin he had been guilty of.</p>
<p>"Howard," she said, "you must tie the horse and let me get out. I may be able to suggest something that will help you."</p>
<p>"That is why I came back, mother," he said.</p>
<p>So he tied the horse to a tree and saw that it was well covered with the blanket, then, accompanied by his mother, stole up to the house again.</p>
<p>He led her to where Peter could be seen, and she looked for a long time before she drew back, and whispered:</p>
<p>"Howard, he is asleep. Let us steal around to the back door and see if we cannot open it softly. If we could only surprise him it would be a triumph for us."</p>
<pb n="304 In a Terrible Rage."/>
<p>Howard was naturally doubtful of his abilities as a house breaker, but the plan was too sensible not to be tried.</p>
<p>So they went around to the back of the house, and easily found the door that led into the lower floor.</p>
<p>"I have nothing to force it open with," Howard said dubiously.</p>
<p>Mrs. Beekman tried the knob softly.</p>
<p>"You need nothing," she said, with a low laugh, "it is open."</p>
<p>The door was indeed open, and the two stole quietly in. They closed the door after them, and groped their way along the hall.</p>
<p>Howard did not dare strike a match, lest in doing so he should give the alarm to Peter. Fortunately it was not needed. They found the staircase and crept up.</p>
<p>In after days, Mrs. Beekman laughed at herself in the role of burglar, but at the time it was a most exciting adventure.</p>
<p>When the stairs creaked under their weight, they stopped and held their breath while they listened.</p>
<p>But Peter was sound asleep, dreaming of his flight and fight with countless pursuers, who were hemming him in on every side.</p>
<p>Howard led the way, and when he reached the upper hall stopped and caught his mother by the sleeve.</p>
<p>He did not dare utter a word, but he pointed toward
<pb n="305 In a Terrible Rage."/>
a light which came under the door of the room Peter sat in.</p>
<p>Softly, and with infinite care, they crept to the door; and when there Howard stooped and looked through the keyhole. His mother followed him in this, and then whispered:</p>
<p>"Rush in suddenly, and grasp him. Give me your pistol, already cocked, and I will threaten him with it if he resists."</p>
<p>Howard knew he would have no trouble in mastering Peter if once he had him in his grasp; so he complied with his mother's wish, and gave her the revolver.</p>
<p>Then he softly turned the knob, and when he found the door yield he threw it open, and with a bound had the arms of the sleeping man pinioned behind him.</p>
<p>Peter awoke with a horrible cry. Somehow the capture of him made a part of his dream, and he waked from a nightmare of terror.</p>
<p>He was in full possession of his senses immediately, and after a fierce but ineffectual struggle gave up sullenly, and twisted about until he caught a glimpse of Howard's face.</p>
<p>When he saw who his captor was, he gave vent to a laugh, as if he was no longer concerned.</p>
<p>"Oh! it's you, is it?" he said. "Well, you might as well let me go. I suppose you are not going to make a fool of yourself, are you?"</p>
<pb n="306 In a Terrible Rage."/>
<p>Mrs. Beekman stepped forward into view before Howard had framed an answer, and Peter's face dropped at sight of her.</p>
<p>"Base wretch!" she exclaimed indignantly, "you may be sure he is not going to make a fool of himself. And you shall pay the penalty of your misdoing."</p>
<p>"My misdoing!" he repeated, recovering himself. "What have I done that your aristocratic son has not been a partner in? Beware what you do, or he shall share my punishment."</p>
<p>"Hold him until I have procured a strap from the buggy, Howard. We will bind him securely this time."</p>
<p>She hastened away, and Peter hissed quickly:</p>
<p>"If you know when you are well off, Howard Beekman, you will let me go before she returns. Your fate is mixed up with mine."</p>
<p>"I would not let you go," Howard answered, "if I knew I would hang within the hour. I have acted the villain far too long. This shall be my atonement."</p>
<p>"You canting fool!" raged Peter.</p>
<p>But he could not move Howard by either prayers or threats, and it was not many minutes ere he was securely bound and lying on the sofa which formed a part of the furniture of the room.</p>
<p>"Now let us find Viola," Mrs. Beekman said.</p>
<pb n="307 In a Terrible Rage."/>
<p>She took the lamp and searched the rooms on that floor.</p>
<p>Then she went below at the suggestion of Howard, and it was but a few minutes ere they opened the door of Viola's prison and looked upon her as she lay in a stupor on the cot.</p>
<p>Mrs. Beekman looked terrified for a moment, then ran to Viola's side and bent anxiously over her.</p>
<p>"Can she be aroused, mother?" Howard asked.</p>
<p>"Yes, she is safe," she replied. "But lift her and carry her upstairs by the fire. But, hark! what is that noise?"</p>
<p>"It is I, Mrs. Beekman!" cried a voice, which both recognized as belonging to Walter.</p>
<p>"Just in time, then," answered the good lady. "We have secured the scoundrel, and were just about to carry his victim upstairs."</p>
<p>Almost before she had finished, Walter had leaped into the room and had fallen on his knees by the side of his darling, raining kisses on her face.</p>
<p>"Oh, my love&#x2014;my love! Can she not be aroused?" he demanded.</p>
<p>"Not until we have her at home," Mrs. Beekman answered; "but she is in no danger."</p>
<p>"Let us hasten, then!" Walter cried, as he took the beloved form in his strong arms and bore her up the stairs.</p>
<pb n="308 In a Terrible Rage."/>
<p>Mrs. Beekman, who carried the lamp, led the way into the room where Peter lay bound, followed by the whole party, the detective, in his silent way, bringing up the rear.</p>
<p>"Caught at last like a rat in a trap, eh?" he said grimly, as his eyes fell on the bound form of the scoundrel.</p>
<p>Peter's eyes were fixed on the picture of Walter with Viola in his arms. That sight more than anything else seemed to make him feel that he had been thoroughly defeated.</p>
<p>A horrible rage took possession of him, and with a hideous, snakelike wriggle he contrived to squirm to his feet.</p>
<p>"You can't touch me without hurting others!" he screamed. "And she is mine. I loved her first. You shall never&#x2014;nev&#x2014; Ah, what is this?"</p>
<p>He reeled as he spoke and fell backward on the sofa. The detective sprang to him and lifted him to place him on the sofa, so that he would lie at length.</p>
<p>"Dead!" he said, in a shocked tone.</p>
<p>"It must have been his heart," whispered Mrs. Beekman, in an awed tone. "Pray remove the bonds from his limbs."</p>
<p>"All of you go," the detective said. "I will remain here until morning. I am an officer of the law, and he is my prisoner. There is no need for any of you
<pb n="309 In a Terrible Rage."/>
to be mixed up in this. All of you go. I will attend to everything."</p>
<p>At his mother's suggestion Howard ran out and brought in a carriage rug to wrap Viola in; and then he volunteered to take Walter's carriage back to its stable while Walter took Mrs. Beekman and Viola home.</p>
<p>Walter was more grateful than he could express for all their kindness, but did not hesitate to accept Howard's offer.</p>
<p>It was just before dawn when they reached Mrs. Beekman's house. Viola was carried to the good lady's room</p>.
<p>"We will let her sleep till daylight," Mrs. Beekman said to Walter. "It will be better for her now if the drug will wear itself off naturally."</p>
<p>So Walter gently kissed the lips of his darling, and retired to the room they gave him, and in spite of his anxiety was so worn out that he fell asleep and did not awake until he felt Howard's hand on his shoulder.</p>
<p>He started up and looked at the other, and his first quick word was:</p>
<p>"Viola!"</p>
<p>"She is awake and quite well," Howard answered. "Mother has told her you are here, and she is eager to see you."</p>
<p>With a glad cry Walter sprang out of bed, every trace of fatigue gone. In a marvelously short time
<pb n="310 In a Terrible Rage."/>
he rejoined Howard, who was waiting in the hall to conduct him to the sitting room, where Viola awaited him.</p>
<p>The young girl rose as she heard his step on the stairs, and was halfway across the room to meet him when he entered.</p>
<p>"My dear love!" he murmured, in an ecstasy of joy, and folded her in his arms.</p>
<p>Mrs. Beekman stole out of the room and closed the door, so that the long-parted lovers might have all the bliss of being alone in the first moments of their transports.</p>
<p>"Never to part again, dear," was one of the things Walter whispered in her ear.</p>
<p>"But your father, Walter?" she queried, fixing her brown eyes on his face.</p>
<p>"My father shall have nothing to say about it, Viola, my love. Never again shall any one decide the question of our love for us. We love and must wed."</p>
<p>She could not combat him when he spoke in that decided way. It was so sweet to have one so strong and masterful to cling to.</p>
<p>"Will you leave everything to me, dear?" he asked.</p>
<p>"Yes, everything," she murmured.</p>
<p>"Then we will be married this afternoon," he said.</p>

<pb n="[311]"/>
<div id="gfprice041" type="chapter">
<head rend = "center">CHAPTER XLI.</head>
<byline>THE SILVER LINING.</byline>
<p>When the subject of the marriage was broached to Mrs. Beekman by Walter, she listend to his arguments, and then agreed that it would be justifiable under the circumstances.</p>
<p>"Viola's mother cannot be present, for which I am sorry," she said, "but I will be the best substitute I can. I think that, after all Viola has undergone, it is in every way best that she should become your wife at once.</p>
<p>So everything was arranged for the afternoon, and they were all pleased when Mr. Phil Dexter, the detective, put in an appearance.</p>
<p>He added his commendation to the others when he learned what was going to be done. He explained to Mrs. Beekman that there would be no notoriety attached to any of them in connection with the death of Peter Harriem.</p>
<p>"I took it all on myself," he said, "and the coroner will see that there is no fuss made."</p>
<p>It was a very happy wedding if not a large or fashionable one, and there was a merry wedding supper after it.</p>
<pb n="312 The Silver Lining."/>
<p>After supper the newly wedded pair was to start on a short bridal trip, after which Walter proposed to return to the city.</p>
<p>Phil Dexter listened to his plans with a quiet smile on his sphinxlike face, and when there was an opportunity, drew Walter aside and said to him:</p>
<p>"I suppose you have plenty of money?"</p>
<p>Walter thought at once of his promise to pay the detective his own price, and answered quickly:</p>
<p>"I have a few thousand dollars. I will pay you whenever you let me know how much I owe you."</p>
<p>"Oh," said Phil, "that can wait well enough. What I was wondering was if you had enough to be independent of your father."</p>
<p>"I intend to find something to do. I suppose he will not like this act of mine, and I don't intend to ask any favors of him; he has not acted rightly toward my wife."</p>
<p>"Nonsense!" said the detective. "He is your father and is very fond of you. Will you give me permission to handle the affair in my own way?"</p>
<p>"I won't have anything done that savors of humbling myself or wife for his favor," Walter answered proudly.</p>
<p>"All right," laughed the detective; "I guess there won't be any proposition of that sort."</p>
<p>Walter had not much hope that the detective would
<pb n="313 The Silver Lining."/>
find the handling of his father an easy task, but his heart was light, and he drove from the house when the time came, with a smile on his face and the little hand he loved held tight in his.</p>
<p>Phil Dexter watched the carriage until it was out of sight; then turned to Mrs. Beekman, with a shake of his head.</p>
<p>"Love is a beautiful thing, Mrs. Beekman, but it will be none the less attractive for a little gold to gild it with."</p>
<p>"Do you really hope to win the father's entire forgiveness?" she asked.</p>
<p>"I have not the slightest doubt of it," he replied, with a grim smile.</p>
<p>"I wish you luck for their sakes," she said. "Thank you; and now, with your permission, I will catch my train and return to the city."</p>
<p>Silas Hardman knew nothing of the wedding of his son to the salesgirl, against whom he had permitted his confidential man to plot, but he was in a very bad humor because that confidential man was not there that morning.</p>
<p>It had been clearly understood between them that Peter was to have his own time in working out his scheme, but he had never taken so much time away before, and it vexed Mr. Hardman.</p>
<p>For one thing, it gave him a great deal of extra
<pb n="314 The Silver Lining."/>
work to do, and perhaps made him realize either that he was growing too old for so much work, or that Peter had always greatly relieved him.</p>
<p>The truth was that Peter had made it his business to shoulder as much as possible of the work that Mr. Hardman would naturally do.</p>
<p>This was the way he had taken to make himself necessary.</p>
<p>"Confound the fellow!" grumbled Mr. Hardman. "I wish I had never entered into the agreement with him. Well, what do you want?"</p>
<p>One of the subordinate clerks had thrust his head doubtfully through the half-opened doorway.</p>
<p>"A gentleman to see you, sir."</p>
<p>"Has Mr. Harriem come yet?" snarled Mr. Hardman.</p>
<p>"No, sir."</p>
<p>"Who is the gentleman? What does he want?"</p>
<p>"I don't know, sir."</p>
<p>"Show him in, idiot!"</p>
<p>It was a very ungracious way of treating the clerk, but the clerks of Hardman & Son were accustomed to being treated so.</p>
<p>"Well, sir?" inquired Mr. Hardman, as a quiet stranger was ushered in.</p>
<p>"I came to see you, sir," said the stranger, "about a matter in which your son is concerned."</p>
<pb n="315 The Silver Lining."/>
<p>"Your name, please?" said Mr. Hardman.</p>
<p>"Philip Dexter. I am a detective, and was employed to arrest a young lady who was accused of theft, and&#x2014;"</p>
<p>"Ah! and let her escape," said Mr. Hardman icily.</p>
<p>"Yes; I told her I knew she was innocent and that she had better run away until I should have time to investigate the matter and clear her."</p>
<p>"Indeed, sir! and have you done so?" demanded Mr. Hardman.</p>
<p>"Yes," replied Phil Dexter dryly, "I have done so. The young lady did not steal the ring. It was placed in her pocket as the result of a conspiracy between Miss Eunice Carroll, Mr. Peter Harriem, and one other gentleman, who was foolish enough to write and sign a paper which clearly mixes him up in the matter."</p>
<p>Mr. Hardman rose to his feet, his face white and set.</p>
<p>"How dare you, sir!" he began.</p>
<p>Phil Dexter held up his hand deprecatingly, and drew a piece of paper from his pocket.</p>
<p>"I have the paper right here."</p>
<p>Mr. Hardman sunk back in his chair. For the first time he realized what he had risked.</p>
<p>"I had no part in&#x2014;"</p>
<p>"My dear sir," interrupted the detective, "I know
<pb n="316 The Silver Lining."/>
all about it. At any rate, I know that you had no part in the attempt on the life of your son."</p>
<p>"Life of my son!" gasped the old man, all the blood leaving his face.</p>
<p>"Yes," said the detective; "Peter Harriem loved the girl who had become your son's betrothed wife, and, finding no other way good enough, tried to murder Walter Hardman."</p>
<p>"Impossible! You are mad! My son is in Europe."</p>
<p>"On the contrary, he is in this country. He learned of the persecution to which Viola Redmond was being subjected, and he came over here at once."</p>
<p>"And now where is he?"</p>
<p>"On his way west, I believe. But Peter Harriem is dead. He died of heart disease."</p>
<p>"Dead!"</p>
<p>"Yes; he died after being foiled in a foul plot to abduct the young lady who is now your son's wife."</p>
<p>"His wife! he has married her?"</p>
<p>"Yes, he has married her; and if you were to seek the world over you could not find a better or more beautiful girl."</p>
<p>"You seem to be interested," said Mr. Hardman.</p>
<p>"Yes," replied the detective, "I am interested. Your son said he would not ask you for any assistance, and I thought that was right. At the same time I have
<pb n="317 The Silver Lining."/>
come to say to you that I think you ought to treat your son as such a son should be treated."</p>
<p>"Thank you," said the old man ironically. "I think I know how and what to do. I wish you a good day."</p>
<p>"Certainly. And this little piece of paper? Shall I put it with another piece I have in Miss Carroll's writing and give them to a lawyer?"</p>
<p>"Do you threaten me by my son's wish?" exclaimed Silas Hardman.</p>
<p>"Your son, as yet, knows nothing of this piece of paper," said the detective.</p>
<p>"Give me the paper," said the old man eagerly. "Give it to me, and I promise you my son shall have all his heart could wish."</p>
<p>Without hesitation the detective handed the paper to the old man and bowed himself out.</p>
<p>When Walter and his bride returned to the city there was a beautifully furnished house waiting for them. And the time came when Silas Hardman wondered at two things: How he had ever conducted his business without Walter, and how he could ever have wished for any other daughter-in-law.</p>

<hi rend="center">THE END.</hi>***check formatting***

Own Sweetheart," by Wenona Gilman, is an in-tensely interesting romance, in which love and money run a race, and the reader will be kept guessing for a long time as to which will win.</p>
